\bigskip


\centerline{\bf{Introduction}}

\bigskip



\noindent
{\emph{ Outline of the contents.}}
The "impatient reader" may
glance at the material in ¤ 0.1-0.7   covering
some essential facts  in measure theory. Already in ¤ 0.1
we expose the Borel-Riesz theory 
which 
culminates in the description of the dual  space of $C^0(S)$ when
$S$ is a compact topological space.
Let us also remark that measure theory on the real line
already contains quite  involved results.
The reader should study the construction by Weierstrass in ¤ 0.4.1
of a continuous function 
which fails to have a derivative at every point, and
the Denjoy-Young theorem in ¤ 0.4.3
about  infinitesmal 
difference quotients of  continuous real-valued functions on
the unit interval.
\medskip

\noindent
The sections listed as ¤ 1-4 treat basic fact in
measure theory. Fir example, in  ¤ 2 we expose
abstract measure theory  due to  Emile Borel in his pionerring
work
\emph{xxxx} from 1908.
Here
one starts from a sample space $\Omega$ and 
a Boolean $\sigma$-algebra $\mathcal B$ of subsets.
The  pair $(\Omega,\mathcal B)$ gives 
a  class of $\sigma$-additive measures and
for each specific measure $\mu$ there exists the family
$\mathcal N_\mu$ of its null sets.
One  proceeds to study 
$\mu$-measurable functions  which are identified 
when they agree outside a null set.
The main results deal with integrals of
$\mu$-measurable functions where 
a crucial  point  is Borel's construction
of integrals of $\mu$-measurable functions
$f$. His 
fundamental idea  was to
use
a partition of  values taken by  $f$.
Namely, one  computes  $\mu$-measures of 
inverse images of sets $\{a\leq f< b \}$
for all pairs of real numbers $a<b$ and after a limit
one gets  Borel's  integral   $\int f\cdot d\mu$.
Another notable  result is Hahn's decomposition theorem which 
asserts  that a signed measure can be expressed  
in a unique way   as the 
difference of two non-negative measures
$\mu$ and $\nu$
which are mutually singular, i.e.
$\mu$ carries its whole mass on a null-set for
$\nu$, and vice versa.
One   consequence
is the Radon-Nikodym theorem which 
is used to construct  the 
singular part of  one measure with respect to another.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{Lebegue theory}}.
It is treated in ¤ 3 
where  the major results are due to Henri Lebesgue in his classic work from 1904.
The notion of Lebesgue points plays a crucial role
and leads to the fundamental fact that every
measurabe function in the sense of Lebegue is a
pointwise limit of a sequence of 
continuou functions outside a null set.
The more extensive class of Riesz measures
in euclidian spaces is studied  in ¤ 4.
Since the  abstract theory is
relatively easy to grasp, 
the less experienced reader
is adviced to read  ¤ 2 before the entering
¤ 3 and ¤ 4.
\medskip



\noindent
\emph{Other topics.}
In ¤ 5  we prove
Stokes Theorem for domains in
${\bf{R}}^n$
where one allows
irregular points on the boundary.
¤ 6 and 7 treat
the Hardy-Littlewood maximal function
and the Rademacher functions.
Section 8 is devoted to the  construction of   the Brownian motion
where the main result is 
the almost everywhere continuity of individual
Brownian paths.


\bigskip




\noindent{\bf{Historic comments.}}
A problem which
played an important role
to develop
general measure theory appeared in
the article
\emph{Sur la distribution de l'ectrecitŽ ˆ la surface
des conducteurs fermŽs et des conducteurs ouverts} from 1886
by  G. Robin. He  asked
if every
compact set
$E$ in ${\bf{C}}$ without interior points 
carries a distribution of mass whose logarithmic potential function is constant
on $E$. Robin's problem led to 
the
notion of
sets of absolute harmonic measure zero and other 
thin sets. Modern integration theory started in work by Stieltjes from 1894
in connection with moment problems. Soon after
Stieltjes' publication, Emile  Borel 
realised that integrals can be defined in a  robust  way by
decomposing the range of the function which is   integrated, instead of
decomposing  the domain
where integration takes place.
This 
led to
abstract measure theory  exposed in ¤ 2.
A great  merit in the constriuction of Borel-Stieltjes integrals is that
they lead to integrals of vector-valued functions in
Banach spaces.
\medskip


\noindent
\emph{Riesz measures.}
In addition to pioneering work by Stieltjes, Borel and Lebesgue,
important contributions are also due
to Vitali and F. Riesz.
Some fundamental   convergence
results were established by Vitali in the article 
\emph{Sulle funzione integrali} [Atti. R. acad. Sci. di Torino: 1905]
which put  earlier results by Lebesgue
in a broader context and clarified  the
the notion of $\sigma$-additive measures.
Staying in an euclidian space
${\bf{R}}^n$, F. Riesz
constructed a class of  measures in
${\bf{R}}^n$ where
Borel's abstract theory is adapted to the  $\sigma$-algebra
of Borel sets in ${\bf{R}}^n$, i.e. the  smallest
$\sigma$-algebra which contains all open and closed sets.
One consequence of  Riesz' theory goes as follows:
The solution of the Dirichlet problem in the unit disc yields
a 1-1 correspondence between
the class of positive  harmonic functions $H(x,y)$ in the open unit disc
$D=\{x^2+y^2<1\}$ normalised so that $H(0,0)=1$,
and the class of probability measures 
supported by the unit circle
$T=\{x^2+y^2=1\}$. 
A notable point
is that the class contains 
measures which are singular with respect to the angular Lebesgue measure on 
$T$.
A more general construction due to Wiener works for
every bounded open set $\Omega$ even if the Dirichlet problem cannot be solved for
every continuous boundary function $f$ in $C^0(\partial\Omega)$.
More precisely, for each  $z\in\Omega$, Wiener  constructed a distinguished
probability measure $\mathfrak{m}^\Omega\uuu z$
supported by $\partial \Omega$ such that
each $f\in C^0(\partial\Omega)$ produces a unique harmonic function $W\uuu f$
defined in $\Omega$ by
\[
W\uuu f(z)=
\int\uuu{\partial\Omega}\, f(\zeta)\cdot \mathfrak{m}^\Omega\uuu z(\zeta)
\]
Wiener's result is proved in the chapter about harmonic and subharmonic functions.
In Ergodic Theory
measures are used  frequently.
Here the literature is extensive and
current research  active. We  shall not enter a discussion of 
this subject except for material about 
almost periodic functions  whose theory was  created 
by
Harald Bohr in the beginning of 1920.
An interesting application of 
Bohr's theory  appears in the section from \emph{Special Topics}
entitled
\emph{Almost periodic functions and additive number theory}
which  presents  a result due to Beurling from seminars at
Uppsala University in 1948.
\medskip

\noindent
\emph{Infinite products of measure spaces.}
A general method to construct integrals
over infinite product spaces is due to Daniell in [Dan].
It is
logically transparent but
has a disadvantage because  one cannot so easily
call upon existing theorems in Lebesgue theory from the finite
dimensional case.
To overcome this,  Norbert Wiener used \emph{binary expansions}
in order to extend 
the Lebesgue measure to an infinite  product  of
${\bf{R}}$. This crucial point  is 
a measure preserving map
from the 
the two-dimensional unit square
$\square=\{(x,y)\colon\, 0<x,y<1\}$ onto the interval $(0,1)$
constructed as follows:
Every real number  $0<x<1$ has a  binary expansion:
\[
 x=\alpha_1/2+\alpha_2/4+\ldots\quad\,\, \alpha_k=0
 \,\,\text{or}\,\, 1
 \]
 Similarly $0<y<1$ has a binary expansion
 \[
  y=\beta_1/2+\beta_2/4+\ldots\quad\,\, \beta_k=0
 \,\,\text{or}\,\, 1
\]
These expansions are unique  when one prescribes that the expansion never terminates 
with a sequence where the number 1 appears. For example, one represents
$1/4$ by $0,1,0,,0\ldots$ while the sequence $0,0,1,1,1,\ldots$ is not allowed.
A pair of sequences as above
is  arranged
into a single sequence which  is used to
express a real number $0<t<1$ whose binary series is:
\[
 t(x,y)=
\alpha_1/2+\beta_2/4+\alpha_2/8+\beta_2/16+\ldots\tag{*}
\]
This yields a  bijective map from $\square$ onto
$(0,1)$ which 
is  \emph{measure preserving}, i.e. if
$F(x,y)$ is a continuous function of $(x,y)$ then
\[
\iint_\square\, F(x,y)\cdot dxdy=\int_0^1\, f(t)\cdot dt
\quad\text{where}\quad f(t(x,y))= F(x,y)
\]
In this way     Lebesgue theory in
dimension two can be recaptured from the one-dimensional case.
Similar measure preserving transformations from
${\bf{R}}^n$ into ${\bf{R}}$ exist for every $n\geq 2$ and once this is
achieved it is not difficult to construct a measure preserving
transformation from a denumerable product to the real $t$-line.
Details of this construction appear in the text\vvv book
[Paley\vvv Wiener].
\medskip


\noindent
{\bf{The Vitali-Hahn-Saks theorem.}}
After the development of  functional analysis
around 1930, the original results by Vitali were put into 
the context of  topological vector spaces.
Many general and conclusive results were established by
Saks in the article
\emph{Integration in abstract metric spaces.} [Duke  Math. J. Vol 4: 1938].
Here one regards a Boolean 
$\sigma $-algebra $\mathcal B$
of subsets of a sample space
$\Omega$ and $\mu$ is a non-negative 
$\sigma$-additive meaasure
which assigns real numbers to sets in
$\mathcal B$.
In ¤ xx we introduce the family of $\mu$-continuous additive functions
and prove a result concerned with uniform convergence which ressembles the
Banach-Steinhaus theorem in  Frechet spaces.
As expected the Baire  category theorem plays an essential role in the proofs.
Another important issue are product spaces with infinitely many factors
where one seeks extensions of the Fubini theorem.
Here a major contribution is due to Jessen in
the   work  \emph{Bidrag til intergalteorin for funktioner av uendlig mange
variabler} [Copenhagen 1930]. See also his article
\emph{The theory of integration  in a space of infinitely  many
variables} [Acta math. 1933]
which is a veritable classic with a wide range of 
applications, foremost in probability theory.
A further extension of Jessen's
work without any use of
topology was treated by J. von  Neumann in
\emph{Functional Operators I.} [Princeton University Press. 1950].
 









\medskip


\noindent
{\bf {Probability theory.}}
Much feeling for results in general measure theory
arise via probabilistic
interpretations.
For example, if $\mu$ and $\nu$ is a pair of Riesz measures on
the real oine which both are non-negstive and have mass one, then
there exists the convolution $\mu*\nu$.
If $\mu$ and $\nu$ describe probability distributions of 
two random variables, then
 $\mu*\nu$ is the probability distribution of their sum under
the condition that they are independent.
In ¤ 8 we study the Brownian motion. Here we first expose Wiener's construction
of a stochastic process in continuous time
with independent increments while the outcome at each 
time-value is a normally distributed random variable.
To analyze individual Brownian paths
one needs a sample space which by Wiener's construction is
found by a denumerable infinite product
of "ordinary" measure spaces.
The whole study culminates in Theorem 9 in from ¤ 8.1  which
is one of the major results in this chapter. In ¤ 8.2 we have also included
the construction of a parabolic PDE-system which
governs a stochastic vector-valued process in continuous time.
This has been inserted since
such equations occur in many applications, foremost in quautm mechanics but also in
the nowadays  popular subject referred to as "matheamatics of finance".
Let us only reamrk that the proof of Thoerem 8.2 is very simple and goes back to
Fourier and Laplace, i.e. it relies upon Taylor's formula up to second order
and
Fourier's inversion formula.

 
\medskip


\noindent

\medskip

\noindent
Measures are also used to   study  algebraic 
number fields. The text-book [Weil] by 
Andre Weil employs 
measure theoretic   results  to establish
reciprocity theorems and 
advanced results in 
class-field theory. In the appendix devoted to functional analysis we
establish the existence of Haar measures on
locally compact groups which in general are non-commutative.
\medskip


\noindent
{\bf{Stokes theorem.}}
Measures appear naturally when one seeks volumes and more generslly ares of
embedded submanifolds in euclidian spaces.
In ¤ 5 we establish the Federer-Stokes formula for domains in
${\bf{R}}^n$ and mention
that
this integral formula can be extended to manifolds.
However, then  one
one must employ
the more sophisticated notion of differential forms 
which would require a rather extensive detour into differential geometry.
\medskip


\noindent
{\bf{Integral inequalities and distribution theory.}}
The study of various $L^p$-estimates is an extensive subject which
we shall not try to treat. An exception is the account about
the Hardy-Littelwood maximal function in
¤ xx.
Here interplay with Fourier snalysis appears
together with
various geometric condtructions such as Whitney coverings.
The reader may consult a number of excellent
text-books by Elias Stein and co-authors 
which treat the theory about  singular integrals.
Passing to differentiability one is led to distribution theory.
In fact, a crucial   point is that
distributions in
${\bf{R}}^n$ are locally expressed by  finite sums of distribution derivatives
of Riesz measures. So in this sense measure theory is very essential to grasp
the theory of distributions.

\medskip


\noindent
Let us finish the introduction
by describing some  
results 
expressed via  measure theoretic
concepts but the  proofs require "hard analysis".
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf Hitting probability for the Brownian motion.}
The following result is due to the late
Bjšrn Dahlberg:

\medskip


\noindent
\emph{Let $U$ be a bounded open set in
${\bf{R}}^n$ defined by $\{F(x)<0\}$ where $F(x)$ is a 
Lipschitz continuous function and $\partial U=\{F(x)=0\}$. Then 
the class of null sets in $\partial U$
for the Brownian motion which starts
at some point $x_0\in U$ is equal to the class of subsets of $\partial U$ whose $(n-1)$-dimensional Hausdorff measure are zero.}
\medskip

\noindent
Above  $E\subset\partial U$ is a null set for the Brownian motion if the
probability to hit $E$ before the Brownian 
path reaches $\partial U$ at points outside $E$
is equal to zero.
For this discovery Dahlberg was awarded  the Salem 
Prize in 197?.
\bigskip

\noindent
{\bf {Carleson' convergence theorem in $L^2$.}}
In 1965 Lennart Carleson proved the following:
\medskip

\noindent
\emph{Let $\{a_n\}$ be a sequence of complex numbers whose $\ell^2$-norm
is finite, i.e. $\sum\, |a_n|^2$ is finite.
To every $N\geq 1$
one constructs Fourier's partial sum}:
\[ 
S_N(x)=\sum_{\nu=-N}^{\nu=N}\, a_ne^{inx}
\]
\emph{Then  $\lim_{N\to\infty}\, S_N(x)$ exists for all $0\leq x\leq 2\pi$
except for a set of 
Lebesgue measure zero.}
\medskip

\noindent
This result is one of the greatest achievements ever in mathematical analysis. 
In 2006 Carleson was awarded   the Abel Prize  for this   theorem
together with   his contributions in other areas of mathematics.
Already Carleson's Ph.D\vvv thesis from
1950 at Uppsala University when he was only 22 years,
contains  deep results concerned
with sets of uniqueness for boundary values taken
by
analytic functions in the unit disc where
measure theoretic concepts are expressed in terms of  capacities.




\medskip

\noindent
\emph{Arithmetical conditions}   appear
in
more subtle problems.
An example is the notion of Kronecker sets on the unit circle which
was introduced  in [Carleson]: Let $E$ be a compact subset
of the unit circle and  consider the class $\mathcal M\uuu *(E)$ of all
real\vvv valued Riesz measures $\mu$ supported by $E$ and of total variation
one. Put

\[
\text{Kron}(E)= \min\uuu{\mu\in \mathcal M\uuu *(E)}\,\,
\max\uuu{n\geq 0} \,\,\bigl|\,\int\uuu E\, e^{in\theta} \cdot d\mu(\theta)\,\bigr|
\]
This yields a number in $[0,1]$. 
If $E$ has positive Lebesgue measure we can choose 
a non\vvv zero function $f\in L^1(T)$
in the class $\mathcal M\uuu *(E)$
and then the sequence $\{\mu\uuu N= e^{iN\theta}\cdot f(\theta)\}$
stay in the class 
while
\[
\max\uuu{n\geq 0} \,\int\uuu E\, e^{in\theta} \cdot d\mu\uuu N(\theta)
=2\pi\cdot \max\uuu {k\geq N}\, |\widehat f(\vvv k)|\tag{*}
\]
where $\{\widehat f(\nu)\}$ are 
the usual Fourier coefficients. The Riemann\vvv Lebesgue
theorem entails that (*) tends to zero as $N\to \infty$
and hence
$\text{Kron}(E)=0$  whenever $E$ has  positive Lebesgue
measure.
But if $E$ is a null\vvv set in the sense of Lebesgue then
its Kronecker
index may be positive and even equal to one.
The positivity  of this index depends upon  arithmetic
properties of $E$.
A closed subset $E$ of $T$ is perfect if it contains no isolated points.
While it is trivial to construct perfect null\vvv sets it requires more
effort to find  closed and perfect sets $E$ such that
$\text{Kron}(E)=1$.
For such constructions we refer to ¤ XX and a good
example of   "refined measure theoretic analysis" is the following
result  from [Carleson: Theorem 9, p. 349]:
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{Theorem.}}
\emph{Let $E$ be a closed subset of $T$ with positive Kronecker index.
Then each continuous function $\phi(\theta)$ on $E$
has a representation}
\[ 
\phi(\theta) =\sum\uuu{n\geq 0}\, a\uuu n\cdot e^{in\theta}
\] 
\emph{where the sequence $\{a\uuu n\}$ is absolutely  convergent.}


\newpage






\centerline{\bf{0.1 The  Borel-Riesz theory.}}
\bigskip


\noindent
Given a 
Boolean algebra $\mathcal B$ of sets we
denote by ${\bf{ba}}(\mathcal B)$
the space of additive and bounded real-valued functions
on $\mathcal B$. Thus, one considers maps
\[ 
E\mapsto \mu(E)
\]
where additivity means that $\mu(\cup \,E_\nu)=\sum\, \mu(E_\nu)$
when $\{E_\nu\}$ is a finite family of disjoint sets
in $\mathcal B$. The boundedness means that there
exists a constant $C$ such that
\[
|\mu(E)|\leq C\quad\colon E\in \mathcal B\tag{*}
\]
Given $\mu$ we construct a 
function $\mu_+$
defined by
\[
\mu_+(E)= \max_{A\subset E}\, \mu(A)
\]
which is non-negative when we declare that
$\mu(\emptyset)=0$.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{Exercise.}}
Show that $\mu_+$ is additive, i.e. that
\[
\mu_+(E\cup F)=
\mu_+(E)+
\mu_+(F)\tag{0.0.1}
\] 
hold for every pair of disjoint sets in
$\mathcal B$.
Now $\mu_+-\mu$ is a non-negative  additive function 
denoted by $\mu_-$ and we get
\[
\mu=\mu_+-\mu_-\tag{0.0.2}
\]
Hence $\mu$ is represented as the difference of two non-negative and additive functions.
If $E\in\mathcal B$ the total variation of $\mu$ over $E$ is defined by
\[
|\mu|(E)=\mu_+(E)+\mu_-(E)
\]
The reader may verify that
(*) gives
\[
|\mu|(E)\leq 2C
\]
for every $E\in \mathcal B$.
\bigskip

\noindent
Let us now consider a compact topological  space $S$ 
which gives the Boolean algebra $\mathcal B_S$ generated by
open sets. It is clear that
every
set in $\mathcal B_S$ is  finite union of disjoint sets of the form
$U\cap F$ where $U$ are open and $F$ are closed.
Denote by $\chi(S)$
the linear space of real-valued functions on
$S$ given by
${\bf{R}}$-linear combinations of characteristic functions of
sets in
 $\mathcal B_S$.
 Thus, a function is of the form
 \[
 \phi=\sum\, c_k\cdot \chi_{E_k}
 \]
 where $\{E_k\}$ is a finite family of disjoint subsets from
 $\mathcal B_S$ and $\{c_k\}$ are real constants.
 To each pair
 $\phi\in\chi(S)$ and $\mu\in {\bf{ba}}(\mathcal B_S)$
 one defines the integral
 \[
 \int\, \phi\cdot d\mu= \sum\, c_k\cdot \mu(E_k)\tag{i}
 \]
Keeping $\mu$ fixed 
these integrals give a linear form
on the vector space
$\chi(S)$. To see this it suffices to
show  additivity:
\[
 \int\, (\phi+\psi)\cdot d\mu
  \int\, \phi\cdot d\mu=+
  \int\, \psi\cdot d\mu\quad\colon \phi,\psi\in \chi(S)\tag{ii}
 \]
 This follows because we can always represent the two functions
 in (i) with respec to a common partition of
 $S$, i.e. one can find
 $\{E_k\}$ so that
 \[
 \phi=\sum\, c_k\cdot \chi_{E_k}\quad\colon 
 \psi=\sum\, d_k\cdot \chi_{E_k}
 \]
 where $\{c_k\}$ and $\{d_k\}$ are constants.
 \medskip
 
 \noindent
 {\bf{0.1.2 Borel functions and their integrals.}}
 Via the maximum norm
 $\chi(S)$ is a normed vector space
 If $\mu\in {\bf{ba}}(\mathcal B_S)$ its total variation was defined in
 (0.1.1 ) and  the construction of integrals gives
 \[
 | \int\, \phi\cdot d\mu|\leq |\phi|_S|\cdot ||\mu||
 \]
 where
 $|\phi|_S$ is the maximum norm of
 $\phi$.
The closure of
$\chi(S)$ taken in the
normed space of bounded
real-valued functions on
$S$
yields a Banach space denoted by
$\mathfrak{B}(S)$ and called the space of bounded Borel functons on
$S$.
For each  $f\in \mathfrak{B}(S)$ a  $\mu$-integral is defined
as follows: By definition there exists a sequence
$\{\phi_n\}$ from $\chi(S)$ such that
$|f-\phi_n|_S\to 0$. When $n,m$ are positive integers we have
\[
|\int\, \phi_n\cdot d\mu
-\int\, \phi_m\cdot d\mu|\leq |\phi_n-\phi_m|_S\cdot ||\mu||
\]
The uniform convergence $\phi_n\to f$
entails that 
$\{|\phi_n-\phi_m|_S\}$ is a Cauchy-sequence
and hence there exists 
\[
\lim_{n\to\infty}\,
\int\, \phi_n\cdot d\mu
\]
It is clear that the last limit only depends on
$f$ and not upon the particular sequence in
$\chi(A)$ which approximates $f$ uniformly.
The common limit is defined as the
$\mu$-integral of $f$. Now   
\[
f\mapsto \int\, f\cdot d\mu
\]
gives a linear functional on
the normed vector space
$\mathfrak{B}(S)$ which
identifies
${\bf{ba}}(\mathcal B_S)$ with
a
subspace of the dual space
$\mathfrak{B}(S)^*$.
Conversely, let $L$ be a continuous linear functional on
$\mathfrak{B}(S)$ which means that there exists a constant
$C$ such that
\[
|L(f)|\leq C\cdot |f|_S\quad\colon f\in
\mathfrak{B}(S)
\]
Now $L$ yields a unique  additive measure
$\mu$ where
\[
\mu(E)=L(\chi_E)\quad\colon E\in\mathcal B_S
\]
We leave it to the reader that this entails that
\[ 
L(f)=\int\, f\cdot d\mu\quad\colon f\in \mathfrak{B}(S)
\]
Hence one has an isomorphism
\[
\mathfrak{B}(S)^*\simeq {\bf{ba}}(\mathcal B_S)\tag{*}
\]
\medskip


\noindent
{\bf{0.1.3 The dual of $C^0(S)$.}}
Tbe space of continuous and real-valued functions on
the compact space $S$ is  a closed  subspace of
$\mathfrak{B}(S)$.
To see this we take some
$f\in C^0(S)$ and notice that for every pair of real numbers
$a<b$, the
set
\[
\{s\colon a\leq f(s)<b\}
\]
belongs to $\mathcal B_S$.
If $\epsilon>0$ we can choose an integer $N$
so that
$N^{-1}>\epsilon$ and introduce the sets
\[
E^f_k(N)=\{s\colon\frac{k}{N}\leq f(s)<\frac{k+1}{N}\}
\]
where $k$ are integers. Since the range of $f$
is compact only a finite family are
non-empty
and we get the function in $\mathfrak{B}(S)$ defined by
\[
\phi=\sum\,\frac{k}{N}\cdot \chi_{E^f_k(N)}
\]
Here the maximum norm
$|f-\phi|_S<\epsilon$ and we conclude that
$f$ belongs to
$\mathfrak{B}(S)$.
Each
$\mu\in {\bf{ba}}(\mathcal B_S)$
restricts to a continuous linear functional on
the normed vector space
$C^0(S)$ which gives  a  map
\[
{\bf{ba}}(\mathcal B_S)\to C^0(S)^*
\]


\noindent
By the Hahn-Bach Theorem  this map is surjective
which gives
an isomorphism
\[
C^0(S)^*\simeq\frac{{\bf{ba}}(\mathcal B_S)}{C^0(S)^\perp}\tag{*}
\]
where
$C^0(S)^\perp$ is the space of  $\mu\in {\bf{ba}}(\mathcal B_S)$
such that
\[
\int\, f\cdot d\mu=0\quad \colon f\in C^0(S)
\]




\noindent
{\bf{0.1.4 Riesz' representation theorem.}}
A  measure $\mu$ in
 ${\bf{ba}}(\mathcal B_S)$ is regular if the following hold 
for every set $A\in\mathcal B_S$:
\[
\min_{A\subset U}\, |\mu|(U\setminus A)=0
\]
 where the minimum  is taken over  open
 sets $U$ which contain $A$ and 
$ |\mu|(U\setminus A)$ is the total variation 
over $U\setminus A$.
The family of these  measures is denoted by
${\mathfrak{M}}(S$) and  called Riesz measures on $S$.


\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.1.5 Theorem.}}
\emph{For each $\mu\in {\bf{ba}}(\mathcal B_S)$
there exists a unique
$\mu_*\in\mathfrak{M}(S)$
such that}
\[
\int f\cdot d\mu=
\int f\cdot d\mu_*\quad\colon\,f\in C^0(X)\tag{*}
\]
\emph{Moreover, one has 
an isomorphism}
\[ 
C^0(S)^*\simeq \mathfrak{M}(S)
\]
\medskip

\noindent
One refers to Theorem 0.1.5   as Riesz's representation theorem.
The proof relies upon the following
construction:
Let $\mu$ be a non-negative
measure in ${\bf{ba}}(S)$ and set
\[
\mu^*(A)= \min_{A\subset U}\, \mu(U)\tag{i}
\]
where $A\in\mathcal B_S$ and the minimum is taken over
open sets containing $A$.

\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.1.6 Exercise.}}
Verify that
$\mu^*$ is additive which amounts to show that
\[
\mu^*(A_1\cup A_2)=\mu*(A_1)+\mu_*(A_2)
\]
hold for each pair of disjoint sets in
$\mathcal B_S$. Notice  that
\[ 
\mu(A)\leq \mu^*(A)
\]
and it is clear that
$\mu_*$ is regular, i.e. $\mu_*$ is 
a non-negative
Riesz measure.
\medskip

\noindent
\emph{Proof of Theorem 0.1.5}.
We can write
$\mu=\mu_+-\mu_-$
and  get the regular measure
$\mu^*=\mu_+^*-\mu_-^*$. Now
(*) follows if  we prove this equality each pair
$(\mu_+,\mu_+^*)$ and
$(\mu_-,\mu_-^*)$. Hence it suffices to treat
the case when
$\mu$ is non-negative.
Since 
every $f\in C^0(S)$
is the difference of two non-negative functions
$f_+=\max(f,0)$ and $f_-=-\min(f,0)$,
additivity of integrals with respect to $f$
implkes that it suffices to show
(*) when $f$ is non-negative. In this case the
last inequality in (0.1.6)  gives
\[
\int f\cdot d\mu\leq \int f\cdot d\mu^*
\]
To prove  the reversed inequality
one proceeds as follows:
For each real number
we have the closed level set
$\{f=a\}$. Since $\mu^*$ is additive and the total mass
$\mu^*(S)$ is finite
we have
$\mu^*(\{f=a\})=0$ outside a (possibly empty)
denumerable set of real numbers.
Given $\epsilon>0$ we
find a finite sequence
$a_1<\ldots<a_N$
where
$\mu^*(\{f=a_k\})=0$ for every $k$ and
$a_{k+1}-a_k<\epsilon$ for each $k$, while
the interval $[a_1,a_N]$ contains the range $f(S)$.
Now
\[
\int f\cdot d\mu^*\leq
\sum\, a_{k+1}\cdot\mu_*(\{a_k\leq f<a_{k+1}\})\tag{i}
\]
Since $\mu^*(\{f=a_k\})=0$ for each
$k$
we  have by additivity
\[
\mu^*(\{a_k\leq f<a_{k+1}\})= \mu^*(\{a_k< f<a_{k+1}\})=
\mu(\{a_k< f<a_{k+1}\})\tag{ii}
\] 
where the last equality follows  since
the sets $\{a_k< f<a_{k+1}\}$ are open.
Hence (i) gives
\[
\int f\cdot d\mu^*\leq
\sum\, a_{k+1}\cdot\mu(\{a_k\leq f<a_{k+1}\})
\]
At the same time
\[
\sum\, a_k\cdot\mu(\{a_k\leq f<a_{k+1}\})\leq 
\int f\cdot d\mu\tag{iv}
\]
From (iii-iv) we see that
\[
\int f\cdot d\mu^*\leq\int f\cdot d\mu+\leq \epsilon
\cdot \mu(S)\tag{v}
\]
Since $\epsilon$ is arbitrary
we get the equality (*).
\medskip

\noindent
\emph{The uniqueness part.}
There remains to show
that the Riesz measures  in
Theorem 0.1.5  are unique. This  amounts to show that if
$\mu\in\mathfrak{M}(S)$ is such that
\[
\int\, f\cdot d\mu=0
\]
for every $f\in C^0(S)$ then
$\mu=0$.
To show this we argue by contradiction, i.e. suppose that
$\mu(A)\neq 0$ for some
$A\in \mathcal B_S$.
Multiplying $\mu$ with a constant we can assume that
$\mu(A)=1$.
Let $\epsilon <1/4$ and by (xx) we find
a compact subset
$F$ of $A$ and an open set $U$ which contains
$A$ such that the total variation
\[
|\mu|(U\setminus F)<\epsilon\tag{i}
\]
Notice that (i)  gives
\[
\mu(F)\geq 1-\epsilon\tag{ii}
\]
Next, there exists $f\in C^0(S)$
whose range is contained in
$[0,1]$ while $f=1$ on $F$ and zero on $S\setminus U$.
It follows that
\[
\int f\cdot d\mu\geq \mu(F)-|\int_{S\setminus F}\, fd\mu\geq
\mu(F)-|\mu|(U\setminus F)\geq 1-2\epsilon>1/2
\]
This proves the uniqueness part in Riesz' theorem.
\bigskip


\noindent
\centerline {\bf{0.1.7 $\sigma$-additivity and the algebra
$\widehat{\mathcal B}_S$}}
\bigskip


\noindent
Above we started from the Bolean algebra $\mathcal B_S$
generated by open subsets of $S$.
By general set-theory there exists a unique smallest
$\sigma$-algebra $\widehat{\mathcal B_S}$
which contains
$\mathcal B_S$.
Sets in this family are called Borel sets.
Let us remark that one can construct
$\widehat{\mathcal B_S}$ by transfinite induction using
ordinal numbers. We shall not pursue this in detail but
refer to original work by Baire and Borel where
these constructions
are carried out in detail.

\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.1.8 Theorem.}}
\emph{Each  $\mu\in\mathfrak{M}(S)$ extends in a unique way
to a $\sigma$-additive measure $\widehat{\mu}$
on
$\widehat{\mathcal B_S}$. Conversely, every
$\sigma$-additive measure  on
$\widehat{\mathcal B_S}$ is equal to
$\widehat{\mu}$ for a unique
Riesz measure.}

\medskip


\noindent
{\bf{Remark.}}
The proof of this result is a bit involved because some transfinite induction is needed.
We refer to Kolmogorov's book [Kol] for a detailed account
about the contruction of $\sigma$-algebras and
extensions of $\sigma$-additive measures.
In ¤ xx we prove
Theorem 0.1.8
when
$S={\bf{R}}^n$ for some $n\geq 1$.
\medskip


\noindent
{\bf{0.}1.9 Null sets.}
Let $\mu$ be a non-negative Riesz measure
on the compact space $S$.
A subset $G$ is a null set with respect to $\mu$ if
\[
\min_{G\subset U}\, \mu(U)=0
\]
where $U$ are open sets containing $G$.
Let $\mathcal N(\mu)$ be the family of these null sets.
Additivity entails that
a finite union of null sets again is  null set.
Less obvious is that a denumerable union of null sets
is a null set.
To show this we consider s sequence $\{G_1,G_2,\ldots\}$
in $\mathcal N(\mu)$.
Let $\epsilon>0$ and for each $n$ we find an open set
$U_n$ containing $G_n$ such that
\[
\mu(U_n)\leq 2^{-n}\epsilon
\]
Now $U^*=\cup \,U_n$ contains
$\cup\, G_n$. Since $\mu$ is regular 
there exists a compact subset
$F$ of $U^*$ such that
$\mu(U^*\setminus F)<\epsilon$.
Now $\{U_n\}$ is an open covering of $F$ and Heine-Borel's
Lemma gives
some integer $N$ such that
the union of $U_1,\ldots,U_N$ contains $F$.
It follows that
\[ 
\mu(U^*)=\mu(U^*\setminus F)+ \mu(F)\leq
\epsilon+\mu(U_1\cup\ldots\cup U_N)\leq
\epsilon+\sum_{k=1}^{k=N}|\,
\mu(U_k)\leq 2\epsilon
\]
Since $\epsilon$ is arbitrsty small we conclude that
$\cup \,G_n$ is a null set.
\medskip

\noindent
In ¤ II where  abstract measure theory is exposed,
the result above is the starting point for the
study of "almost everywhere defined 
functions"
with respect to $\mu$
which leads to the space $L^1(\mu)$ of functions which are
integrable with respect to $\mu$.
\medskip


\noindent
{\bf{0.1.10 An example.}}
Consider the case when $S=[0,1]$ is the compact unit interval.
Here we find the Borel function $b(s)$ which is 0 when
$0\leq s<1/2$ while $b(s)=1$ if $1/2\leq s\leq 1$.
So $b$ has a jump discontinuity at $s=1/2$.
It is clear that
\[
\min_f\, |b-f|_S= 1/2
\]
where the minimum is taken over continuous functions $f$.
The Hahn-Banach theorem gives
a linear functional $L$ with norm
two on $\mathfrak{B}(S)$
such that
$L(b)=1$, while $L(f)=0$ for every
continuous function on $[0,1]$.
By (*) in 0.1.2 there exists
an additive measure $\mu$
which represents $L$. 
The uniqueness  in Riesz' theorem entails that
$\mu_*=0$
so $\mu$ is not regular.
Let us remark that no explicit description of $\mu$ is available since the
existence relies upon the Hahn-Banach theorem which
uses the Axiom of Choice.
So every non-vanishing
additive measure $\mu$ which represents a linear functional
vanishing on
$C^0(S)$
is a "phantom"
which does not admit an explicit analytic description.
However, we have the
space
$\mathfrak{B}(S)$ which
is an example of a commutative Bnach algebra
and in ¤ xx we shall learn that
it can be identified with
the continuous functions
on the associated Gelfand space
which we denote by $\mathcal G_S$. Here Riesz representation theorem
applies and via (*) in (0.1.2) one has a
canonical isomorphism
\[ 
{\bf{ba}}(S)\simeq \mathfrak{M}(\mathcal G_S)
\]
Even though this appears as a conclusive formula we remark that
the Gelfand space
is huge since
the Banach space
$\mathfrak{B}(S)$ is not separable.



































 


















\medskip



\centerline{\bf{0.2  Lebesgue theory.}}
\medskip

\noindent

\noindent
The theory was  created  by Henri Lebesgue 
and presented in 
\emph{Lecons sur l'integration et la recherche
des fonctions primitives} from 1904. We  expose  some
basic facts from his work and remark
that 
Vitali and de la VallŽ Poussin
later gave some simplifications of Lebesgue's original proofs.
A major result in ¤ III
is the existence of
\emph{Lebesgue points} which
implies that  measurable 
functions in Lebesgue's sense are 
realized in a  concrete way. For example, a bounded
Lebesgue measurable function $f$ is 
the pointwise limit outside a null set of 
a sequence of Lipschitz continuous functions
defined by mean values of  $f$ over smaller and smaller cubes
centered at the point where  pointwise
convergence takes place.
In ¤ 3.xx we also show that
outside a null set an arbitrary bounded
measurable
function
$f$ differs from an everywhere defined function
$f_*$ given as the pointwise limit of
a
non-increasing sequence of upper semi-continuus functions which
means that $f_*$ belongs to the second Baire class of functions. So
when one agrees to identify Lebegue measurable
functions which are equal  outside a null set, then
this class is reduced to a  restricted family of everywhere
defined functions.

\medskip


\noindent
The crucial  point in Lebesgue theory is to define
the $n$-dimensional volume of a bounded open set.
To achieve this we use boundary expansions of real numbers.
If $n\geq 1$ and
$\square$ is a cube placed in
${\bf{R}}^n$
it has $2^n$ many corner points
and its size is measured
by the  positive number $a$ where  the distance between
two adjacent corner points is $a$, and the $n$-dimensional volume
is  $a^n$.
To construct the  $n$-dimensional measure
of an  open set $\Omega$ in ${\bf{R}}^n$ we employ
binary expansions 
of real numbers $x$:
\[ 
x=[x]+\sum_{k=1}^\infty\,\epsilon_k(x)2^{-k}\tag{i}
\]
where $[x]$
is the largest integer $\leq x$ and 
each $\epsilon$ is 0 or 1. The expansion is unique 
under the rule  that
the the sequence 
$\{\epsilon_k(x)\}$ never terminates with
1 all the time. For example,  the number $x=1/8$ 
has $\epsilon_3(x)=1$ while all other $\epsilon$-numbers are zero.
So it is not permitted  to represent $1/8$ by
the geometric series
$1/16+1/32+1/64+\ldots$.
If $N=0$ we put $\xi_0(x)=[x]$ and if $N\geq 1$
we put
\[
\xi_N(x)=[x]+\sum_{k=1}^{k=N}\,\epsilon_k(x)2^{-k}
\implies
\xi_N(x)\leq x< \xi_N(x)+2^{-N}\tag{ii}
\]
Next, if $n\geq 2$ and $p=(x_1,\ldots,x_n)$ is a point in
 ${\bf{R}}^n$ one takes the binary expansion of each
 coordinate
 and set
 $\xi_N(p)=(\xi_N(x_1),\ldots,\xi_N(x_n))$.
For each non-negative integer $N$
one associates the half-open cube
\[
\square_N(p)=\xi_N(p)+\square_N^*
\quad\colon\quad 
\square_N^*=\{(x_1,\ldots,x_n)\,\colon\, 0\leq x_k< 2^{-N}\}
\]
The reader may verify that
\[
q\in 
\square_N(p)\implies\xi_N(q)=\xi_N(p)\tag{iii}
\]
and the last equality entails that
$\square_N(q)=\square_N(p)$.
Let us now consider a bounded open set
$\Omega$ in ${\bf{R}}^n$.
To each $p\in \Omega$ we find the unique smallest
non-negative integer $N_\Omega(p)$
such that
$\square_{N_\Omega(p)}\subset\Omega$.
If $k\geq 0$ we put
\[
D_k(\Omega)=\{p\in\Omega\,\colon\,N_\Omega(p)=k\}\tag{iv}
\]
Here (iii) entails that 
$\{D_k(\Omega)\}$
are disjoint subsets of
$\Omega$. Since $\Omega$ is open it is clear
that the union is equal
to $\Omega$.
Moreover (iii) entails 
that when $D_k(\Omega\neq \emptyset $
then it is 
a  union of  
pairwise disjoint half-open cubes:
\[
D_k(\Omega)=\cup\, \square_N(p_\nu)
\]
Since $\Omega$ is bounded
 every such union is finite. Denote by 
$\mu_k(\Omega)$  the number
of cubes in this disjoint union.
The $n$-dimensional measure is defined by
 \[
 \text{vol}_n(\Omega)=
\sum_{k=0}^\infty\,  \mu_k(\Omega)\cdot 2^{-kn}\tag{*}
\]
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.2.1 Unbounded open sets.}}
If $\Omega$ is an open unbounded set
we still get the unique partition 
formed by the disjoint subsets $\{D_k(\Omega)\}$.
One says that
$\Omega$ has a finite measure if
every 
$D_k(\Omega)$ is a finite union  and the series
\[
\sum\, \mu_k(\Omega)\cdot 2^{-kn}<\infty
\]

\medskip

\noindent
Starting from the construction of $n$-dimensional measures
of  open sets one 
constructs  outer and inner measures of an
arbitrary bounded subset $A$  of ${\bf{R}}^n$.
The  outer measure is defined by:
\[
|A|^*= \min_{A\subset \Omega}\, \text{vol}_n(\Omega)
\]
In particular the outer measure 
is defined for every compact set $E$ and the inner measure of
$A$ is defined by
\[
|A|_*= \max_{E\subset A}\, |E|^*
\] 
where the maximum is taken over compact subsets of $A$.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.2.2 Definition.}}
\emph{A set $A$ in 
 ${\bf{R}}^n$ has a finite Lebesgue measure 
if
$|A|_*=|A|^*<\infty $ and this common number
is denoted by
$|A|_n$.}
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.2.3 Null sets.}}
A  set is called a null set if
its outer measure is zero. The family of 
null sets is denoted
by
$\mathcal N({\bf{R}})^n$.
A property is said to hold almost everywhere if it is valid
outside a null set.
In Lebesgue theory one 
is often content to establish a result "almost everywhere", i.e.
null sets are reduntant.
Recall that a  set
$S$ is of type $G_\delta$ if it is
the intersection  of an decreasing sequence of open
sets $\{\Omega_n\}$. It is a set of type $F_\sigma$ if it is given
as the union of a decreasing familly of compact
sets.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.2.4 Exercise.}}
Show that if $A$ is  Lebesgue   measurable 
and
$\{E_\nu\}$ an increasing sequence of compact subsets such that
$|E_\nu|_n\to |A|_n$,
then $A\setminus \cup\, E_\nu$ is a null-set. Similarly, if
$\{\Omega_\nu\}$ an decreasing sequence of open sets
containing $A$ and
$|\Omega_\nu|_n\to |A|_n$ then
$\cap\, \Omega_\nu\setminus A$ is a null set.
Hence every measurable
set differs from
a set of type $F_\sigma$ by a null set, and similarly from
a set of type $G_\delta$.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.2.5 Lebesgue points.}}
Let $A$ be a  measurable set.
A point $x\in A$ has unit   density 
if
\[
\lim_{|\square|_n\to 0}\,
\frac {\text{vol}\uuu n(\square\setminus A)}{\text{vol}\uuu n(\square)}=0\tag{0.5.1}
\]
where 
the limit  is taken over
cubes which contain $x$.
It is not requested
that $x$ is the center of the cubes
and their sides need not be
parallell to the coordinate
axis.
The set  where  (0.5.1) holds is denoted by
$\mathfrak{Leb}(A)$.
A major result due to  Lebesgue 
asserts the following:
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.2.6 Theorem.}}
\emph{For  every  measurable set $A$
it follows that
$A\setminus \mathfrak{Leb}(A)$ is a null set.}
\medskip

\noindent
This fundamental  result is proved in ¤ 3.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.2.7 Measurable functions.}}
Let $f(x)$ be a real-valued function defined in some open and bounded
subset
$\Omega$ of
${\bf{R}}^n$.
It is  measurable in the sense of Lebesuge if the sets
\[
A_f(a;b)= \{ x\in\Omega\colon a\leq f(x)<b\}
\]
are measurable for all pairs of real numbers $a<b$.
The function $f$ has  Lebesgue value $a$ at a point
$x_0\in\Omega$ if
\[
\lim_{|\square|_n\to 0}\, \frac{|A_f(a-\epsilon,a+\epsilon)\cap \square|_n}
{|\square|_n|}=1\tag{0.7.1}
\]
hold for every $\epsilon>0$ where the limit of cubes is taken as in (0.5.1).
It is obvious that if (0.7.1)
hold for some
real number $a<$,
then it is uniquely determined and we set
$\mathfrak{Leb} f(x_0)=a$.
In ¤ xx we show that
 the measurable function $f$ has a Lebesgue value almost everywhere.
 More preciely, there exists a
null set $W$
of
$\Omega$ such that 
 $\mathfrak{Leb} f$ is defined in
$\Omega\setminus W$ and is equal to
$f$ in this set.
So up  to null sets every measurable function
$f$
is recaptured from its Lebesgue function, i.e.
ignoring a null set
$f$ can always be chosen so that
its values coincide with its Lebesgue values.
\medskip


\noindent
{\bf{0.2.8 Fubini's theorem.}}
Let $n$ snd $m$ be a pair of positive integers and 
$A$  a bounded and measurable subset of
${\bf{R}}^{n+m}$
whose points are written as $(x,y)$ with
$x\in {\bf{R}}^n$ and $y\in {\bf{R}}^m$.
To every $x$ we get the slice
\[
A(x)= \{ y\colon (x,y)\in A\}
\]
In ¤¤ we prove that
$A(x)$ are measurable sets in
${\bf{R}}^m $ for all $x$ outside a null set and
the almost everywhere defined function
\[
x\mapsto |A(x)|_m
\]
is measurable. After we have learnt how to construct
Lebegue integrals we also prove  the equality
\[
\int_{{\bf{R}}^n}\,  |A(x)|_m\, dx= |A|_{n+m}\tag{0.8.1}
\]







\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.2.9 Non-measurable sets.}}
There exist
non-measurable sets but in 
practice one does not encounter these ugly sets. In fact, one must appeal to
the Axiom of Choice to exhibit examples.
Using this axiom there exists a subset
$E$ of  the open interval $(0,1)$ with the property
that the
sets $\{E+ q\}$ are disjoint when $q$ runs over the set 
of rational numbers.
This set cannot be measurable. The reason is that
the disjointness would entail that if $q_1,\ldots,q_N$ is a set of rational numbers
between 0 and 1 then
\[
N\cdot |E|_*=
\sum_{\nu=1}^{\nu=N}= |E+q_\nu|_*\leq 2
\]
where the last equality holds since
each set $E+q_\nu\subset [0,2]$.
Since $N$ can be arbitrary large we have
$|E|_*=0$.
So if $E$ is measurable we have
$|E|= |E|_*=0$, i.e $E$ is a null set. 
Since $E+q$ is  translate of $E$ they also give  null sets 
for every rational number.
But a denumerable union of null sets is a null set which
gives a contradaction since
 $\cup \, (E+q)$ with the union taken over all rational numbers
is the whole real line.



\newpage



\centerline {\bf{0.3 Riesz measures in ${\bf{R}}^n$.}}
\medskip


\noindent
To each integer $N\geq 0$ 
we have the lattice
$2^{-N}\cdot {\bf{Z}}^n$
of points
$p=(p_1,\ldots,p_n)\in {\bf{R}}^n$ 
such that $2^Np_\nu$ are integers for
every
$1\leq \nu\leq n$. 
With the notstions from 0.2.4 we obtain for
each
$p\in 2^{-N}\cdot {\bf{Z}}^n$
the half-open cube
\[
\square_N(p)=p+\square_N^*
\]
Set
\[
\mathcal D_N=\{\square_N(p)\colon\, p\in 2^{-N}{\bf{Z}}^n\}
\]
Notice that each cube in $\mathcal D_N$ is the union of $2^n$ many disjoint cubes from
$\mathcal D_{N+1}$.
Suppose that  non-negative numbers
$\{\mu(\square_N(p))\}$ are assigned for all $N\geq 0$ and every
$p\in {\bf{Z}}^n$.
The assignement is additive if
\[
\mu(\square_N(p))=
\sum\, \mu(\square_{N+1}(q))
\]
where the sum is taken over the $2^n$ many
cubes
in $\mathcal D_{N+1}$  contained in
$\square_N(p)$.
Given  an additive assignement we
construct a $\mu$-measure for every bounded open
set $\Omega$  as follows: To each $k\geq 0$ we have the famliy
$D_k(\Omega)$ from (0.4)
and set
\[
 \mu_k(\Omega)=\sum\, \mu(\square_N(p))
\]
with the sum taken over a disjoint family on $D_k(\Omega)$.
Now
\[ 
\mu(\Omega)=\sum_{k=0}^\infty\, \mu_k(\Omega)
\]
Additivity  implies that
if $\{\Omega_\nu\}$
is a finite family of disjoint bounded open sets
then
\[
\mu(\cup\, \Omega_\nu)=\sum \, \mu_k(\Omega_\nu)
\]
Next, if $E$ is a compact set
we set
\[ 
\mu(E)=\min_{E\subset \Omega}\, \mu(\Omega)
\]
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.3.1 The regularity condition.}}
If  $N\geq 0$ and 
$0<\delta<1$ one has the compact cube
\[
\square_N[\delta]=\{x\colon 0\leq x_k\leq \delta\cdot 2^{-N}\}
\]
To each $p\in {\bf{Z}}^n$ we get the compact cube
\[
\square_N(p)[\delta]=p+\square_N[\delta]
\]

\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.3.2 Definition.}}
\emph{An additive $\mu$-measures is regular if
the following hold for every $N\geq 0$ and every lattice point
$p\in {\bf{Z}}^n$:}
\[
\mu(\square_N(p))= \lim_{\delta\to 1}\, 
\mu(\square_N(p)[\delta])\tag{0.9.2}
\]

\noindent
\emph{The class of such regular  measures is
denoteb by $\mathfrak{M}({\bf{R}}^n)$
and called the Riesz measures in
${\bf{R}}^n$.}

\medskip

\noindent
It turns out that (0.3.2) and additivity enable us to
define $\mu$-measures in a similar fashion as in Lebegue's case.
For example, (0.3.2) entails that
when $\Omega$ is a bounded open set then
\[
\mu(\Omega)= \sup_{E\subset\Omega}\,
\mu(E)
\] 
where the supremum  is taken over compact subsets.
Next, if $A$ is a bounded set one defines
its outer $\mu$-measure by
\[ 
\mu^*(A)=\min _{A\subset\Omega}
\mu(\Omega)
\]
Similarly the inner measure is defined
by
\[ 
\mu_*(A)=\max _{A\subset E}
\mu(E)
\]
One says that $A$ is $\mu$-measurable if
$\mu^*(A)=\mu_*(A)$ and the common number is denoted by
$\mu(A)$.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.3.4 Remark.}}
In ¤ IV we expose the theory about Riesz measures
where $\mu$-measurable functions and their $\mu$-integrals
are constructed.
We shall learn that  there exists an extensive class of
non-negative Riesz messures whose mass 
is concentrated to null sets in Lebesgue's sense.
The construction of  Riesz measure is not easy to grasp.
One reason is that apart from the additivity the regularity 
introduces a condition which
cannot be predicted in a straightforward manner since 
one needs the axiom of choice
to
obtain the whole class of non-negative Riesz measures
which correspond to non-negative linear functionals on the space of 
real-valued and continuous functions in
${\bf{R}}^n$ having compact support.
Important examples of a geometric  origin
appear
in connection with
Stokes Theorem 
in ¤ V.
\bigskip

\centerline{\bf{0.4 The 1-dimensional case.}}
 \medskip


\noindent
A construction due to Weierstrass
gave motivation for
measure theory. 
\medskip

\noindent{\bf{0.4.1 Weierstrass example.}}
Let $0<b<1$ be a real number and $k$ a positive integer.
Define  $f(x)$ by the series
\[
f(x)=\sum_{n=1}^\infty\, b^n\cdot \cos (k^n \pi x)
\]

\medskip

\noindent
Since $b<1$  the series of cosine-functions converges
uniformly so $f$ is a continuous
and $2\pi$-periodic.
Let $b=1/5 $ and the integer $k>5 $ is so large that
\[
\frac{3\pi}{k/5-1}\leq \frac{1}{4}\tag{*}
\]



\noindent
When (*) holds we will show  that
limes superior of inifinitesmal  difference quotients of $f$
are everywhere
$+\infty$, and similarly limes inferior are everywhere
$-\infty$. 
To prove this
we proceed as follows:
To each $x>0$ and every positive integer $n$
we find   the  integer
$\{k^nx\}$
such that
\[
k^nx=\{k^nx\}+q_n\quad\text{where}\quad
-1/2\leq q_n <1/2 
\]
Fix  some $m\geq 2$ and put
\[ 
h_m=\frac{1-q_m}{k^m}\tag{1}
\]
If $n\geq m$ we obtain

\[
k^n(x+h_m)= k^{n-m}(\{k^m x\}+q_m+1-q_m)=
k^{n-m}(\{k^m x\}+1)\tag{2}
\]

\medskip

\noindent
When $\{k^m x\}$ is an odd integer we 
take $n=m$ above and get
\[
\cos (k^m \pi (x+h_m))=\cos\, \pi(\{k^m x\}+1)=1
\]
At the same time
\[
\cos (k^m \pi x)=\cos\, \pi(\{k^m x\}+q_m)=\cos\,(\pi(1+q_m))\leq 0
\] 
where the last equality follows since
the absolute value $|q_m|\leq 1/2$.
Hence
\[
\cos (k^m \pi (x+h_m))-\cos (k^m \pi x)\geq 1\tag{3}
\]


\noindent
Next, consider the partial sum difference
\[
D_{m-1}(x) =\sum_{n=1}^{n=m-1}\, b^n\cdot [
\cos (k^n \pi (x+h_m))
-\cos (k^n \pi x)]
\]
The derivative of the cosine-function is the sine-function
whose maximum norm
is $\leq 1$. Hence
Rolle's theorem gives the inequality:
\[
|\cos (k^n \pi (x+h_m))
-\cos (k^n \pi x)|\leq k^n\pi h_m\quad\text{for all}\quad 1\leq n\leq m-1\implies
\] 
\[
|D_{m-1}(x)|\leq\pi h_m\cdot 
 \sum_{n=1}^{n=m-1}\, b^n\cdot k^n=
\pi h_m\cdot \frac{(bk)^m-1}{bk-1}
\leq 
\]
\[
\pi h_m\cdot \frac{(bk)^m}{bk-1}=
\pi(1-q_m)\frac{b^m}{bk-1}\leq \frac{3\pi}{2(bk-1)}\cdot b^m\tag{4}
\]
where the last inequality follows since $|q_m|\leq 1/2$.
Next, since the maximum norm of the cosine function is
$\leq 1$ the triangle inequality gives
\[
\sum_{n=m+1}^\infty\, b^n\cdot [
\cos (k^n \pi (x+h_m))
-\cos (k^n \pi x)]\leq 2\,\sum_{n=m+1}^\infty\, b^n\cdot=
\frac{2}{1-b}\cdot b^{m+1}\tag{5}
\]


A summation over $n$ and the triangle inequality therefore give:
\[
f(x+h_m)
-fx)\geq b^m- \frac{3\pi}{2(bk-1)}\cdot b^m-
\frac{2}{1-b}\cdot b^{m+1}\tag{6}
\]
\medskip

\noindent
Here
$b=1/5$ and together eith (*) we see (6) gives
\[ 
f(x+h_m)-f(x)\geq \frac{b^m}{4}\tag{7}
\]


\noindent
Inserting $h_m$ from (1) above
we obtain
\[
\frac{f(x+h_m)-f(x)}{h_m} \geq \frac{(bk)^m}{1-q_m}\geq
\frac{1}{6}\cdot (bk)^m\tag{8}
\]

\medskip
\noindent
where $1-q_m\geq 1/2$ was used.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{Conclusion.}}
Since $bk>1$ the right hand side tends to $+\infty$ when $k$ increases
and at the same time
$h_m\to 0$.
Following Weierstrass we  take 
some $0<x<1$ and
get the sequence of integers $\{ k^2x\}$ as $k=1,2,\ldots$.
It is clear that we find arbitrary large integers $k$ such that
$\{ k^2x\}$ is an odd integer and  (8) entails that
\[
\limsup_{h\to 0}\, 
\frac{f(x+h)-f(x)}{h} =+\infty
\]
\medskip

\noindent
At this stage we leave it to the reader to
verify that
the limes inferior is $-\infty$ when we instead use integers $k$ such that
$\{k^2x\}$ are even integers. So Weierstrass cosine-series gives a continuous
function
which fails to have derivatives at every $x$.







\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.4.2 Existence of derivatives.}}
Weierstrass' example lead us to find  of sufficient conditions
in order that a function has derivatives.
Lebesgue   proved that if $f(x)$ is a continuous and
monotone function, i.e. non\vvv increasing of
non\vvv decreasing, then $f$ has an ordinary derivative almost everywhere.
This is shown in  ¤ 1 where we also prove a  general
 result due to Denjoy and Young
which applies to an arbitrary real\vvv valued continuous function
$f(x)$ defined on some open interval $(a,b)$.
For each $a<x<b$ we set
\[ 
D^*(x)=\limsup\, \frac{f(x+h)\vvv f(x\vvv k)}{h+k}
\quad\text{and}\quad
D\uuu *(x)=\liminf\, \frac{f(x+h)\vvv f(x\vvv k)}{h+k}
\]
where $h$ and $k$ are positive when we pass to the limes
superior. 
It is clear that $f$ has a derivative in the usual sense
at a point $x$ if and only if
$D_*(x)=D^*(x)$.
The Denjoy\vvv Young result is:

\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.4.3 Theorem.}}
\emph{Outside a (possibly empty) null\vvv set $E$ of $(a,b)$
the following two possibilities occur for each
$x\in (a,b)\setminus E$: Either there exists a common finite limit
$D^*(x)= D\uuu *(x)$
or else one has}
\[
D^*(x)=+\infty\quad\text{and}\quad D\uuu *(x)=\vvv \infty
\]
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{Remark.}}
The specific example by Weierstrass is no accident. In fact, later work
by Dini, Mazurkiewics and Banach demonstrated
that in the family  of all continuos functions
defined on the unit interval $[0,1]$,
the generic function fails to have derivatives
at all points $0\leq x\leq 1$. 
From this point of view the discoveries by Lebesgue
were invaluable.


\medskip
\noindent
{\bf{0.4.4 Absolute continuity.}}
Let $f(x)$ be a real-valued continuous function
on $[0,1]$.
To each sequence $0=a_0<a_1<\ldots <a_N=1$
we set
\[
\text{Var}_f(a_\bullet)=
\sum\, |f(a_{\nu+1})-f(a_\nu)|
\]
Set 
\[
\rho(a_\bullet)=\max_\nu \,  a_{\nu+1}-a_\nu)
\]
One says that $f$ is absolutely continuous if
there to every $\epsilon>0$ exists $\delta>0$
such that 
the following impliction hold for all 
\[
\rho(a_\bullet)<\delta\implies
\text{Var}_f(a_\bullet)< \epsilon
\]
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.4.5 Theorem.}}
\emph{If $f$ is absolutely continuous then its derivative
$f'(x)$ exists almost everywhere and the following hold:}
\[
\int_0^1\, |f'(x)|<\infty\quad\text{and}\quad
f(x)= f(0)+ \int_0^x\, f'(t)\, dt\quad\colon \quad 0<x\leq 1
\]
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.4.6 Remark.}}
The theorem asserts that an absolutly continuous function
is the primitive of its almost everywhere existing derivative.
Conversely, if $\phi$ is an $L^1$-function on
$[0,1]$ in Lebegue's sense, then
\[
\Phi(x)= \int_0^x\, \phi(t)\, dt
\] 
is absoluterly continuous and
for every Lebegue point of $\phi$, the
derivative $\Phi'(x)$ exists and is equal to $\phi(x)$.
The proof is given in ¤ xx after we have introduced the space
$L^1(0,1)$ of Lebegue meausurable and integrable functions on
$[0,1]$.









\bigskip



\centerline{\bf{0.5  ‚antor sets.}}
\medskip


\noindent
On the  unit interval $[0,1]$
closed Cantor sets
arise by a succesive removal of open intervals.
A general construction goes as follows:
In the first step
an open interval  $J_1=(a_1,b_1)$  is removed where
$0<a_1<b_1<1$.
In the second   step one removes an open interval 
$J_{12}=(a_{12}<b_{12})$ from $(0,a_1)$
and an open interval 
$J_{22}=(a_{22},b_{22})$ from $(b_1,1)$.
One continues and after $n$ steps 
$1+2+\ldots+2^n$ many disjoint open intervals 
have been removed
and
there remains a disjoint union of closed intervals
$\{[a_\nu^{(n)},b_\nu^{(n)}]\colon  1\leq \nu\leq 2^{n+1}\}$.
Here $a_1^{(n)}=0$ and $b_{2^n}^{(n)}=1$
while
\[
a_\nu^{(n)}<b_\nu^{(n)}<a_{\nu+1}^{(n)}
\] 
In step $n+1$ one removes
$2^{n+1}$ open subintervals from each of the 
closed intervals above.
The union of all removed open intervals gives
an open set $U$ whose closed complement is called a Cantor set.
The  Cantor  set is of null-type arises if
the sum of the lengths of the removed open intervals is 
equal to one which means that
the closed complement $\mathcal C=[0,1]\setminus U$
is a null-set in Lebesgue's sense.

\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{Lebesgue functions.}}
To every  Cantor set
of null-type Lebegue constructed
a continuous non-decreasing function
$L(x)$ where $L(0)=0$ and $L(1)=1$ while $L$ stays constant on 
the removed open intervals.
The $L$-function is found via a limit of piecewise linear functions
$\{f_n(x)\}$. Namely, after $n$ steps
we have the closed intervals 
$\{[a_\nu^{(n)},b_\nu^{(n)}\}\colon  1\leq \nu\leq 2^{n+1}$ 
above.
Now
\[ 
f_n(x)= (\nu-1)2^{-n-1}+
2^{-n-1}\cdot \frac{x-a_\nu^{(n)}}
{b_\nu^{(n)}-a_\nu^{(n)}}\quad\colon 
a_\nu^{(n)}\leq x\leq b_\nu^{(n)}
\]
while $f_n(x)$ is constant on open intervals which have been
removed up to the $n$:th step.
So each $f_n$ is a non-decreasing piecewise linear function.
From the construction it is clear that the maximum norms
\[ 
\max_x\,
|f_n(x)-f_{n-1}(x)|\leq 2^{-n}
\]
for every $n$. Hence
$\{f_n\}$ converge uniformly to a continuous function
which yields the Lebesgue function associated to the Cantor set.
Since $\mathcal C$ is a null-set the derivative of the Lebesgue function
$L(x)$ is zero almost everywhere and yet
$L$ is a non-constant function.
This leads to an example of a  singular measure.
More precisely there exists a non-negative and $\sigma$-additive measure
$\mu_\mathcal C$ on $[0,1]$ where
the mass over an interval $[a,b]$ is the ordinary Riemann integral of
$L$ taken over this interval.
So Cantor's set-theoretic constructions give
a family of Riesz measures on $[0,1]$
which carry their  mass on
a null-set.
\medskip

\noindent {\bf{0.5.1 Arithmetic properties.}}
For certain applications one seeks special Cantor sets
$\mathcal C$ whose 
associated singular measure $\mu_\mathcal C$
has special properties. One such property
is
the behaviour of its Fourier series.
In ¤xx we expose a construction by Salem which shows that for
every number $0<p<1/2$ there exists a Cantor  set
$\mathcal C$ if null-type such that $\mu_\mathcal C$ satisfies
\[
\bigl|\int_0^1\, e^{2\pi i nx}\, d\mu_F(x)\bigr|\leq C\cdot |n|^{-p}
\]
for all $n$ and a constant $C$.



\bigskip

\centerline{\bf{0.6 Examples from function theory.}}









\bigskip

\noindent
Measure theoretic assertions   tend to be
vague when   results only asserts that
some "nice property" holds almost everywhere, while the
actual null\vvv set on which the property  is not
described.
To give an example  we consider a continuous and
real\vvv valued function $u(\theta)$ on the unit circle.
Define  $v(r,\phi)$
when $0\leq r <1$ and $0\leq\phi\leq 2\pi$ by

\[
v(r,\phi)=\frac{r}{\pi}\int\uuu 0^{2\pi}
\frac {\sin(\theta\vvv \phi)\cdot u(\theta)}{1+r^2\vvv 2r\cos(\theta\vvv \phi)}\cdot
d\theta
\]
It means that  $v$ is the conjugate harmonic function in
the unit disc of the harmonic extension of
$u$.
The Brothers Riesz' Theorem
asserts that
\[ 
\lim\uuu{r\to 1}\, v(r,\phi)=v^*(\phi)\tag{*}
\] 
exists almost everywhere, i.e. for all $\phi$ outside a null\vvv
set on $[0,2\pi]$.
However, the precise description of this null\vvv set in terms of the given
continuous function $u$ cannot be attained in general.
So  one must be content with assertion that radial limits
exist
almost everywhere.

\medskip


\noindent
{\bf{0.6.1 A boundary value problem}}
Let $f(x,y)$ be a real-valued and continuously differentiable  function
defined in an open rectangle $\square=\{ 0<x<a\}\times \{-1<y<1\}$.
The first order
partial derivatives
$f'_x$ and $f'_y$ may
satisfy integrability conditions.
For a given $p\geq 1$ we impose the $L^p$-condition:
\[
\iint_\square\, \bigl(|f'_x(x,y)|^p+
|f'_y(x,y)|^p\bigr)\, dxdy<\infty\tag{i}
\]
A result due to to Hardy, Littlewood and Paley
asserts that if $p>2$ then
$f(x,y)$ extends to a continuous function on
the half-open rectangle $\{0\leq x<a\}\times\{-1<y<1\}$, i.e.
the $L^p$-condition ensures that $f$ extends to define
a continuous function on boundary line where
$x=0$.
The case when $p=2$ leads to a  more involved situation.
The condition that
$f'_x$ and $f'_y$ are square integrable does not
ensure that $f$ is a bounded function in $\square$.
 Examples arise via Riemann's conformal mapping
 theorem
 which is explained in ¤ X from Chapter VI. However, 
the $L^2$-condition in (i) implies that
 \[
 \iint_\square\, |f(x,y)|^q\,dxdy <\infty
 \quad\text{hold for all}\quad q>2 \tag{ii}
\]
There remains to study the eventual existence of limits
\[
\lim_{x\to 0}\, f(x,y)\quad\colon\quad -1<y<1\tag{iii}
\]
when (i) holds with $p=2$.
When $f$
is harmonic in $\square$ and the $L^2$-condition holds in (i)
precise limit results due to Beurling appear in 
Special Topics ¤ xx. Here advanced analytic function theory is needed to
attain precise  measure theoretic results.
\bigskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.6.2 Measures in a logarithmic scale.}}
Let $E$ be a closed null-set on the unit circle $T$.
The  complement consists of open
intervals 
$\{\omega_\nu=\{e^{i\alpha_\nu}<
e^{i\theta}<e^{i\beta_\nu}\}$
and the condition that $E$ us a null-set means that
\[
\sum\, \beta_\nu-\alpha_\nu=2\pi
\]
Next, to each $t>0$ we have the closet
set
$E(t)$ of points
$e^{i\theta}$ such that
\[
\min_{e^{is}\in E}\,
|e^{i\theta}-e^{is}|\leq t
\]
Let $\phi_E(t)$ denote the linear Lebesgue measure of
$E(t)$.
Now one can impose the condition that
\[
\int_0^1\, \frac{\phi_E(t)}{t}\, dt<\infty\tag{*}
\]
Example show that this  is a more
restrictive than the sole condition that
$E$ is a null-set.
It turns out that (*) is a crucial condition in
several situations  related to analytic function theory.
In ¤ xx we prove a result due to Carleson 
which shows that  (*)   entails that
there exist non-vanishing 
analytic functions $f(z)$ in the disc algebra $A(D)$
which
are zero on $E$
and at the same time can be made differentiable of any
order $N\geq 1$.
Conversely a result due to Beurling shows that if
(*) diverges and $f\in A(D)$
is Hšlder continuous of some order
$>0$ on $T$, then $f$ cannot be zero on $E$ unless it is identically zero.
The proofs rely upon constructions using Herglotz integral and
a  measure theoretic result
which goes as follows:
Given the null set $E$ we have the complementary open intervals
$\{\omega_\nu\}$.
Denote by  $\ell_\nu$  the length of $\omega_\nu$.

\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.6.3 Proposition.}}
\emph{The integral (*) converges if and only if}
\[
\sum\, \ell_\nu\cdot \log \ell_\nu<\infty\tag{**}
\]
\medskip

\noindent
\emph{Proof.}
Arrange the intervals so that
$\{\ell_1\geq \ell_2\geq \ldots\}$ and
if $N$ is a non-negative integer we
denote by
$S_N$ the number of intervals such that
$2^{-N-1}<\ell_\nu\leq 2^{-N}$.
It is easily seen that
(**) converges if and only if
\[
\sum_{n=0}^\infty\, 2^{-N}\cdot N\cdot S_N<\infty\tag{i}
\]
For each $t>0$ we set
\[
\psi(t)= \sum_{\ell_\nu\leq t}\, \ell_\nu\tag{ii}
\]
It is clear that
\[
2^{-N-1}S_N\leq \psi(2^{-N})-\psi(2^{-N-1})\leq
2^{-N}S_N\tag{iii}
\]
Hence (i) converges if and only if
\[
\sum_{n=0}^\infty\, N\cdot( \psi(2^{-N})-\psi(2^{-N-1}))\tag{iii}
\]
Abel's partial sum formula entails
that
(iii) converges if and only if
\[
\sum_{n=0}^\infty\, \psi(2^{-N})<\infty\tag{iv}
\]
Moreover, the reader can check
that
(iv) holds if an only if the integral
\[
J=\int_0^1 \frac{\psi(t)}{t}\,dt<\infty\tag{v}
\]
To finish the proof there remains to show that
the convergence of (*) and (v) are equivalent.
One direction is easy for if $t>0$ and $\ell_\nu\leq t$
then $\omega_\nu$
is contained in
$E(2t)$. It follows that
\[
\psi(t)\leq \phi_E(2t)
\]
Using the variable subsitution $t\mapsto 2t$
we conclude that the convergence of (*) entails that of (v).
To get the converse we shall majorize $\phi_E(t)$.
With $t>0$ kept fixed we take an integer $N\geq 0$
and denote by $F_N$
the union  of $\omega$-intervals such that
$2^{N}t<\ell_\nu\leq 2^{N+1}t$ which gives
\[ 
\phi_E(t)\leq \psi(t)+\sum_{N=0}^\infty\, |F_N\cap E(t)|_1\tag{vi}
\]
Notice
that $|\omega\cap E(t)|\leq t$  for
each
$\omega\in F_N$.
Hence the last sum in (vi) is majorized by
\[
t\cdot \sum_{N=0}^\infty\, \psi(2^{N+1}t)-\psi(2^Nt)
\]
The summation only extends as long as $2^N\leq 2\pi$
and 
is therefore taken over a finite set of integers
whose  sum obviously is majorized by
$(2\pi-\psi(t))t$.
We conclude that
\[ 
\phi_E(t)
\leq (1-t)\psi(t)+ 2\pi t\leq
\psi(t)+ 2\pi t
\]
and then 
the convergence in (v) entails that of (*).

\bigskip




\centerline {\bf{0.7 Rational series}}
\bigskip

\noindent
We shall consider  series of the form:
\[ 
\sum\,\frac{A_\nu}{z-a_\nu}\tag{*}
\]
\noindent
Before general measure theory was
developed such
series  were 
studied by PoincarŽ, Goursat and Pringheim who used them to 
construct functions via  lacunary expansions.
The  general theory about  the series (*)  is foremost due Emile Borel who 
introduced
a class of generalised analytic functions
by extending Cauchy's concept  \emph{de fonction monogne}.
A full account of Borel's work would lead us too far
so the interested reader may consult his
text-book [Borel] for details. 
Non-trivial situations arise when the sequence
$\{a_\nu\}$ is everywhere dense in an open subset
$\Omega$ of ${\bf{C}}$, or even in the whole complex plane.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.7.1 A special case}}.
Suppose there exists some $\gamma>1/2$ such that
\[ 
\sum\, |A\uuu\nu|\cdot \nu^\gamma <\infty\tag{1}
\]
For a given
$\ell>0$ we consider the open discs $\{D\uuu \nu\}$
of radius $\ell\cdot \nu^{\vvv \gamma}$
centered at the points $\{a\uuu\nu\}$. The 2\vvv dimensional area
of the domain given by the union of there discs
is majorized by:
\[
\pi\cdot \ell^2\cdot \sum \nu^{\vvv 2\gamma}\tag{2}
\]
Let $C\uuu\ell ={\bf{C}}\setminus\,\cup \, D\uuu \nu$
be the closed complement.
These sets increase as $\ell\to 0$ and (2)
shows that 
the complement of $\cup\, C\uuu\ell$ has planar measure zero, i.e. 
it is a null\vvv set
in the complex plane. Next,
if $z\in C\uuu\ell$ the series
(*) is absolutely  convergent since
\[
\frac{1}{|z\vvv a\uuu\nu|}\leq \ell^{\vvv 1}\cdot \nu^\gamma
\]
hold for each $\nu$ and (1) gives therefore the absolute convergence
\[
\sum\, \frac{|A\uuu\nu|}{|z\vvv a\uuu\nu|}\leq \ell^{\vvv 1}
\]
When the sequence $\{a\uuu\nu\}$ is everywhere dense 
the interior of $C\uuu\ell$ is empty so
the resulting function 
\[ 
f(z)= 
\sum\,\frac{A_\nu}{z-a_\nu}\tag{*}
\]
is defined on  every closed set $C\uuu\ell$ so it
cannot be regarded as an analytic function.
But $f$ is the limit of meromorphic functions
\[ 
f\uuu m(z)=\sum\uuu{\nu=1}^{\nu=m}\,\frac{A_\nu}{z-a_\nu}\tag{*}
\]
where the convergence  holds uniformly on $C\uuu\ell$ for every fixed $\nu$
and
one may therefore expect some analyticity properties of $f$.
Let us say that $f$ is of quasi\vvv analytic type
if it cannot vanish identically on any Jordan arc
$\gamma$ situated in $C\uuu\ell$ for some $\ell>0$.
The condition for $f$ to be quasi\vvv analytic depends on
$\{A\uuu\nu\}$. 
Even if $\{A\uuu\nu\}$ decreases quite rapidly 
there may exist
non\vvv quasi\vvv analytic series.
Examples where
\[ 
|A\uuu\nu\leq e^{\vvv n^\alpha}\quad\colon\quad 0<\alpha<1/2
\]
are given in [Denjoy 1922].
However, if $\{A\uuu\nu\}$ decreases sufficiently rapidly  then
quasi\vvv analyticity holds.
The following sufficiency result was proved by Carleman in [Carleman 1922):
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.7.2  Theorem.}}
\emph{Suppose there exists
$\epsilon>0$ such that}
\[ 
|A\uuu n|\leq e^{\vvv(\gamma+\epsilon)\cdot n\cdot \log n}
\quad\colon\quad n\geq 1
\]
\emph{Then $f(z)$ is quasi\vvv analytic in $C\uuu\ell$ for every $\ell>0$.}

\medskip


\noindent
The proof 
employs analytic function theory
and is given  in ¤ XX  from Chapter III.
Carleman's  book [xxx] contains several examples including necessity conditions for
certain classes of series in (*).
The interested reader should also consult [Wolff]
where  J. Wolff
constructs series as above
when $\{a\uuu\nu\}$ is everywhere dense and
(*) converges  at every point outside this denumerable set.
\medskip


\noindent{\emph{Another  case.}}
Consider the series (*) under the condition that
\[
\sum_{\nu=1}^\infty\, \sqrt{\bigl|A_\nu\bigr|}<\infty\tag{***}
\]
\medskip


\noindent
Let us  take a dense set
$\{a_\nu\}$ in some   open set
$\Omega$ and
an analytic Jordan curve $\Gamma$
given by the image under a
bijective and real analytic function
$t\mapsto z(t)$ from the  unit interval $[0,1]$.
When all the $a$-points are outside $\Gamma$ we can define
the partial sums
\[ 
S^\Gamma _N(t)=\sum_{\nu=1}^{\nu=N}\,\frac{A_\nu}{z(t)-a_\nu}\tag{1}
\]
We say that the infinite series (*) converges uniformly on $\Gamma$
if
$\{S^\Gamma_N(t)\}_1^\infty$ converges uniformly on
$[0,1]$.
Borel   proved that  (***) implies that
this uniform convergence holds
on  \emph{almost every
closed line segment} in the complex plane. 
To be precise, if we represent a line segment by 
its two end-points $(z_0,z_1)$ then 
there exists a nullset  $\mathcal N$ in ${\bf{C}}^2$
such that  
the series
\[ 
t\mapsto \sum\, \frac{A\uuu\nu}{tz\uuu 0+(1\vvv t)z\uuu 1
\vvv a\uuu \nu}
\]
converges uniformly for $0\leq t\leq 1$ whenever
$(z\uuu 0,z\uuu 1)$ is outside $\mathcal N$.
Next, relax the condition of uniform convergence on
$[0,1]$ to the weaker condition that $\{S^\Gamma_N(t)\}$
converge pointwise for all $t$ outside a null set on
$[0,1]$. With this relaxed notion of convergence 
Borel proved the following:
\bigskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.7.3 Theorem.}} \emph{Assume that $\{A\uuu\nu\}$ satisfies}
\[
\sum_{\nu=1}^\infty\, \bigl|A_\nu\bigr|^{\frac{2}{3}}<\infty
\]


\noindent
\emph{Then the  partial sum sequence $\{S^\Gamma_N(t)\}$
converges almost every along  every real\vvv analytic
Jordan arc $\Gamma$ in ${\bf{C}}$.}

\medskip

\noindent
The results above illustrate that the study of series (*) is a rich subject 
with  wide range of applications and  
many important problems remain unsolved. See
[Borcea. et.al.] for an account.


























\newpage

\centerline {\bf\large {I.  Measure theory on the real line.}}

\bigskip





\noindent
{\bf {Introduction.}}
The modern theory of integration
started in 1894 when Stieltjes published the article
\emph{Recherches sur les fractions continues} which contains a wealth
of new ideas; among others, a new conceptual integral
which   was used
to study
\emph{moment problems}. 
Given    a sequence of non-negative real numbers
$\{ c_n\}_0^\infty$ one asks if there exists
a non decreasing  function
$f(t)$ defined on the non-negative real axis $t\geq 0$
such that
\[ 
c_n=\int_0^\infty\, t^n\cdot df(t)
\quad\text{hold for all}\,\, n=0,1,\ldots\tag{*}
\]
To analyze this problem
Stieltjes constructed  integrals of the form:
\[
\int_a^b\, g(x)\cdot df(x)\tag{**}
\]
where $f(x)$ is a continuous and non-decreasing function
and $g(x)$ is a continuous function on the closed interval $[a,b]$.
Stieltjes defined the integral (**) as the limit of sums:
\[ 
\sum\, g(x_\nu)\cdot \bigl[f(x_{\nu+1}-f(x_\nu)\bigr]\tag{***}
\]
where $a=x_0<\ldots<x_N=b$ and $\max\,(x_{\nu+1}-x_\nu)$
tends to zero. The \emph{uniform continuity} of $g$ on 
$[a,b]$  gives a limit 
exactly  as for the ordinary Riemann integral.
Namely, consider the modulus
of
continuity:
\[ 
\omega_g(\delta)=
\max_{x_1,x_2}\,|g(x_1)-g(x_2)|\quad\colon\,
\text{maximum taken over pairs}\,\,\, |x_2-x_1|\leq \delta
\]
Put $M=f(b)-f(a)$.
If   $\delta>0$ and  $x_{\nu+1}-x_\nu\leq\delta$
hold for every $\nu$ in the partition which defines
(***), then Stieltjes proved that this 
sum  differs from a limit by a quantity which is 
$\leq M\cdot\omega_g(\delta)$. Hence the 
integral (**) exists since
uniform continuity of $g$
entails that 
\[
\lim_{\delta\to 0}\, \omega_g(\delta)=0
\]


\noindent
{\bf{Remark.}}
Stieltjes's beautiful discoveries about the moment problem
will not be
treated in these notes. We remark only 
that Stieltjes found a necessary and sufficient condition on
the sequence $\{c\uuu n\}$ in order that
there exists a function $f$ such that (*) above holds
expressed by a condition on the series of continued fractions associated to
$\{c\uuu n\}$. 


\medskip



\noindent
{\bf{Exercise.}}
Let
$f$ be a $C^1$-function, i.e.  the  derivative
$f(x)$ exists as a continuous function. Show that 
(**) is equal to the ordinary Riemann integral
\[
\int_a^b\, g(x)f'(x)\cdot dx
\]
If $g$ also is a $C^1$-function
then
partial integration gives
\[
\int_a^b\, g(x)f'(x)=g(b)f(b)-g(a)f(a)\int_a^b\, f(x)g'(x)dx\tag{1}
\]
\medskip

\noindent
Verify that 
the partial integration formula (1) remains valid
for Stieltjes' integrals, i.e. if  
$f$ and $g$ are 
continuous and non-decreasing on $[a,b]$ then   partial summations
give the equality:
\[
\int_a^b\, f(x)\cdot dg(x)+
\int_a^b\, f(x)\cdot dg(x)=f(b)g(b)-f(a)g(a)
\]



\newpage

\noindent
{\bf{0.1 Functions of bounded variation.}}
A continuous function $f(x)$ on $[a,b]$ has a bounded
variation if there exists a constant $M$ such that
\[
\sum_{\nu=0}^{\nu=N-1}\, \bigl|f(x_{\nu+1})-f(x_\nu)\bigr|\leq M
\]
for all partitions $a=x_0<x_1<\ldots<x_N=b$.
The Stieltjes integrals
\[ 
\int_a^b\, g(x)\cdot df(x)
\] 
are again defined for every continuous function $g(x)$.
To prove this one uses the
following result:
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.2 Decomposition Lemma.}}
\emph{Let $f$ have a bounded variation. Then there
exists a unique pair of continuous functions $(f_*,f^*)$ where
$f^*$ is non-decreasing and $f_*$ is non-increasing such that}
\[ 
f(x)=f^*(x)-f_*(x)\quad\text{where}\quad
f^*(a)=f(a)\quad \text{and}\,\, f_*(a)=0
\]
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{Exercise.}}
Prove this result.
The hint is to define
$f^*(x)$ for every  $a\leq x\leq b$ by 
\[
f^*(x)=f(a)+\max\, \sum\, f(\xi_\nu)-f(\eta_\nu)
\]
where the maximum is taken over sequences
$0\leq\eta_1<\xi_1<\eta_2<\ldots<\eta_N<\xi_N\leq x$.


\bigskip


\centerline {\bf{0.3 Borel's construction of integrals}}.
\medskip


\noindent
Stieltjes'  integral  can be computed in another way
using
the variation of $f$ over a certain  family of subsets
of $[a,b]$. This fundamental method   was introduced by Emile Borel in 1895.
Recall
that  a  bounded and open
subset $\Omega$ of ${\bf{R}}$  is  a unique 
union of pairwise disjoint open interval $\{(a_\nu,b_\nu)\}$.
In general this family is infinite but it is at most denumerable.
Let  $f(x)$   be continuous and non-decreasing.
The variation of $f$ over $\Omega$ is defined by
\[ 
\text{Var}_f(\Omega)=
\sum_{\nu=1}^\infty\, \bigl[f(b_\nu)-f(a_\nu)\bigr]
\]
If $E$ is a closed subset of $[a,b]$
we 
put:
\[
\text{Var}_f(\Omega\cap E)=
\text{Var}_f(\Omega\cap E)-
\text{Var}_f(\Omega\setminus E)\tag{i}
\] 
Next,
consider a
continuous function $g$ on $[a,b]$.
For each $\nu$
we get the following subset of $[a,b]$:
\[
S_g[a_\nu,b_\nu)= \{ x\quad\colon\quad a_\nu\leq g(x)<b_\nu\}
\]
Since  $\{g<b_\nu\}$ is open and  $\{g\geq a_\nu\}$ is closed, it follows from
(i) that 
the variation of $f$ is defined over 
these $S_g$-sets.
Suppose that  the range $g([a,b])$ is contained in
the interval $[-A,A]$ for some $A>0$. For
every
sequence
\[
 -A\leq \xi_0<\xi_1<\ldots<\xi_N=A
 \]
we set
\[
S_*(\xi_\bullet)=\sum\,\xi_\nu\cdot\text{Var}_f(S_g[\xi_\nu,\xi_{\nu+1})
\quad\text{and}\quad
S^*(\xi_\bullet)=\sum\,\xi_{\nu+1}\cdot\text{Var}_f(S_g[\xi_\nu,\xi_{\nu+1})
\]
When $M=f(b)-f(a)$ 
we see that
\[ 
S^*(\xi_\bullet)-S_*(\xi_\bullet)\leq M\cdot 
\max_\nu\, (\xi_{\nu+1}-\xi_\nu)\tag{0.3.1}
\]
Hence there exists 
\[ 
S=\lim\, S_*(\xi_\bullet)\tag{*}
\] 
where the limit is taken over arbitrary partitions of
$[-A,A]$ such that 
\[
 \max_\nu\, \, \xi_{\nu+1}-\xi_\nu\to 0
\]
Abel's summation formula entails that the limit  (*) is equal to
the  Stieltjes' integral, i.e.:
\[
S=\int_a^b\, g(x)\cdot df(x)\tag{0.3.2}
\]
This equality paves the way  to  constructions of integrals where
the regularity  of $g$ can be relaxed.
The reason is that  Borel's limit 
is more \emph{robust} as compared with
the construction by Stieljes. For let $A>0$ and consider the class
$\mathcal C_A$ of continuous functions $g(x)$ on $[a,b]$ whose range is contained in
$[-A,A]$.  To every $\xi$-sequence
$-A=\xi_0<\xi_1<\ldots<\xi_N=A$
we set
\[ 
\delta(\xi_\bullet)=\max\uuu\nu \, \xi_{\nu+1}-\xi_\nu
\]
By (0.3.1)  the lower- resp. the over-sums
$S_*$ and $S^*$  differ from the limit $S$  in (*) by
number majorized by
$A\cdot \delta(\xi_\bullet)$.
This hold for
 all $g$-functions in $\mathcal C_A$. So  
the rate of convergence 
 depends upon $\delta(\xi_\bullet)$ and not upon the chosen
 $g$-function in $\mathcal C_A$,
 i.e. the rate of convergence in Borel's  construction 
 is \emph{independent} of the modulus of continuity of an
 individual $g$-function.
 \bigskip
 

\centerline {\bf{0.4 Borel functions.}}
\medskip

\noindent
The  robust limit  above enable us
to construct integrals
where the $g$-functions need not be
continuous.
In fact,  there exists the integral
\[ 
\int_a^b\, g(x)df(x)\tag{0.4.1}
\] 
under the sole assumption that $g$ is a \emph{bounded
and Borel measurable function}. This 
means that $g(x)$ is a  function on
$[a,b]$ with some bounded range $[-A,A]$ and 
for each pair of real numbers
$\eta<\xi$ the
set $\{\eta\leq g<\xi\}$
belongs to the Borel algebra $\mathfrak  B$
which by definition
is the 
smallest Boolean $\sigma$-algebra of subsets of ${\bf{R}}$  containing all half-open
intervals. 
To define (0.4.1) for a  bounded Borel function $g$
one  first constructs the variation of $f$ over an arbitrary Borel set.
Once this is done the existence  of limits which
give (0.4.1) follow exactly as in the case when
$g$ is a continuous function. 
\medskip



\centerline {\bf{0.5 Baire classes.}}
\medskip


\noindent
The class $\mathcal B$ of Borel measurable functions
arises from 
increasing  families of functions.
First
$\mathcal B_0$ is the family of continuous functions.
Next, a function $g$ is of the first Baire class if it
is equal to the \emph{pointwise limit} of some sequence of
continuous functions.
This gives the  class $\mathcal B_1$. Next we get the class 
$\mathcal B_2$ which consists
of pointwise limits of $\mathcal B_1$-functions. One proceeds by
an induction over positive integers and arrive at the class
\[
 \mathcal B_\infty=\cup\, B_n
\]
However, this does not stop the process via 
pointwise convergence.
The reason is that 
there exist
sequences $\{g_k\}$ of functions in $B_w$
which have a pointwise limit function which does not
belong to $B_\infty$, i.e. the limit function does not belong to
$B_w$ for a given integer $w$.
To get a family of functions which is stable under pointwise
limits one must continue and define
$\mathcal B$-classes by an induction using
\emph{ordinal numbers}.
We shall not pursue this any further but refer to the extensive literature about
Baire's  construction of all Borel functions on the real line.
See for example [XXX].


\bigskip




\centerline{\bf\ {0.6 Monotone functions and their Riesz measures}}.
\medskip

\noindent
On an interval $[a,b]$ we  consider a
non-decreasing and continuous functions $f(x)$.
We have the $\sigma$-algebra $\mathfrak{B}[a,b]$ of Borel sets in
$[a,b]$. Consider 
a denumerable family of disjoint half-open intervals $\{[a_\nu,b_\nu)\}$
on $[a,b]$.
Then
the positive series

\[ \sum\, f(b_\nu)-f(a_\nu)\tag{1}
\] 
converges.
Next, since
$\mathfrak{B}[a,b]$ is the smallest $\sigma$-algebra which contains
half-open subintervals of $[a,b]$, the convergence in (1)
and 
general set-theoretic arguments imply that
the variation of $f$ is defined over
every Borel set $S$, i.e. we obtain a $\sigma$-additive measure
$\mu_f$ on $\mathfrak{B}[a,b]$
where

\[ 
\mu_f(S)=\text{Var}_f(S)
\] 
holds for every Borel set $S$
in $[a,b]$. More precisely, 
\[ 
S\mapsto \mu_f(S)\tag{2}
\]
yields a $\sigma$-additive map.
\medskip


\noindent
{\bf{A converse result.}}
Suppose that we have a non-negative $\sigma$-additive map
\[
S\mapsto \mu(S)
\]
from
$\mathfrak{B}[a,b]$ into the set of non-negative real numbers.
Thus, if $\mu$ is normalised so that
its mass on $[a,b]$ is one, then
it is a probability measure on the sample space
formed by
$[a,b]$ and $\mathfrak{B}[a,b]$ in the sense of abstract measure theory.
To $\mu$ we associate the non-decreasing function
$f(x)$ defined by
\[
f(x)= \mu([a,x])\quad\colon a\leq x\leq b
\]


\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{Exercise}}.
Prove  the equality
\[ 
\mu=\mu_f\tag{1}
\] 
under the assumption that
$\mu$ has no \emph{discrete point masses},
i.e. that
the $\mu$-mass is zero on every singleton set. An equivalent
condition is that
\[ 
\mu(\eta,\xi)=\mu([\eta,\xi])\tag{2}
\]
hold for all pairs $\leq \eta<\xi\leq b$.
When $\mu$ satisfies this condition
one says that
$\mu$ has no atoms or refer to  an atomless measure.
Hence   we can conclude:
\medskip


\noindent
{\bf{0.6.1 Proposition.}}\emph{
 There exists  a 1-1 correspondence between
the class of non-decreasing continuous functions on
$[a,b]$ and the class of $\sigma$-additive and 
non-negative measurs on
$[a,b]$ without atoms.}
\bigskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.6.2 Discrete measures and jump functions.}}
Let $\{x_k\}$ be a sequence of points on
${\bf{R}}$  indexed by positive integers.
No special assumption is imposed. For example, the sequence may
be some enumeration of all rational numbers.
If $\{p_k\}$ is a sequence of positive numbers such that the series
$\sum\, p_k<+\infty$ then we get the jump-fuction
\[ 
s(x)=\sum\, p_k\cdot H_{x_k}(x)\tag{1}
\] 
Above we  introduced  Heaviside functions, i.e. for every
real number $x_*$ we define $H_{x_*}(x)=1$ if
$x\geq x_*$ and 0 when $x<x_*$.
To the $s$-function corresponds the discrete measure which assigns
the mass $p_k$ at every $x_k$.
\medskip

\noindent
This yields a 1-1 correspondence between non-decreasing jump functions and
non-negative discrete measures.
A non-trivial result due to Lebesgue and Vitali
goes as follows:

\medskip

\noindent{\bf{0.6.3 Theorem.}}
\emph{Every jump-function $s(x)$ has an ordinary derivative whole value is zero outside
a nullset.}
\medskip

\noindent
We prove this in ¤ 1.15 below.
Let us now consider an
arbitrary $\sigma$-additive and non-negative measure $\mu$
defined on $\mathfrak{B}[a,b]$.
It may have point masses.
Since the total mass of $\mu$ over $[a,b]$ is finite
the sum of all mass assigned to
the 
atoms is finite, i.e.
we get a discrete part given by

\[
 \mu_d=\sum\, p_k\cdot \delta_{x_k}
\]
Here $\sum\,p_k<\infty$ and $\{x_k\}$ is at most a denumerable
subset of $[a,b]$.
The difference $\mu-\mu_d$ has no atoms and corresponds 
to a non-decreasing continuos function
$f$ as above. So here $\mu-\mu_d=\mu_f$ and one refers to
$\mu_f$ as the continuous part of $\mu$. It
is clear that this decomposition of $\mu$ is unique.




\newpage


\centerline {\bf{0.7 Signed measures.}}
\bigskip

\noindent
A continuous function
$f$ on $[a,b]$ has a bounded variation if there exists a constant
$C$ such that
\[
\sum\, \bigl|f(\xi_{\nu+1})-f(\xi_\nu)\bigr|\leq C
\] 
for all partitions
$a=\xi_0<\ldots<\xi_N=b$.
The smallest number $C$ for which this holds is called the total
variation of $f$ and is denoted by
$V(f)$.
A  result  due to  Ascoli asserts the following:
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.8 Theorem.}}
\emph{Let $f$ be a continuous function with bounded
variation. Then there exists a unique pair of non-decreasing functions
$g,h$ such that}
\[ 
f(x)=g(x)-h(x)
\]
\emph{where $g(a)=f(a)$ and $h(a)=0$.}
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf {Exercise.}}
Prove this. Here is the hint is to construct the $g$-function. Put
\[
\phi (x)=f(a)+
\max\, \sum_{\nu01}^{\nu=N}\, f(\xi_\nu-f(\eta_\nu)
\] 
where the maximum is taken over
all sequences 

\[
a<\eta_1<\xi_1<\eta_2<\ldots<\xi_{N-1}<\eta_N<\xi_N\leq x
\]
