Intuitively, we seek  intervals in $[0,x]$ where
$f$ increases as much as possible, while eventual intervals where
$f$ decrease are omitted.
The reader should verify that
the $\phi$-function is non-decreasing and continuous and that
the function
\[
x\mapsto f(x)-\phi(x)
\]
is non-increasing.
Now 
$g=f(a)+\phi(x)$ and 
$h=f(x)-\phi(x)-f(a)$. This proves the existence in Theorem 0.8 and the
proof of uniqueness is left to the reader.
\bigskip

\noindent
{\bf{The signed measure $\mu_f$.}}
The decomposition above gives
a  signed measure $\mu_f$ defined by

\[ 
\mu_f=\mu_g-\mu_h
\]
In
this decomposition  there
exists a Borel set $S$ in $[a,b]$ such that
\[ 
\mu_g([a,b])=\mu_g(S)
\quad\text{and}\quad 
\mu_h([a,b])= 
\mu_h([a,b]\setminus S)
\]
This means that
$\mu_g$ and $\mu_h$ are 
orthogonal and one writes
\[
 \mu_g\perp\mu_h
\]


\noindent
Summing up we have the following
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.9 Theorem}}.
\emph{There exists a 1-1 correspondence between the family of signed Riesz measures
$\mu$ on $[a,b]$ without atoms and the class of continuous 
functions $f(x)$ of bounded variation
normalised with $f(a)=0$.}
\medskip

\noindent
Let us remark that the results
above
are special cases
of  the general decomposition by
Hahn which is proved in  the section devoted to  abstract measure theory.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.10 Borel-Stieltjes  integrals.}}
The previous material shows that if
$f$ is a continuous function of bounded variation on
$[a,b]$ then there exists an integral
\[ 
\int_a^b\, g(x)\cdot d\mu_f(x)=
\int_a^b\, g(x)\cdot df(x)\tag{*}
\] 
for every bounded Borel function $g(x)$.
\medskip


\centerline{\bf{0.11 Weak limits of measures}}
\medskip

\noindent
Above we have seen
that every  signed Riesz measure $\mu$
on $[a,b]$ is  a unique sum of
an atomless measure $\nu_c$ and a discrete measure $\mu_d$.
It turns out that the class of discrete measures recapture all Riesz
measures after suitable passage to the limit.
Let us clarify this and begin with the case of non-negative measures.
Consider a  positive integer $N$ and some
$N$-tuple $a\leq\xi_1<\ldots<\xi_N\leq b$ and a sequence
$\{c_\nu\geq 0\}$ where $\sum\, c_\nu=1$
To this we associate the discrete measure
$\mu_N$ and obtain: 
\[
\int\, g\cdot d\mu_N=\sum\, c_\nu\cdot g(\xi_\nu)\quad\text{for every}\,\,  g\in C^0[a,b]
\] 
\medskip

\noindent
Suppose now that there exists the limits:
\[
\lim_{N\to\infty}\, 
\int\, g\cdot d\mu_N\quad\text{for all}\quad g\in C^0[a,b]\tag{*}
\]
When (*)  holds we say that $\{\mu_N\}$ is weakly convergent.
Now the following holds:
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.12 Theorem}}.
\emph{Let $\{\mu_N\}$ be  weakly convergent.
Then there
exists a unique non-negative Riesz measure 
$\mu$  such that}
\[
\lim_{N\to\infty}\, 
\int\, g\cdot d\mu_N=\int_a^b\, g\cdot d\mu
\]
hold for all $g\in C^0[a,b]$.

\medskip

\noindent
\emph{Proof.}
The existence of limits
mean that we have a linear map
\[ 
g\mapsto \lim_{N\to\infty}\, 
\int\, g\cdot d\mu_N=\int_a^b\, g\cdot d\mu
\]

\noindent
on the  vector space
$C^0[a,b]$. Let us denote it by $L$.
Notice that $g\geq 0$ gives
$L(g)\geq 0$ and taking the identity function we have $L(1)=1$.
Now we   construct a non-decreasing function
$f(x)$ as follows:
To each $a<x\leq b$
we consider the family $\mathcal F_x$
of continuos functions
$g$ such that $0\leq g\leq 1$  and
there exists some $\epsilon>0$ with
$g(y)=0$ when $y\geq x-\epsilon$. In other words, $g$ has a support given by a compact subset
of the half-open interval 
$[a,x)$.
Set
\[ 
f(x)=\max_{g\in\mathcal F_x}\, L(g)
\]
Here $f(a)=0$ and it is obvious that $f$ is non-decreasing.
The reader should also verify that
$f$ is left continuous, i.e.
\[
\lim_{\epsilon\to 0}\, f(x-\epsilon)=f(x)
\] 
hold for every $a<x\leq b$.
There may exist a 
set $\{\eta_\nu\}$
where $f$ is discontinuous. Here a jump at
$\eta_\nu$ is given by a positive number
\[ 
\rho_\nu=\lim_{\epsilon\to 0}\, f(\eta_\nu+\epsilon)-f(\eta_\nu)
\]
To every such point we have the 
Heaviside function $H_\nu(x)$ and 
define the jump function
\[ 
s(x)=\sum\,\rho_\nu\cdot H_\nu(x)
\]
Then $f-s=f_*$ is continuous and we get the Riesz measure
\[ 
\mu= \mu_d+\mu_{f_*}
\] 
where the discrete part
\[
\mu_d=\sum\, \rho_\nu\cdot \delta_{\eta_\nu}
\]


\noindent
At this stage we leave it to the
reader
to verify the
equality below  for every  continuous functions $g(x)$.
\[ 
L(g)=\int_a^b\, g\cdot d\mu
\] 
Now Theorem 0.12 follows from the  Riesz representation theorem for 
non-negative and continuous linear functionals
on $C^0[a,b]$.


\medskip


\noindent
{\bf{Remark.}}
If $X^*$ denotes the dual space of the Banach space
$C^0[a,b]$ the sequence
$\{\mu_N\}$ converge in the so called weak-star
topology to $\mu$.
Conversely,
let $\mu$ be a non-negative Riesz measure on $[a,b]$ of total mass one.
We also assume that $\mu$ is continuous. So $\mu=\mu_f$ for some
non-decreasing and continuous function $f$ on $[a,b]$ with $f(a)=0$.
In this situation we can approximate
$\mu$ weakly by a sequence of discrete measures as follows:
For every positive integer $N\geq 2$
we define the discrete measure
\[ 
\mu_N=\sum_{k=1}^{k=N}\, p_k\delta_{x_k}
\]
where
\[
p_k=f(a+\frac{k(b-a)}{N})-f(a+\frac{(k-1)(b-a)}{N})
\quad\text{and}\quad  x_k=a+\frac{k(b-a)}{N})
\]
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{Exercise.}}
Show that the sequence $\{\mu_N\}$ converges weakly to $\mu_f$.
The hint is to use uniform continuity of  the $g$-functions
in Theorem 0.12.

\bigskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.13 Cantor's function.}}
Consider the unit interval $[0,1]$.
Let $\mu_1=\frac{1}{2}[\delta_0+\delta_1]$.
Next, we get 
\[
\mu_2=\frac{1}{4}[\delta_0+\delta_{1/3}+\delta_{2/3}+\delta_1]
\]
\[
\mu_3= \frac{1}{8}\cdot 
[\delta_0+\delta_{1/9}+\delta_{2/9}+\delta_{3/9}+
[\delta_{6/9}+\delta_{7/9}+\delta_{8/9}+\delta_1]
\]
and so on where
the reader can recognize the inductive
construction.
Intuitively one removes
one third  of middle intervals at each step.
For every integer  $k\geq 4$ we get a discrete measure $\mu_k$
which assigns the point mass $2^{-k}$ at $2^k$ many points.

\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.14 Exercise.}}
Show
that
the sequence $\{\mu_k\}$ converges
weakly to a  limit measure  $\mu_f$ where $f$ is  continuous and
non-decreasing. The notable fact is that
$f$ is constant on
many intervals. To begin with it is constant on the middle interval $(1(3,2/3)$.
It is also constant on the intervals $(1/9,2/9)$ and $(7/9,8/9)$.
The total length on intervals where $f$ is constant becomes

\[
\frac{1}{3}+2\cdot \frac{1}{3^2}+\ldots=
\frac{1}{3}\cdot \sum_{\nu=0}^{\infty}\, (2/3)^\nu=1\tag{*}
\]

\noindent
The support of the measure $\mu_f$ is the closed complement of the removed
intervals above.
The construction is remarkable since
$f$ has a vanishing derivative in each  removed
open interval and at the same time   (*) holds while 
$f$ increases from zero to $f(1)=1$
so the function is not constant.
Hence Cantor's  function $f$ violates the fundamental theorem of calculus, i.e.
it is not recovered by an integral of its derivative.
Moreover, there is  defined intervals on the closed complement of the removed intervals above.

\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.15 Absolutely continuous functions.}}
Let $f$ be a non-decreasing 
and continuous function on $[0,1]$ with $f(0)=0$ and $f(1)=1$.
To each $0<\delta<1$ we consider
open  sets which consist of pairwise disjoint open intervals
$\{(a_\nu,b_\nu)\}$ where

\[
\sum\, (b_\nu-a_\nu)<\delta\tag{*}
\]

\noindent
No condition is imposed on the number of these intervals.
With $\delta$ fixed we set:
\[
V_f(\delta)= \max\, \sum\, f(b_\nu)-f(a_\nu)
\] 
where the maximum is taken over all finite families
$\{(a_\nu,b_\nu)\}$
for which (*) hold.
\bigskip


\noindent
{\bf{0.16 Definition.}}
\emph{The function $f$ is called absolutely continuous if}
\[
\lim_{\delta\to 0}\, V_f(\delta)=0
\]
\emph{The space of such functions is denoted by
$AC^0[0,1]$.}
\medskip


\noindent
It turns out that if 
$f\in AC^0[0,1]$ then it is recaptured from its derivative $f'(x)$.
This relies upon the theorem by F. Riesz in the next section which shows that
$f$ has an ordinary derivative outside a null set in the sense of Lebesgue.
After this one constructs the Lesbegue integral
of $f'$ to get a primitive function defined by

\[ 
F(x)=\int_0^x\, f'(t)\cdot dt
\]
The almost everywhere
existence of Lebesgue points implies that
the derivative of $F$ is equal to that of $f$ almost everywhere and
there remains to consider the difference
\[
g=f-F
\]
Here $g$ belongs to 
$AC^0[0,1]$  and its derivative is almost everywhere zero.
From this one can show that $g$ is identically zero and which gives
the equality
\[ 
f(x)=\int_0^x\, f'(t)\cdot dt\tag{*}
\]
In other words, every  absolutely continuous function is recovered by its derivative.
\bigskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.17 Fubini's theorem.}}
Let $\{f\uuu n\}$ be a sequence of continuous and non\vvv decreasing functions
defined on some interval $[a,b]$. We also assume that
every $f\uuu n$ is non\vvv negative and that the positive series
\[
\sum\uuu{n=1}^\infty\,f\uuu n(x)
\]
converges for every $x$. The sum yields a 
non-decreasing function $s(x)$.
As we shall explain in more detail later on, it follows that
$s(x)$ is a sum of a non\vvv decreasing function $s\uuu *(x)$
and a discrete jump\vvv function $s\uuu d(x)$.
Moreover, $s(x)$ has a derivative almost everywhere
and the same is true for each $f\uuu n$ by Lebesgue's theorem to be proved
in the next section.
Hence there exists a null\vvv set $E\uuu 0$
such that every $f\uuu n$ and $s$ have derivatives at
points outside $E\uuu 0$. In this situation Fubini proved
that the sum of derivatives converges, i.e. when
$x$ is outside $E\uuu 0$ then
\[ 
s'(x)=\lim\uuu{N\to \infty}\, \sum\uuu{n=1}^{n=N}\, 
f'\uuu n(x)
\]

\noindent
The proof of Fubini's theorem occurs at the end of the next section under the headline
Exercises and Examples.
\medskip

\centerline{\bf{0.18 Points of density.}}

\medskip

\noindent
Recall  from
Calculus  that a bounded and open set
$U$ 
is the disjoint union of intervalÊ$\{(a\uuu n, b\uuu n)\}$.
Its Lebesgue   measure is
defined by $\sum\, (b\uuu n\vvv a\uuu n)$ and denoted by $|U|$.
A subset $S$ of the real line is a null\vvv set if for every
$\epsilon>0$ there exists an open set $U$ such that
$S\subset U$ and $|U|<\epsilon$.
One shows easily that
every denumerable union of null\vvv sets again is a null\vvv set.
Next, let $E$ be a compact subset of ${\bf{R}}$. To each $\delta>0$
we get the open set
\[
E\uuu\delta=\{ x\,\colon\, \text{dist}(x,E)<\delta\}
\]


\noindent
These sets decrease with $\delta$ and the Lebesgue measure of $E$ is defined to be
\[
 |E|= \lim\uuu{\delta\to 0}\, |E\uuu\delta|\tag{i}
\]
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{Exercise.}}
Show that a compact set $E$ is a null\vvv set if and only
if (i) above is zero. The hint is that whenever $U$ is an open set
which contains $E$, then there exists $\delta>0$ such that
$E\uuu\delta\subset U$.

\medskip

\noindent
Next, let $E$ be compact and
consider a point $x\in E$. It is called a point of density in $E$ if:
\[
\lim\uuu {h+k\to 0}\,
\frac{\bigl |E\cap [x\vvv k,x+h]\bigr|}{h+k}=0\tag{*}
\]
where $h,k$ both tend to zero under the sole assumption that
$h+k\to 0$.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.19 Theorem.}}
\emph{Almost every  $x\in E$ is a point of density.}
\medskip

\noindent
This result was established by Lebesgue. A simplification of the original proof
was later found by Vitali using a general covering lemma which
we announce below while the proof of Theorem 0.19 is postponed until
¤ 0.23.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.20 Vitali's covering theorem. }}
Let $F$ be an arbitrary bounded subset of ${\bf{R}}$.
Let $\mathcal V$ be a family of open intervals
with the properties: To every $x\in F$ and $\delta>0$ there
exists an intervalÊ$\omega\in\mathcal V$ such that
$x\in\omega$ and $|\omega|<\delta$.
In addition we assume that $\omega\subset (\vvv A,A)$ hold 
for some $A>0$ which is chosen so large that
$F$ is contained in a compact subset of $(\vvv A,A)$.
If $\omega=(a,b)$ is an interval we denote
by $3\cdot \omega$ the enlarged interval
$(a\vvv \ell,b+\ell)$ where $\ell=b\vvv a$ is the length of
$\omega$ so that $3\cdot \omega$ has length
$3\ell$. With this notation one has:
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.21 Theorem.}}
\emph{There exists a sequence of pairwise disjoint intervals $\{\omega\uuu n\}$
in $\mathcal V$
which covers $F$ in Vitali's  sense which means
that for every positive integer $N$ one has
the inclusion}
\[
F\subset \bar\omega\uuu 1\cup\ldots\cup \bar \omega\uuu N\,\bigcup
\uuu{n>N}\, 3\cdot \omega\uuu n\tag{*}
\]


\noindent
\emph{Proof.}
Put
\[ 
\delta\uuu 1=
\sup\uuu{\omega\in \mathcal V}\, |\omega|
\]
Choose $\omega\uuu 1$ with $|\omega\uuu 1|>2\delta\uuu 1/3$.
Let $\mathcal V\uuu 1$ be the subfamily of intervals
$\omega\in\mathcal V$ which have empty intersection with
the closed interval $\bar\omega\uuu 1$.
Put
\[
\delta\uuu 2=
\sup\uuu{\omega\in \mathcal V\uuu 1}\, |\omega|
\]
Choose $\omega\uuu 2\in\mathcal V\uuu 1$ with
$|\omega\uuu 2|>2\delta\uuu 2/3$.
In the next step $\mathcal V\uuu 2$ is the subfamily of
intervals in $\mathcal V$ which have empty intersection with
$\bar\omega\uuu 1\cup\bar\omega\uuu 2$ and
\[
\delta\uuu 3=
\sup\uuu{\omega\in \mathcal V\uuu 2}\, |\omega|
\]
Choose $\omega\uuu 3\in\mathcal V\uuu 2$ with
$|\omega\uuu 3|>2\delta\uuu 3/3$.
We continue in this way and get a sequence $\{\omega\uuu n\}$
where the intervals by construction are pairwise disjoint.
At this stage we leave it to the reader to verify that
the sequence $\{\omega\uuu n\}$
satisfies (*) in Theorem 0.21 for every positive integer $N$.
\bigskip

\noindent {\bf{0.22 Exercise}}
Since $\{\omega\uuu \nu\}$ is a sequence of disjoint intervals which all are 
contained in a bounded
set, it follows that
\[ 
\sum\,\delta\uuu n<\infty
\]
Use
this convergence and the inclusions in (*) for every positive integer $N$
to show that the outer measure of $F$ is majorized by:
\[
|F|^*\leq \sum\,\delta\uuu n
\]

\bigskip



\noindent
\emph{0.23 Proof of Theorem 0.19.}
For each  integer $n\geq 2 $ we denote by 
$E(n)$ the subset of $E$ which consists of points
such that
\[
\liminf\uuu{h+k\to 0} \,\, \frac{ \bigl|E\cap (x\vvv k,x+h)\bigr  |}{h+k} \leq  1\vvv 
\frac{1}{n}\tag{i}
\]
Let $\mathcal L(E)$ be the set of points of density in $E$.
It is clear that 
\[
 E\setminus \mathcal L(E)=\bigcup\uuu{n\geq 2}\,
E(n)
\] 
We 
conclude that $E\setminus \mathcal L(E)$ is a null\vvv set if
$E(n)$ are null\vvv sets for every $n\geq 2$.
To prove this for a given $n$ we proceed as follows:
For  each  $\epsilon>0$ we can choose
$\delta>0$ such that
\[
|E\uuu\delta\setminus E|<\frac{\epsilon}{n}\tag{ii}
\]
If $x\in E(N)$ the definition of limes inferior means that we can choose 
arbitrary small open intervals $\omega$ which contain $x$
while $\omega\subset E\uuu\delta$ and at the same time
\[
\frac{ \bigl|E\cap \omega \bigr|}{|\omega|} \leq  1\vvv\frac{1}{n}\tag{iii}
\]
It is clear that the family of such intervals is a Vitali covering of
$E(n)$.
Hence we find a sequence $\{\omega\uuu \nu\}$
from Theorem XX and by the remark in XX the outer measure
\[ 
|E(n)|^*\leq \sum\, |\omega\uuu\nu|
\]
Next, for each fixed $\nu$ we have
\[
|\omega\uuu\nu|=
|\omega\uuu\nu\cap E|+
|\omega\uuu\nu\cap (E\uuu\delta\setminus E)|
\]
Since $\{\omega\uuu\nu\}$ are disjoint and
(iii) above hold for every $\nu$ it follows that
\[
\sum\, |\omega\uuu\nu|\leq (1\vvv \frac{1}{n})
\cdot \sum\, |\omega\uuu\nu|+|E\uuu\delta\setminus E|
\]
Hence (ii) gives
\[
\sum\, |\omega\uuu\nu|\leq \epsilon
\]
Since $\epsilon$ was arbitrary we conclude that $E(n)$ is a null\vvv set
and Lebegue's theorem is proved.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{0.24 Exercise.}} Use Lebegue's theorem to show that if
$F$ is a set such that there exists a constant $c<1$ for which
\[ 
\bigl| F\cap (a,b)\,\bigr|^*\leq c(b\vvv a)
\] 
hold for every interval $(a,b)$, then $F$ is a null\vvv set.













\newpage


\centerline{\bf\large {1.B. Derivatives of functions}}

\bigskip 
 

 \noindent
In the text\vvv book \emph{ThŽorie de l'intŽgration} from 1904, Lebesgue proved that
a monotone function defined in a real interval has an ordinary
derivative outside a null\vvv set.
For an arbitrary continuous function
a  more general result  was discovered by M. Young
and Denjoy which goes as follows:
Let $f(x)$ be a real\vvv valued continuous function defined on some interval $(a,b)$.
For each $a<x<b$ we set

\[ 
D^*(x)=\limsup\uuu{h+k\to 0}\, \frac{f(x+h)\vvv f(x\vvv k)}{h+k}
\]
where $h$ and $k$ are positive when we pass to the limes
superior. Similarly
\[
D\uuu *(x)=\liminf\uuu{h+k\to 0}\, \frac{f(x+h)\vvv f(x\vvv k)}{h+k}
\]

\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{1.1 Theorem.}}
\emph{Outside a (possibly empty) null\vvv set $E$ of $(a,b)$
the following two possibilities occur for each
$x\in (a,b)\setminus E$: Either there exists a common finite limit}
\[
D^*(x)= D\uuu *(x)\tag {*}
\]
Or else one has
\[
D^*(x)=+\infty\quad\text{and}\quad D\uuu *(x)=\vvv \infty
\tag{**}
\]
\medskip

\noindent 
{\bf{Remark.}}
Above the pair $h,k)$
tends to zero under the sole condition that $h+k\to 0$. We can  take
$k=0$ or $h=0$ and consider one\vvv sided limits:

\[
D^+(x)=\limsup\uuu{h\to 0}\, \frac{f(x+h)\vvv f(x)}{h}
\quad\text{and}\quad
d^+(x)=\liminf\uuu{h\to 0}\, \frac{f(x+h)\vvv f(x)}{h}\tag{i}
\]
\[
D\uuu +(x)= \limsup \uuu{k\to 0}\, \frac{f(x)\vvv f(x\vvv k)}{k}
\quad\text{and}\quad
d\uuu + (x)=\liminf\uuu{k\to 0}\, \frac{f(x)\vvv f(x\vvv k)}{k}\tag{ii}
\]
With these notations it is clear that:
\[
D\uuu *(x)\leq d^+(x)\leq D^+(x)\leq D^ *(x)
\]
So the equality $D\uuu *(x)= D^*(x)$ entails that $f$
has an ordinary right derivative. Since 
\[
D\uuu *(x)\leq d\uuu +(x)\leq D\uuu +(x)\leq D^ *(x)
\]
we conclude that if (*) holds in the theorem, then
$f$
has a ordinary derivative at $x$.
If (**) occurs at a point
$x$, then
the graph of $f$ close to $x$ is  steep but may also change sign 
in a small interval around such a point. Take for example $x=0$
and let
\[
f(x)=\sqrt{x}\quad\text{when}\quad x>0\quad\colon\quad
f(x)=\sqrt{\vvv x}\quad \text{when}\quad x<0
\]
With $k=0$ and $h>0$ we see that
$D^*(0)=+\infty$ and with $h=0$ and $k>0$ we see that
$D\uuu *(=)=\vvv \infty$. 
Next,
recall Weierstrass'
construction of
a continuous function
$f(x)$ which fails to have an ordinary
derivative at every point in the interval $(a,b)$. The Denjoy\vvv Young
theorem shows that
such a continuous function has a "turbulent" graph where
$D^*(x)=+\infty$ and $D\uuu *(x)=\vvv \infty$
both hold for all $x$ outside a null\vvv set.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{1.2 The case of monotone functions.}}
If the continuous function $f$ is non\vvv increasing or
non\vvv decreasing, then 
case (**) cannot occur. So   Theorem 1.1 implies that
a monotone continuous function has an ordinary derivative almost everywhere.
Concerning Theorem 1.1 its
proof relies upon 
Lebesgue's result about points of density.
The interested reader may
consult Riesz'  plenary talk at the IMU\vvv congress in ZŸrich (1932) for
a historic account about derivatives of functions on
the real line
and  the subsequent proof follows 
Riesz' presentation in [ibid] closely.
 
\medskip


\noindent
{\bf 1.3 Forward Riesz intervals.} 
Let $g(x)$ be a real-valued and continuous function  
defined on some  open   interval  $(a,b)$,
The forward Riesz set $\mathcal F_g$
consists of all points $a<x<b$ 
for which there exists some $x<y\leq b$ such that
\[
g(x)<g(y)\tag{*}
\]
If $g$ is  
non-decreasing then $\mathcal F\uuu g=\emptyset$.
Excluding this case, continuity entails that
$\mathcal F_g$
is an open subset of $(a,b)$ and hence a disjoint union of intervals
\[ 
\mathcal F_g=\cup\,(\alpha_\nu,\beta_\nu)\tag{1}
\]
Each  interval in (1)
is called a \emph{forward Riesz interval} of $g$. It may occur that
some interval is of the form $(\alpha,b)$ i.e. $b$ is a right end-point. Similarly
$a$ can be a left end-point. For example, if $g$ from the start is
strictly increasing then
$\mathcal F_g=(a,b)$.



\medskip

\noindent {\bf 1.4 Proposition} \emph{For each forward Riesz interval $(\alpha,\beta)$
one has }
\[
g(\beta)=\max_{\alpha\leq x\leq\beta}\, g(x)\tag{*}
\]
\medskip

\noindent 
\emph{Proof.}
Assume the contrary. This gives some
maximum point $\alpha\leq x^*<\beta$ for the $g$-function on the closed interval 
$[\alpha,\beta]$.
Now $x^*\in\mathcal F_g$ which means that
\[ 
\exists\, y>x^*\quad\text{and}\quad 
g(x^*)>g(y)
\]
Since $x^*$ is a maximum point over $[\alpha,\beta]$ we must have $y>\beta$. But
then $\beta\in\mathcal F_g$ which is impossible since
$\beta$ was a boundary point of the open set $\mathcal F_g$.
\bigskip

\noindent
{\bf  1.5 Backward Riesz intervals}
Put
\[
\mathcal B_+g=\{ a<x<b\colon\,\,\exists\, a<y<x\,\quad\colon\quad \, g(y)>g(x)\}
\]
Again $\mathcal B_g$ is open and hence a disjoint union of open intervals
$(c_\nu,d_\nu)$. They are  called backward Riesz intervals. By  similar
reasoning as above one shows that if $(c,d)$ is a backward Riesz interval then
\[ 
g(c)=\max_{c\leq x\leq d}\, g(x)\tag{**}
\] 





\bigskip



\centerline {\bf 1.6 A study of monotone functions.}
\bigskip

\noindent
Let $f(x)$ be a continuous and
\emph{non-decreasing} function on $[a,b]$.
To each $a<x<b$ we set 
\[ 
D^+(x)=\limsup_{h\to 0}\,
\frac{f(x+h)-f(x)}{h}\tag{1}
\] 
where  limes superior is taken as
$h>0$  decrease to zero.
The function
$x\mapsto D^+(x)$ takes values
in $[0,+\infty]$.
\medskip

\noindent 
{\bf{ 1.7 Proposition.}} \emph{For each positive number
$C$
the following set-theoretic inclusion holds:}

\[
\{ D^+(x)>C\}\subset \mathcal F_g\quad\text{where}
\quad g(x)= f(x)-Cx
\]
\medskip

\noindent 
\emph{Proof.}
Suppose that $D^+(x)>C$ for some $a<x<b$. The definition of
limes superior gives some $y>x$ such that 
\[ 
\frac{f(y)-f(x)}{y-x}>C\tag{i}
\]
Then $g(y)-g(x)= f(y)-f(x)-C(y-x)>0$ and 
hence $x\in\mathcal F_g$.


\medskip

\noindent 
{\bf 1.8 Proposition} \emph{For every $C>0$
the outer 
Lebesgue measure
of the set $\{D^+>C\}$  satisfies the inequality}
\[
\bigl|\{D^+>C\}\bigr|^*
 \leq\,\frac{f(b)-f(a)}{C}
 \]
\medskip

\noindent 
\emph{Proof.}
With $g(x)=f(x)-Cx$ as above  one has an interval decomposition
$\mathcal F_g=\cup\,(\alpha_\nu,\beta_\nu)$ and
the inclusion from Proposition 1.4 gives
\[
|\{D^+>C\}|^*\leq \sum\,(\beta_\nu-\alpha_\nu)\tag{1}
\]

\medskip

\noindent
Apply Proposition 1.4  to the forward Riesz intervals of $g$. This gives
for every $\nu$:
\[
f(\alpha_\nu)-C\cdot \alpha_\nu\leq f(\beta_\nu)-C\cdot \beta_\nu
\]
Rewriting the last inequality we get
\[
C(\beta_\nu-\alpha_\nu)\leq f(\beta_\nu)-f(\alpha_\nu)
\]
Taking the sum over all $\nu$ we obtain
\[ 
C\cdot \sum\,(\beta_\nu-\alpha_\nu)\leq\,\sum\,
f(\beta_\nu)-f(\alpha_\nu)\leq f(b)-f(a)\tag{2}
\]
where the last inequality holds since $f$ is non-decreasing.
Now (1) and (2) give the requested inequality in
Proposition 1.8.

\bigskip

\noindent
{\bf 1.9 The $d_+$-function}. To each $a<x<b$ we put
\[
d_+(x)=\liminf_{k\to 0}\,\,
\frac{f(x)-f(x-k)}{k}
\]


\noindent
Let $c>0 $ and set
\[
h(x)=f(x)-c
\]
A similar reasoning  as in Proposition 1.4 gives the inclusion
\[
\{ d_+ < c\}\subset\mathcal B_h
\]
where the right hand side is the backward Riesz set from  1.5.
\bigskip


\noindent
{\bf{1.10 Some inequalitites.}}
Consider a pair $0<c<C$ and the intersection
\[
E=\{d\uuu +<c\}\,\cap \{D^+>C\}
\]
Now (1.9) gives the inclusion
\[ 
E\subset \{D^+>C\}\,\cap \mathcal B\uuu h\tag{i}
\]
Let  $\{(\alpha\uuu \nu,\beta\uuu\nu)\}$
be the interval decomposition of the open set
$\mathcal B\uuu h$.
For each $\nu$ we  consider the restriction of $g(x)= f(x)\vvv Cx$
to the interval
$(\alpha\uuu \nu,\beta\uuu\nu)$ and Proposition 1.8 gives the inequality
\[
|\{D^+>C\}\,\cap (\alpha\uuu \nu,\beta\uuu\nu)\,|^*\leq
\frac{f(\beta\uuu \nu)\vvv f(\alpha \uuu\nu)}{C}\tag{ii}
\]
Since $(\alpha\uuu\nu,\beta\uuu\nu)$
is a backward Riesz interval of $f(x)\vvv c$ we have
$f(\beta\uuu \nu)\vvv f(\alpha \uuu\nu)\leq c(\beta\uuu\nu\vvv \alpha\uuu\nu)$.
Hence (i) gives:
\[
|\{D^+>C\}\,\cap (\alpha\uuu \nu,\beta\uuu\nu)\,|^*\leq
\frac{c}{C}\cdot (\beta\uuu \nu\vvv \alpha \uuu\nu)\tag{iii}
\]
Since the backward Riesz intervals are disjoint 
a summation over $\nu$ and the inclusion (i) give:
\[
|\{D^ +>C\}\,\cap \{d\uuu +<c\}|^*\leq \frac{c}{C}(b\vvv a)\tag{*}
\]
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{1.11 Proof of Lebesgue's theorem.}}
The  function $f$ restricts to a non\vvv decreasing function on
an arbitrary open subinterval $(a\uuu *,b\uuu *)$ of $(a,b)$
and since both $D^+$ and $d\uuu +$ are constructed by limits close to a point we
get the same inequality as in (*) above,  i.e. one has
the inequality

\[
|\{d\uuu +<c\}\,\cap \{D^+>C\}\,\cap (a\uuu *,b\uuu *)|^*
\leq \frac{c}{C}\cdot (b\uuu *\vvv a\uuu *)
\]


\noindent
Now the criterion from ¤XX implies that
$\{d\uuu +<c\}\,\cap \{D^+>C\}$ is a null\vvv set.
Apply  this for  pairs $c=q<r=C$ where $q,r$ are rational numbers. Since
a denumerable union  of null\vvv sets  is a null\vvv set
we conclude that  the equality
\[ 
d\uuu +(x)= D^+(x)\tag{i}
\]
holds almost everywhere.
In the same way one proves that the equality
\[
d^+(x)= D\uuu +(x)\quad\text{holds almost everywhere}\tag{i}
\]

\medskip

\noindent
Finally, it is obvious that when (i\vvv ii) hold then
$f$ has an ordinary
derivative which proves Lebegue's theorem that every
monotone function
has a derivative almost everywhere.
\bigskip

\noindent
{\bf{1.12 An extension of Lebesgue's theorem.}} 
Let $f$ be a continuous function on the closed unit interval $[0,1]$.
Suppose that $E$ is a measurable subset of $(0,1)$
such that the restriction of $f$ to $E$ is non\vvv decreasing.
Removing an eventual zero set we also assume that
$E=\mathcal L(E)$, i.e. every $x\in E$ is a point of density for
$E$ as explained in ¤ XX.
Using exactly the same methods as above it follows that 
there is a (possibly empty) null\vvv set
$S\subset E$, there exists a derivative at every
$x\in E$ in the sense that
\[
\lim\uuu{h+k\to 0}\, \frac{f(x+h)\vvv f(x\vvv k)}{h+k}
= f'\uuu E(x)\tag{1}
\] 
exists for each $x\in E\setminus S$ where
the limit is restricted in the sense that $x+h$ and $x+k$ belong to $E$
during the passage to $h+k\to 0$.
But since $x$ is a point of density 
(1) holds without this restriction, i.e. $f'\uuu E(x)$ gives an ordinary
derivative of $f$.
Let us supply the details for this assertion. We may take $x=0$
and replacing $f$ by $f\vvv f'\uuu E(0)x\vvv f(0)$ we can assume that
$f'\uuu E(0)=f(0)=0$.
Next, let $0<\epsilon<1/4$ which gives some $\delta>0$ such that
if $0<x<\delta$ and $x\in E$ then
\[ 
f(x)\leq \epsilon\cdot x
\]
At the same time the density condition entails
that if $\delta$ is small enough then 
\[
|E\cap (\vvv x,x)|\geq 2x(1\vvv\epsilon)
\quad\colon\quad 0<x<\delta
\]
If we  consider some $0<x<\delta/2$ we see that
(xx) implies 
the interval $(x+4\epsilon\cdot x,x)$
must intersect $E$ and if $x^*\in E$ is in this interval we get
\[ 
f(x)\leq f(x^*)\leq \epsilon\cdot x^*\leq
\epsilon\cdot 2x
\]
Since $\epsilon>0$ this proves that $D^+(0)=0$ and in the same way the
reader can verify that the right
derivative at $x=0$ vanishes.


 



\bigskip


\centerline {\bf{1.13 Proof of Theorem 1.1}}
\bigskip

\noindent
For each non\vvv negative integer $n=0,1,2,\ldots$ and every
rational number $r\in (a,b)$ we denote by
$E\uuu{n,r}$ the set 
of all $r<x<b$ such that
\[
\frac{f(x)\vvv f(\xi)}{x\vvv \xi}>\vvv n
\quad\colon\quad r<\xi<x
\]

\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{Exercise.}}
Show the set\vvv theoretic inclusion

\[
\{D\uuu *(x)>\vvv \infty\}\subset
\bigcup E\uuu{n,r}
\]
where the union is taken over allÊ$n\geq 0$ and
every rational number $a<r<b$.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{1.14 Proposition.}} \emph{For each  pair $(n,r)$ the equality}
\[ 
D^*(x)= D\uuu *(x)
\] 
\emph{holds almost everywhere in the measurable set $E\uuu{n,r}$.}
\medskip

\noindent
\emph{Proof.}
Replacing the interval $(a,b)$ by $(r,b)$
and $f$ by $f(x\vvv r)+nx$ we can assume that
$r=n=0$
and now $E\uuu {0,0}\subset (0,b\vvv r)$
where the restriction of $f$ to this measurable set is
monotone, i.e.
\[
0<\xi<x\implies f(x)>f(\xi)
\]
holds for every pair $\xi<x$ in $E\uuu{0,0}$.
To simplify notations we set $E=E\uuu{0,0}$.
Let $E\uuu *$ be the set of density for $E$ as defined in 
XX and recall from XX  that $E\setminus E\uuu *$ is a null\vvv set.
Ignoring this null\vvv set we consider the 
restriction of $f$ to $E\uuu *$ which again is a 
non\vvv decreasing function. The extended version from 1.12 of
Lebegue's theorem applies and 
shows that  after removing  another null\vvv set from
$E\uuu *$ if necessary, then 
the limit below exists for each $x\in E\uuu *$:
\[
D(x)=\lim\uuu{h+k\to 0}\, \frac{f(x+h)\vvv f(x\vvv k)}{h+k}\tag{*}
\]

\noindent
In the same way one proves that if a   null\vvv set
is removed from the set
\[ D^*(x)=+\infty\}
\] 
then $f$ has an ordinary derivative so that  $D^*(x)= D\uuu *(x)$.
This finishes the proof of the Denjoy\vvv Young theorem.


\centerline{\bf{1.15 Examples and Exercises.}}
\bigskip

\noindent
Above we have studied monotone continuous functions.
There also exist non\vvv decreasing jump functions
which arise as follows: Let $\{\xi\uuu n\}$ be a
sequence of real numbers in $(0,1)$.
They are not ordered and may give a dense set. For example, we can take some
enumeration of all rational numbers in $(0,1)$.
Next, let $\{\delta\uuu n\}$ be a sequence of positive numbers such that
$\sum\,\delta\uuu n<\infty$. To each $n$ we get the jump function
$H\uuu n(x)$ where
\[
H\uuu n(x)=0\quad\colon\quad x<\xi\uuu n\quad
\text{and}\quad
H\uuu n(x)=\delta\uuu n\quad\colon\quad x\geq \xi\uuu n
\]
Now
\[ 
s(x)=\sum\, H\uuu n(x)
\]
is a non\vvv decreasing function which has jump\vvv discontinuites at
each $\xi\uuu n$.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{Exercise.}} Show that $s$ is pointwise continuous at every
$x$ outside the set $\{\xi\uuu n\}$, i.e. show that if
$\epsilon>0$ then there exists $\delta>0$ such that
\[
s(x+\delta)<s(x)+\epsilon
\quad\text{and}\quad
s(x\vvv \delta)>s(x)\vvv \epsilon
\]


\noindent 
Less evident is the following:

\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{1.16 Theorem.}}
\emph{$s(x)$ has an ordinary derivative which is equal to zero almost everywhere.}
\medskip

\noindent
\emph{Proof.}
Let $\alpha>0$ and denote by  $E$ be the subset of $(0,1)$ which consists of
numbers $0<x<1$ such that
\[
\limsup\uuu{h+k\to 0}\, \frac{
s(x+h)\vvv s(x\vvv k)}{h+k}> \alpha
\]
It suffices to show that $E$ is a null\vvv set.
To prove this we consider some $\epsilon>0$ and
choose $N$ so large that
\[ 
\sum\uuu{n>N}\, \delta\uuu n<\alpha\cdot \epsilon\tag{i}
\]
Set $s\uuu *(x)= s(x)\vvv (H\uuu 1(x)+\ldots+H\uuu N(x))$.
If $E\uuu *$
is the corresponding set in (x) with $s$ replaced by $s\uuu *$ then
$E$ and $E\uuu *$ only differ by the finite set $\xi\uuu 1,\ldots,\xi\uuu N$
so the measures of $e$ and $E\uuu *$ are the same.
Now we apply 
Vitali's covering theorem using $s\uuu *$ and obtain a
a sequence of disjoint intervals $\{a\uuu n,b\uuu n)\}$
which yields a Vitali covering of $E\uuu *$ and at the same time
\[
\frac{s\uuu *(b\uuu\nu)\vvv s\uuu *(a\uuu\nu)}{b\uuu\nu\vvv a\uuu\nu}
\geq \alpha
\]
It follows that
\[
s\uuu *(1)\vvv s\uuu *(0)\geq
\alpha\cdot \sum\, (b\uuu\nu\vvv a\uuu\nu)\tag{ii}
\]
At the same time (i) entails that $s\uuu *(1)\vvv s\uuu *(0)\leq
\alpha\cdot \epsilon$ and hence we have
\[
|E|^*= |E\uuu *|^*\leq \sum\, (b\uuu\nu\vvv a\uuu\nu)\leq \epsilon
\]
Since $\epsilon$ was arbitrary we get $|E|^*=0$ as requested.



\newpage


\centerline{\bf{1.17 Stieltjes' Moment problem.}}
\bigskip

\noindent
For a full account of Stieltjes'  results  we refer to
his collected work
[Stieltjes]. Here we shall give some examples to illustrate
the flavour of moment
problems. We are given
a real\vvv valued and cointinuous function $f(x)$ defined on
$x\geq 0$ whose absolute value gets so small as
$x\to+\infty$ that the integrals
\[ 
\int_0^\infty\, x^n\cdot |f(x)|\, dx<\infty
\] 
for all positive integers $n$. At first sight one may expect that
if
\[
\int_0^\infty\, x^n\cdot f(x)\, dx=0
\] 
hold for all $n$, then $f$ must be identically zero. However, this is not
true. 
We shall give examples below. But first we
insert the following

\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{Exercise.}}
Let $g(x)$ be an arbitrary continuous function on
$[0,1]$. Then the following two equalities hold:
\[
\int_0^1\, g^2(x)\, dx=
\frac{2}{\pi}
\lim_{R\to \infty}\int_0^R\,\bigl[ \int_0^1\, \cos (st)g(t)\,dt\bigr
]^2\, ds
\]
\[
\int_0^1\, g^2(x)\, dx=
\frac{2}{\pi}\lim_{R\to\infty}\int_0^R \,\bigl[ \int_0^1\, \sin (st)g(t)\,dt\bigr
]^2\, ds
\]
The hint is to apply  Parseval's formula for $L^2$-functions on
the real line as explained in ¤¤- distribution theory.
\medskip

\noindent
Next, consider a test\vvv function $\phi(s)$ supported by $[0,1]$.
Now there exists the cosine integral
\[ 
\Phi(x)=\int_0^1\, \cos(sx)\cdot \phi(s)\, ds
\]
This function satisfies the integrability condition (*). 
For example, if $m\geq 1$ we perform $2m$-many partial
integrations and find that
\[ 
x^{2m}\Phi(x)=(-1)^m\cdot \int_0^1\, \cos(sx)\cdot 
\phi^{(2m)}(s)\, ds
\]
Similarly we have the sine-transform:
\[ 
\Psi(x)=\int_0^1\, \sin(sx)\cdot \phi(s)\, ds
\]
For each positive integer $p$ a partial integration gives
\[ 
\Phi(x)+i\Psi(x)=\frac{(-1)^p}{(is)^p}\int_0^1
e^{ixs}f^{(p)}(s)\, ds
\]
\medskip


\noindent
Using this and the exercise above the reader may verify the equalities
below for every non-negative integer $p$:
\[
\frac{2}{\pi}\int_0^\infty\, x^{2p}\cdot \Phi(x)^2\,dx=
\frac{2}{\pi}\int_0^\infty\, x^{2p}\cdot \Psi(x)^2\,dx
=m_p^2
\] 
where we have put
\[ 
m_p^2=\int_0^1\, [ f^{(p)}(s)\,]^2\, ds
\]
\medskip

\noindent
Now we consider the non-decreasing functions
\[ 
G(x)=\frac{2}{\pi}\int_0^{\sqrt{x}}\, \Phi(s)^2\, ds
\quad\text{and}\quad
H(x)=\frac{2}{\pi}\int_0^{\sqrt{x}}\, \Psi(s)^2\, ds
\]
From the above we obtain

\[
m_p^2=\int_0^\infty\, x^p\cdot G(x)\, dx=
\int_0^\infty\, x^p\cdot H(x)\, dx
\]
Since $G\neq H$ this gives an example where
the moment problem has no unique solution with
$c_p= m_p^2$.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{Remark.}}
The reason why uniqueness fails above is that
the sequence $\{m_p\}$ increases rather rapidly. In
fact, whenever $\phi$ is a test-function which is not identically zero then
\[ 
\sum_{p=1}^\infty
\frac{1}{m_p^{\frac{1}{p}}}=+\infty
\]

\noindent
This divergence is proved by analytic function theory in
¤ XX.
Stieltjes said that a sequence
of non-negative numbers
$\{c_n\}$ yields a \emph{determined moment problem}
if there exists a unique non-decreasing and 
continuous function $f(x)$ which satisfies (*)( and (**) above.
We refer to the notes sbout functional analysis where
the moment problem is
discussed in more detail which includes conditions for the 
moment problem to be determined.




































\newpage


\centerline{\bf II. Abstract measure theory}


\bigskip



\centerline {\bf{Introduction.}} 

\bigskip


\noindent
To get  some intuition we  present the theory
using concepts
from
probability theory.
A sample space
consists of a pair  $(\Omega,\mathcal B)$ where
$\Omega$ is a set and  $\mathcal B$ a
Boolean $\sigma$-algebra of subsets.
Thus, if $\{A_\nu\}$ is a  sequence of sets in $\mathcal B$ 
indexed by non-negative
integers, then  $\cap \,A_\nu$ and $\cup \,A_\nu$  stay in
$\mathcal B$.


\medskip


\noindent 
{\bf 0.1 Probability measures.}
A probability measure $\mu$ is a map
\[
\mu\colon\,\mathcal B\to [0,1]\quad\colon\,\quad
A\to \mu(A)
\]
which is $\sigma$-additive.
This means that
if $\{A_\nu\}$ is a sequence of pairwise
disjoint sets in $\mathcal B$, then
\[ 
\mu(\cup\,A\nu)=\sum\,\mu(A_\nu)
\] 
Finally, every probability measure is normalized, i.e. 
$\mu(\Omega)=1$. 
The family of probablility measures  is denoted by
$P(\Omega,\mathcal B)$.





\medskip

\noindent
{\bf 0.2 Nullsets.}
A subset $F$ of $\Omega$ is  a null set with respect to a
probability measure $\mu$
if there to each $\epsilon>0$ exists $A\in\mathcal B$ such that
\[ F\subset A\quad\text{and}\quad \mu(A)<\epsilon
\]
The class of null-sets  is denoted by
$\mathcal N_\mu$. By
$\sigma$-additivity  
$\mathcal N_\mu$ is stable under  a denumarable 
union, i.e.
\[
\{F_\nu\}\subset\mathcal N_\mu\implies
\cup\,F_\nu\in\mathcal N_\mu
\]


\noindent
Notice that one does not require that a null-set belongs to
$\mathcal B$.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf {0.3 $\mu$-measurable sets.}}
Let $B\subset \Omega$ be an
arbitrary  subset. Its outer - resp. inner
$\mu$-measure are defined by
\[ 
\mu^*(B)=\min_{A\in\mathcal B}\,\mu(A)\quad\colon B\subset A\quad\colon\quad
\mu_*(B)=\max_{A\in\mathcal B}\,\mu(A)\quad\colon 
\,\, A\subset B
\]
If $\mu_*(B)= \mu^*(B)$
one says that
$B$ is $\mu$-measurable and
the common  number is denoted by $\mu(B)$.
Let $\mathfrak{M}_\mu$
be the family if all $\mu$-measurable subsets of $\Omega$.
It is clear that we have the inclusion
\[
\mathcal B\subset\mathfrak{M}_\mu
\]



\noindent
{\bf{0.4 Exercise.}}
Show that a set $B$ is $\mu$-measurable if and only if there exists
a set $B_*\subset B$ such that $B\setminus B_*\in\mathcal N_\mu$ 
and
$B$ belongs to $\mathcal B$. Conclude that
$\mathfrak {M}_\mu$ is a Boolean $\sigma$-algebra 
of subsets of $\Omega$
generated by $\mathcal B$ and $\mathcal N_\mu$. Show also that the map
\[ 
B\mapsto \mu(B)
\]
is
$\sigma$-additive on $ \mathfrak {M}_\mu$.


\bigskip

\noindent {\bf 0.5 Measurable functions.}
A real valued function $f$  defined on $\Omega$ is measurable if
the inverse sets 
\[
f^{-1}(-\infty,a)\,\,\text{and}\,\,
f^{-1}(-\infty,a]\,\,\,\text{both belong to}\,\,\mathcal B\quad\colon\quad 
a\,\,\text{any real number}
\]
\medskip

\noindent
The class of measurable functions on $\Omega$
is denoted by $\mathcal M_\mathcal B$.
Notice that  $\mathcal M_\mathcal B$ is defined without any specific
reference to a probability measure.
\medskip

\noindent 
{\bf 0.6 $\mu$-measurable functions.} Let
$\mu$ be a probability measure.
A real-valued function $f$ on $\Omega$ is 
$\mu$-measurable if
\[
f^{-1}(-\infty,a)\,\,\text{and}\,\,
f^{-1}(-\infty,a]\,\,\,\text{both belong to}\,\,\mathfrak{M}_\mu\quad\colon\quad 
a\in\bf R
\]
Denote this class by $\mathcal M_\mu$.
Since $\mathcal B\subset \mathfrak{M}_\mu$ 
this is a more relaxed condition, i.e.
one has the inclusion
\[
\mathcal M_\mathcal B\subset\mathcal M_\mu
\]
\medskip




\noindent
{\bf{0.7 Teorem.}}
\emph{Let $f\in \mathcal M_\mu$. Then 
there exists a null set
$F\in\mathcal N_\mu$  and some $g\in \mathcal M_\mathcal B$ such that
$f=g$ in $\Omega\setminus F$. Thus, after modifying a $\mu$-measurable 
function on a 
null set with respect to $\mu$ it becomes "truly measurable".}
\medskip


\noindent
\emph{Proof.}
Let 
$\{q_\nu\}_1^\infty$  enumerate  the set $Q$
of
rational numbers.
The exercise after 1.4
shows
that
to each
$\nu$ there exists a  pair  of disjoint sets
$F_\nu\in \mathcal B$ and $G_\nu\in\mathcal N_\mu$
such that
\[
f^{-1}(-\infty,q_\nu)=F_\nu \cup\,G_\nu
\]
We also regard the inverse image of the singleton sets
and find for every
$\nu$ a disjoint pair $H_\nu\in\mathcal B$ and $S_\nu\in\mathcal N_\mu$
\[
f^{-1}(\{q_\nu\})=H_\nu \cup\,S_\nu
\]
Set
\[
G^*=\cup\, G_\nu\bigcup\cup S_\nu
\]
Here $G^*\in\mathcal N_\mu$ which gives
the existence
of a decreasing sequence of sets
$W_\nu\in\mathcal B$ such that
$\mu(W_\nu)<2^{-\nu}$ and $G^*\subset \cap\, W_\nu$.
Now  $W_*=\cap W_\nu$ belons to $\mathcal B$
and it is also a null-set for $\mu$. Finally, 
define the function
$f_*$ by
\[
f_*(\omega)=f(\omega)\quad\colon
\omega\in \Omega\setminus W_*\quad\text{and}\quad
f_*|W_*=0 
\]
By this construction $f_*\in\mathcal M_\mathcal B$ and $f_*$ 
differs from $f$ on a null set.

\bigskip


\noindent 
{\bf {0.8 Equivalence classes of $\mu$-measurable functions.}}
A pair $f,g$ in $\mathcal M_\mu$ are called equivalent if they
are equal outside a null set.
Theorem 1.7
shows that
every equivalence class can be represented by
a 
$\mathcal B$-measurable function.




\medskip


\noindent
{\bf 0.9 Convergene in $\mu$-measure.}
We say that a  sequence $\{f_n\}$ in $\mathcal M_\mu$
converges in $\mu$-measure to a limit
function $g$ in
$\mathcal M_\mu$ if
\[ 
\lim_{n\to\infty}\, \mu(\{|f_n-g|\geq \epsilon\}]=0
\] 
hold for every $\epsilon>0$.
When it holds we write
\[ 
f_n\overset\mu\to g\tag{0.9.1}
\]

\medskip



\noindent 
{\bf 0.10 Convergence almost everywhere.}
A sequence of $\mu$-measurable functions
$\{f_\nu\}$ is said to converge $\mu$-almost everywhere to a limit function
$g$ if there exists a null set $F\in\mathcal N_\mu$ such that
every $f_\nu$ is defined in $\Omega\setminus F_\nu$
and one has a pointwise limit:
\[ 
\lim_{\nu\to\infty}\, f_\nu(\omega)=g(\omega)\,\colon\,
\omega\in\Omega\setminus F
\]
\emph{When it holds we write}
\[ 
f_n\overset{a.e}\to g
\]
\medskip


\noindent
{\bf 0.11 Remark.} Almost everywhere convergence means that   pointwise 
convergence holds outside a null set $F$ for $\mu$.
So above the limit function $g$ is only defined in
$\Omega\setminus F$.
But when we regard $\mu$-measurable functions one can ignore  null sets
for $\mu$ and the following hold:

\medskip

\noindent
{\bf {0.12  Proposition.}}
\emph{If $g$ is an almost everywhere limit of a sequence
$\{f_\nu\}$ in $\mathcal M_\nu$ then
$g\in \mathcal M_\mu$.}


\medskip

\noindent
\emph{Proof.}
For a real number $a$ and a positive integer $N$ we put
\[
A_N(a)=
\{w\in \Omega\setminus F\colon \max_{\nu\geq N}\, f_\nu(w)\leq a\}
\]
If $\{g\leq a\}= \{ w\in \Omega\setminus F\,\colon\, g(w)\leq a\}$
it is clear that
\[
A_N(\epsilon)\subset \{g\leq a\}
\]
hold for every $N$.
Conversely, if
$\delta$ is a positive number the pointwise convergence entails that
\[
 \{g\leq a\}\subset
 \bigcup_{n\geq 1}\, A_N(a+\delta)
 \]
 Notice that the sets $A_N(a)$
 increase with $N$
 and let $A^*(a)$ be the union.
 Then (i-ii) entail that the following inclusions hold for
 every positive integer $m$:
 \[
 A^*(a)\subset \{g\leq a\}\subset A^*_N(a+1/m)
\]
This implies that
\[
 \{g\leq a\}=\bigcap_{m\geq 1}\, A^*_N(a+1/m)
\]
and the reader may verify that
the right hand side
is a $\mu$-measurable set.
Hence 
$\{g\leq a\}\in\mathcal {M}_\mu$. In a similar fashion one shows that
$\{g\geq b\}\in\mathcal {M}_\mu$
hold for every real number and
the reader can conclude that $g$ is $\mu$-measurable.




\medskip


\noindent
{\bf{0.13  A warning }}
Convergence  in measure need not imply convergence almost everywhere.
Consider the case when
$\Omega=[0,1]$ and $\mu$ is the Lebesgue measure.
Given a sequence of real numbers
$\{0<a_\nu<1-1/\nu)\}_2^\infty$ we
define a sequence of functions
$\{f_\nu\}$ where
\[
f_\nu(x)=1\quad a_\nu<x<a_\nu+1/\nu
\] 
while $f_\nu(x)=0$ holds outside this interval. It is clear that
$\{f_\nu\}$ converges to the zero function in measure.
On the other hand the reader should find
a sequence $\{a_\nu\}$ as above such that for
every $0<x<1$ there exists an
\emph{infinite} family of $\nu$:s 
such that
$a_\nu<x<a_\nu+1/\nu$
and as a consequence we never have a pointwise convergence
except at the two end-points $x=0$ or $x=1$.
On the other hand we have a positive result if 
we pass to suitable subsequences.
\bigskip


\noindent
{\bf 0.14 Proposition.} \emph{Let $\{f_n\}$ be a sequence
of measurable functions which converges in $\mu$-measure to a $\mu$-measurable 
function
$g$. Then there exists a subsequence}
\[
\phi_k=f_{\nu_k}\quad\,\colon\quad \nu_1<\nu_2<\ldots
\]
\emph{such that  $\phi_k\overset{a.e}\to  g$}.
\medskip


\noindent
\emph{Proof.}
Regarding the sequence $\{f_n-g\}$ it suffices to prove the result when
$g=0$.
We first find an integer $\nu_1$ such that
\[
\nu\geq \nu_1	\implies \mu(\{|f_\nu\geq 2^{-1}\}\leq 2^{-1}
\]
Next, we find
$\nu_2>\nu_1$ such that
\[
\nu\geq \nu_2	\implies \mu(\{|f_\nu\geq 2^{-2}\}\leq 2^{-2}
\]
Inductively we get a sequence $\nu_1<\nu_2\ldots$ where
\[
\nu\geq \nu_k	\implies \mu(\{|f_\nu\geq 2^{-k}\}\leq 2^{-k}
\]
Then, the sequence  $\{g_k=f_{\nu_k}\}$ 
converges almost everywhere to the zero function.
To see this we set:
\[
g^*_k=\text{max}_{\nu\geq k}\, |g_\nu|
\]


\noindent
The inductive construction above gives
\[
 \mu(\{g^*_k\geq 2^{-k}\}\leq
\sum_{\nu\geq k}\, 
 \mu(\{|g_\nu|\geq 2^{-k}\}\leq \sum_{\nu\geq k}\, 2^{-\nu}= 2^{-k+1}\tag{i}
 \]
Here $g^*_1\geq g^*_2\geq\ldots$ is a monotone sequence and
(i) shows that 
\[ 
g^*_k\overset{a.e}\to 0
\]
From this it is clear that
$\{g_k\}$ also converges almost everywhere to zero.






\bigskip

\noindent 
{\bf 0.15 Exercise}
Let $\{f_n\}$ be a sequence in $\mathcal M_\mu$
such that
\[
\lim_{n,m}\, \mu(\{|f_n-f_m|\geq|\epsilon\})=0
\]  
hold for each $\epsilon>0$, where $(m,n)$ ted to $\infty$, i.e.
to every $\epsilon>0$ there exists
some integer $N$ such that for every pair $n\geq N$ and $m\geq N$ one has

\[
\mu(\{|f_n-f_m|\geq|\epsilon\})<\epsilon
\] 
\medskip


\noindent
When it holds we say that $\{f_n\}$ is a Cauchy-sequence with 
respect to the $\mu$-measure
Prove by a similar method as in Proposition 0.14 that there
exists a subsequence $\{g_k=f_{n_K}\}$
which converges almost everywhere to a limit function
$f_*$. Show also that $f_*$ is unique in $\mathcal M_\mu$
since the almost everywhere convergence of the subsequence entails that we have

\[ 
f_n\overset\mu\to f_*
\]


\bigskip

\centerline{\bf 1. Integrals}


\medskip


\noindent {\bf{1.1 Elementary functions.}}
If $B\in\mathcal B$ we get the characteristic function
$\chi_B$ which is 1 on $B$ and 0 in $\Omega\setminus B$.
An elementary function is a finite linear sum 
\[ 
F=\sum\, a_k\cdot \chi_{B_k}\tag{*}
\] 
where $B_1,\ldots,B_N$ is a finite set of disjoint
sets in $\mathcal B$ whose union is $\Omega$ and
and $a_1,\ldots,a_N$ are real numbers. 
For example, with $B\in\mathcal B$ then $\chi_B$ is an elementary
function
where the $a$-number of $B$ is one and the $a$-number of
$\Omega\setminus B$ is zero.
Notice that $F$ is unchanged if we take a refined partition, i.e. if every
$B_k$ is a disjoint union of some $\mathcal B$-sets
$\{C_{k\nu}\}$ and set

\[
F=\sum\sum\, c_{k\nu}\cdot \chi_{C_{k\nu}}\quad \colon
c_{k\nu}=a_k
\]
Let $E(\mathcal B)$ denote the family of all elementary functions.
which becomes
a linear space of functions on $\Omega$. If
$F$ and $G$ belong to $E(\mathcal B)$ we can represent both
as in (*) after a common refinement and
the product $G\cdot F$ is the elementary
function defined by
\[
G\cdot F=\sum\, b_k\cdot a_k\cdot \chi_{B_K}
\] 
where $F=a_k$ and $G=b_k$ hold on $B_k$ for every $k$.
Next, let $F$ be a bounded 
$\mathcal B$-measurable function i.e. 
there exists a constant $K$ such that
$-K\leq F(\omega)\leq K$ hold for all $\omega\in\Omega$.
Then $F$ can be \emph{uniformly approximated} by
elementary functions.
Namely, if $N\geq 1$ we set
\[ 
B_N(\nu)=\{ -K+K\cdot \nu\cdot 2^{-N}\leq F<
-K+K\cdot (\nu+1)\cdot 2^{-N}\} \quad\colon\,0\leq \nu\leq 2^{2N}-2
\]
\[
\text{and}\quad  B_N(2^{2N}-1)=\{ K-2^{-N}\leq F\leq K\}
\]
We get the elementary function
\[ 
F_*=\sum_{\nu=0} ^{2^{2N}-1}
\,(-K+\nu\cdot 2^{-2N})\cdot \chi_{B_N(\nu)}
\]
It is clear that 
\[
F_*\leq F\leq F_*+2^{-N}
\]
Since $N$ can be arbitrary large this shows that
$F$ is uniformly
approximated by elementary functions.















\bigskip

\noindent
{\bf{1.2 Integrals of elementary functions.}}
Let $\mu$ be a probability measure.
To each
elementary function
\[
F=\sum\, a_k\cdot \chi_{B_k}
\] 
we assign the $\mu$-integral
\[
 \int\, F\cdot d\mu=\sum\, a_k\cdot \mu(B_k)\tag{1.2.1}
\]
It is clear that (1.2.1 ) yields an additive map from
$E(\mathcal B)$ into the complex number where we regard
complex-valued functions.
The maximum-norm $||F||_\infty$
is the maximum of $\{|a_k|\}$. Since
$\mu$ is a probability measure we get:
\[
\bigl| \int\, F\cdot d\mu\,\bigr|\leq
||F||_\infty\tag{1.2.2}
\]
\medskip


\noindent
{\bf{1.3  Integrals of bounded functions.}}
If $K>0$ is a positive number we denote by
$\mathcal M_\mu(K)$ the class of $\mu$-measurable functions
$f$ such that the absolute value $|f|$ is $\leq K$
almost everywhere. So in the equivalence class we can take $f$ to be
a function in $\mathcal M_\mathcal B$ where
this everywhere defined function has maximum norm 
$K$ at most. By (1.1) 
$f$ can be uniformly approximated
by a sequence $\{F_n\}$ from $E(\mathcal B)$
with maximum norms $\leq K$.
For each pair $n,m$ the triangle inequality gives
\[
\bigl| \int\, F_n\cdot d\mu-
\int\, F_m\cdot d\mu\,\bigr|\leq
\int\,|F_n-F_m|\cdot d\mu\leq
||F_n-F_m||_\infty\tag{1.3.1}
\]
Since $||F_n-f||_\infty\to 0$ it follows that
(1) tends to zero as $n$ and $m$ increase. Hence 
the evaluated integrals $\{\int\, F_n\cdot d\mu\}$ is a 
Cauchy sequence of complex numbers and 
there exists a limit
\[ 
\lim_{n\to\infty}\, \int\, F_n\cdot d\mu\tag{1.3.2}
\]
Moreover, (1.2.1 ) implies  that this limit is intrinsic, i.e. 
independent
of the chosen  sequence $\{F_n\}$ which approximates $f$ uniformly.
The limit (2) is called the $\mu$-integral of $f$ and is denoted by
\[
\int\, f\cdot d\mu\tag{1.3.3}
\]

\medskip

\noindent
{\bf 1.4 Absolutely integrable functions.}
For each  positive real number $K$ we define the 
the  truncation operator $T_K$ from $\mathcal M_\mu$ to 
$\mathcal M_\mu(K)$ by:
\[ 
T_K(f)(x)=f(x)\,\colon\,|f(x)|\leq K\quad\text{and}\quad
 T_K(f)(x)=0\,\colon\,|f(x)|> K
\]

 
\noindent
When $f\in\mathcal M_\mu$ 
we
get bounded functions
$\{T_K(f)\,\colon\, K>0\}$.
We can also take  absolute values and notice that
\[
 K_1<K_2\implies |T_{K_1}(f)|\leq |T_{K_2}(f)|\tag{1}
\]
From (1)  the $\mu$-integrals of the
non-negative functions $\{|T_K(f)|\}$ increase with $K$, i.e. 
\[
 K_1<K_2\implies \int\, |T_{K_1}(f)|\cdot d\mu\leq
\int\, |T_{K_2}(f)|\cdot d\mu\tag{2}
\]


\noindent
We can impose the condition that the non-decreasing sequence 
(2) is bounded. This leads to 
\medskip

\noindent
\noindent 
{\bf 1.5  Definition}.
\emph{A function $f\in\mathcal M_\mu$ is said to be absolutely integrable
if there exists a
constant $C$ such that}
\[
\int\, |T_{K_1}(f)|\cdot d\mu\leq C\quad\text{for all}\quad K>0\tag{*}
\]
\emph{
The class if these functions is denoted
$L^1(\mu)$.}
\bigskip

 

 

\noindent 
{\bf {1.6 Integrals of $L^1(\mu)$-functions.}}
Let $f$ belong to $L^1(\mu)$.
From the construction of the $T$-operators it is clear that
if $K_2>K_1>0$ then
\[
\bigl|\int T_{K_2}(f)\cdot d\mu-
\int T_{K_1}(f)\cdot d\mu\bigr|\leq
\int\, |T_{K_2}(f)|\cdot d\mu-
\int\, |T_{K_1}(f)|\cdot d\mu\tag{i}
\]


\noindent
Since $f\in L^1(\mu)$
the non-decreasing sequence
$\{\int\, |T_{K_2}(f)|\cdot d\mu\}$
is bounded above and therefore convergent. Since a 
convergent sequence also is a Cauchy sequence, it follows from (i) that
\[
\lim_{K_2,K_1}\, 
\int T_{K_2}(f)\cdot d\mu-
\int T_{K_1}(f)\cdot d\mu=0
\] 
as $K_1$ and $K_2$ tend to $+\infty$.
Since  Cauchy sequences of complex numbers converge
there exists a limit
\[ 
\lim_{K\to\infty}\, 
\int T_K(f)\cdot d\mu\tag{*}
\]
This limit is denoted by $\int\, f\cdot d\mu$ and is called the
$\mu$-integral of $f$.


\medskip

\noindent 
{\bf 1.7 Exercise.} Show that the integral on $L^1(d\mu)$ is additive, i.e.
\[
\int f d\mu+
\int g d\mu=
\int (f+g) d\mu\,\quad\colon\quad f,g\in L^1(d\mu)
\]
Hence the $\mu$-integral yields a linear functional on the 
space of absolutely integrable functions.
Show also that if $f\in L^1(\mu)$ then the absolute value $|f|$ 
belongs to $L^1(\mu)$.


\medskip

\noindent
{\bf 1.8 The $L^1$-norm.}
The linear space $L^1(d\mu)$ becomes a normed space when we set
\[ 
||f||_1=\int\,|f[ d\mu\quad\colon\quad
f\in L^1(d\mu
\]



\noindent
{\bf{1.9 Exercise.}}
Show that $E(\mathcal B)$ is a dense subspace of $L^1(\mu)$.
\bigskip


\noindent
{\bf{1.10 Convergence in the $L^1$-norm.}}.
By definition
a sequence $\{f_n\}$ converges in the $L^1$-norm to a limit function
$g$ in $L^1(\mu)$ when
\[
\lim_{n\to\infty}\, ||f_n-g||_1=0\tag {i}
\]
If $\epsilon>0$ we notice that
\[
\mu(\{|f_n-g|\geq\epsilon\}\leq\epsilon^{-1}\cdot ||f_n-g||_1\tag{ii}
\]
Hence (i) implies that
\[
\lim_{n\to\infty}\
\mu(\{|f_n-g|\geq\epsilon\}=0\tag{3}
\]
Here $\epsilon$ is arbitrary small
which  means that $f_n\overset\mu\to g$. Thus
$L^1$-convergence implies convergence in $\mu$-measure.
\medskip


\noindent 
{\bf{1.11  Example.}}
The converse is not true.
For example, let $\mu$ be the Lebesgue measure on $[0,1]$. To each
$n\geq 1$ we define $f_n(x)$ to be $n$ if $0\leq x\leq \frac{1}{n}$ 
and otherwise zero. Then 
\[ 
\int_=^1\, f_n(x)\cdot dx=1
\]
hold for all $n$. At the same time $f_n(x)\to 0$ for every $x>0$, i.e. the sequence converges almost every where to zero and hence also in measure.
But if we restrict the attention to bounded functions  a converse result holds.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf 1.12  Proposition} 
\emph{Let $K>0$ be fixed. Then a sequence $\{f_\nu\}$
in $\mathcal M_\mu(K)$ converges to a limit function $g$ in the $L^1$-norm
if and only if the sequence  converges in measure to $g$.}

\bigskip


\noindent
\emph{Proof.} Consider a pair $f,g$ in $\mathcal M_\mu(K)$.
If $\epsilon>0$ we get the measurable set
\[ 
E_\epsilon(f,g)=\{ |f-g|\geq\epsilon\}
\]
Since the maximum norm   $||f-g||_\infty\leq 2K$ 
it follows that

\[ 
||f-g||_1=
\int_{E_\epsilon(f,g)}\,|f-g|d\mu+
\int_{\Omega\setminus E_\epsilon(f,g)}\,|f-g|d\mu\leq 2K\cdot \mu(E_\epsilon(f,g))\tag{i}
+\epsilon
\]
\medskip

\noindent
If  $f_\nu\overset\mu\to g$
where $\{f_\nu\}$ in $\mathcal M_\mu(K)$ one has 
\[
\lim_{\nu\to\infty}\, \mu(E_\epsilon(f_\nu,g))=0\quad\text{hold for each}\quad
\epsilon>0\tag{ii}
\] 
Hence (i) implies that
$||f_n-g||_1||_1\to 0$.
Since we already proved that $L^1$-convergence entails convergence in measure we get
the equivalence assertion in
Proposition 1.12 follows.



\medskip

\centerline {\bf 1.13 Dominated convergence theorem}
\medskip

\noindent 
Let 
$\{f_n\}$ be a  sequence in $L^1(\mu)$
which converges in $\mu$-measure
to a limit function
$g$. The example in 1.11  shows that
convergence need not hold
in the $L^1$-norm.
 To compensate for this we impose a certain bound so that the situation
is essentially the same as in 
Proposition 1.12
\medskip

\noindent 
{\bf 1.14 Theorem}
\emph{Let $\{f_n\}$ be a sequence in $L^1(\mu)$ where
$f_n\overset\mu\to g$ holds. Assume in addition that there exists
a non-negative $\phi\in L^1(\mu)$ such that}
\[
|f_n|\leq \phi\tag{1}
\] 
\emph{hold almost everywhere for each $n$.
Then the limit function $g$ belongs to $L^1(\mu)$ and}
\[ 
\lim_{\nu\to\infty}\,||f_n-g||_1=0\tag{2}
\] 
\bigskip

\noindent {\bf{1.15  Exercise}} Prove Theorem 1.14.
The hint is to apply  truncation operators to $\phi$
so that the $L^1$-norm of $\phi-T_K(\phi)$ are small and then
use Theorem 1.13.

\bigskip

\noindent
{\bf{1.16  Remark.}}
Recall that almost everywhere convergence implies convergence in measure.
So if $\{f_n\}$ is some $L^1(\mu)$-sequence and 
a dominating $\phi$-function as above, then
an almost everywhere convergene $f_n\overset{a.e}\to g$
gives the $L^1$-convergence.

\bigskip

\centerline
{\bf{2.  A completeness theorem.}}
\medskip


\noindent
Let $\{f_n\}$ be  Cauchy sequence in the $L^1$-norm, i.e. 
\[
\lim_{n,m\to\infty}\,\int\,|f_n-f_m|\cdot d\mu=0\tag{*}
\]
\medskip


\noindent
{\bf{2.1 Theorem}}
\emph{When (*) holds there exists   a unique  $L^1$-function $f_*$
such that}
\[
\lim_{n,m\to\infty}\,\int\,|f_n-f_*|\cdot d\mu=0
\]
\emph{This means that the normed space
$L^1(\mu)$ is complete, i.e. it is a Banach space.}
\medskip

\noindent
\emph{Proof.}
From (*) it follows that there exists a subsequence
$\{n_k\}$ such that if $g_k=f_{n_k}$
then
\[
||g_{k+1}-g_k||_1\leq 2^{-k}\tag{i}
\]
Since $\sum\, 2^{-k}<\infty$
the non-negative function
\[ 
G=|g_1|+\sum_{k=1}^\infty\, |g_{k+1}-g_k|
\]
is integrable.
It is clear that 
\[
|g_k|\leq G\quad\colon k=1,2,\ldots\tag{ii}
\]
Together with (i) this entails that
$\{g_k\}$ is a Cauchy sequence in the complete metric space
from ¤ xx and has therefore a limit function $g$, i.e.
$g_k\overset{\mu}\to g$. Now 
(ii) enable us to apply Theorem 1.14 which gives
\[
||g_k-g||_1\to 0
\]
Above $\{g_k\}$ is a subsequence
of $\{f_n\}$ and the triangle inequality
shows that
$||f_n-g||_1\to 0$ which shows that $g$ is the 
requested limit in $L^1(\mu)$ of the given Cauchy-sequence.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{2.2 The space $L^2(\mu)$.}}
On elementary functions we can introduce 
the $L^2$-norm, i.e.
set
\[
||f||_2=\sqrt{\, |f|^2\cdot d\mu}
\]
Passing to the closure
of elementary
functions this yields a normed
inear space denoted by $L^2(\mu)$ which
again is complete and therefore becomes a Hilbert space.
The details are left to the reader.










\newpage






\centerline{\bf 3. Signed measures and the  Radon-Nikodym  theorem.}

\bigskip

\noindent
Let $(\Omega,\mathcal B)$ be a sample space.
Consider an additive real-valued map $\mu\colon\mathcal B\to
\bf R$ which may take negative values. But we impose the condition that 
$\mu$  is bounded, i.e. there exists a constant $C$ such that
\[
 -C\leq\mu(A)\leq C\quad\colon\,\quad A\in \mathcal B\tag{*}
\]
\medskip

\noindent
This uniform bound and additivity  imply
that
if $\{A_\nu\}$ is a sequence of disjoint sets in $\mathcal B$ then
\[
\sum\, |\mu(A_\nu)|< 2C
\]
Hence the series $\sum\,\mu(A_\nu)$ is absolutely convergent.
We say that $\mu$ is $\sigma$-additive if
\[ 
\sum\, \mu(A_\nu)=\mu(\cup\,A_\nu)
\]
hold for every sequence of disjoint sets in $\mathcal B$ and  refer to $\mu$
as a  \emph{signed} measure. From now on all measures are $\sigma$-additive and
bounded, i.e. (*)  holds for some constant $C=C_\mu$.
If $\mu(A)\geq 0$ for all $A\in\mathcal b$ we say that
$\mu$ is a positive measure.
\medskip

\noindent {\bf 3.1 Definition}
\emph{Two positive measures $\mu$ and $\nu$
are mutually singular if there exist nullsets  $A\in\mathcal N_\mu$
and $B\in\mathcal N_\nu$ such that}
\[
\mu(B)=\mu(\Omega)\quad\colon\quad \nu(A)=\nu(\Omega)
\] 
\emph{When this holds we write $\mu\perp\nu$.}




\bigskip

\noindent
{\bf 3.2 Hahn's Theorem}
\emph{Every  signed measure $\mu$ has a unique decomposition}
\[
\mu=\mu_+-\mu_-\quad\text{where}\,\quad
\mu_,\mu_-\,\,\text{
are both  positive and}\,\ \mu_+\perp\mu_-
\]
\medskip

\noindent \emph{Proof.}
$A\in\mathcal B$ is called a $\mu$-positive set  if
\[
E\subset A\quad\text{and}\quad E\in\mathcal B \implies \mu(E)\geq 0\tag{i}
\]

\noindent
Denote this class by $P_+(\mu)$.
Obviously the union of two $\mu$-positive sets is again $\mu$-positive and by
$\sigma$-additivity there exists a $A^*\in P_+(\mu)$ such that
\[
\mu(A^*)=\max_{A\in P_+(\mu)}\,\mu(A)\tag{ii}
\]


\noindent
\emph{Sublemma.} One has  $\mu(B)\leq 0$ for each $B\subset\Omega\setminus A^*$.
\medskip

\noindent
\emph{Proof of Sublemma.} 
We argue by contradiction.
Suppose there is  some $B_0\subset\Omega\setminus A^*$ with 
$\mu(B_0)=\delta>0$.
The maximality of $\mu(A^*)$ implies that $B_0$ does not belong to
$P_+(\mu)$ which gives
some $\delta_1>0$ such that
\[ 
-\delta_1=\min_{E\subset B_0}\,\mu(E)\tag{i}
\] 
Choose $E_1\subset B_0$ with $\mu(E_1)\leq -\delta_1/2$ and set $B_1=B\setminus E_1$.
Now $\mu(B_1)\geq \delta+\delta_1/2$ and 
exactly as above
we get a negative number
\[
-\delta_2=
\min_{E\subset B_1}\,\mu(E)
\]
Choose $E_2\subset B_1$ with $\mu(E_2)\leq-\delta_2/2$.
Inductively we get a decreasing sequence of
sets
\[ 
B_\nu=B_0\setminus (E_1\cup\ldots\cup E_\nu)
\]
where $\{E_\nu\}$ are disjoint. Moreover, we have
a sequence $\{\delta_\nu\}$ of positive numbers where
\[
-\delta_\nu=
\min_{E\subset B_\nu}\,\mu(E)\quad\text{and}\quad \mu(E_{\nu+1})\leq
-\delta_\nu/2\tag{ii}
\]
Since $\mu$ is a signed measure there is  a constant $A$
such that 
\[
\mu(E_1)+\ldots+\mu(E_N)\geq -A\quad\text{for all}\quad N\geq 1\tag{iii}
\]
It follows that
\[ 
\delta_1+\ldots+\delta_N\leq 2A\quad\text{for all}\quad N\geq 1\tag{iv}
\]
Hence the positive series
$\sum\,\delta_\nu$ is convergent which gives
$\delta_\nu\to 0$ as $\nu\to+\infty$.
Put
\[
B_*=\cap \, B_\nu\tag{v}
\]
For each $\nu\geq 1$ the inclusion $B_*\subset B_N$ 
and the definition of $\delta_\nu$ from (ii)  give:
\[ 
\min_{E\subset B_*}\, \mu(E)\geq -\delta_\nu\tag{vi}
\]
This hold for every $\nu$ and since $\delta_\nu\to 0$
the minimum in (vi) is 
$\geq 0$ which means that $B_*$ belongs to 
$P_+(\mu)$.
At the same time the construction above gives

\[ 
\mu(B_\nu\geq \delta_0+\frac{1}{2}(\delta_1+\ldots+\delta_\nu)\geq \delta_0\tag{vii}
\]
for every $\nu$. By $\sigma$-additivity we have

\[ 
\mu(B_*)=\lim_{\nu\to\infty}\,\mu(B_\nu)
\] 
and then
(vii) entails that $\mu(B_*)>0$.
This contradicts the  (1) since $A^*$ and $B_*$ are disjoint
and the Sublemma is proved.









\bigskip
 
{\emph{Final part of the proof }}. 
The Sublemma gives  positive measures
$\mu_+$ and $\mu_-$ defined 
 by
 \[ 
 \mu_+(E)=\mu(E\cap A^*)\quad\colon\quad
 \mu_-(E)=-\mu(E\cap(\Omega\setminus A^*)
\]
We see that
$\mu_+\perp\mu_-$ and $\mu=\mu_+-\mu_-$.
This proves the existence of at least one
Hahn-decompostion.
The proof of  \emph{uniqueness} of such a 
decompositon
is left to the reader. 


\bigskip

\centerline {\bf 3.3 Radon-Nikodym derivatives.} 
\bigskip

\noindent
Let $\mu$ be a positive measure and consider
a
non-negative function $f\in L^1(d\mu)$.
Theorem XX   gives a  positive measure defined
by the $\sigma$-additive map

\[
E\mapsto 
\int_E\, f\cdot d\mu\quad\colon\, E\in\mathcal B
\]
Denote this positive measure by $f\cdot \mu$.
If $E\in\mathcal N_\mu$  the construction of $\mu$-integrals implies that
$\int_E\, f\cdot d\mu=0$. So one has the inclusion
\[ 
\mathcal N_\mu\subset
\mathcal N_{f\cdot \mu}\tag{**}
\]
In general, a positive measure $\nu$ is called \emph{absolutely continuous} with respect
to $\mu$ if one has the inclusion
\[
\mathcal N_\mu\subset
\mathcal N_\nu
\]
It turns out that such positive measures are
of the form $f\cdot\mu$ with $f\in L^1(\mu)$.

\bigskip

\noindent
{\bf 3.4 Theorem.}
\emph{Let $\mu$ be a  positive measure. Then every positive measure
$\nu$ which is absolutely continuous with respect to
$\mu$ is of the form
$f\cdot \mu$ for a unique non-negative function
$f\in L^1(d\mu)$.}
\bigskip


\noindent
\emph{Proof.} 
For each pair $(k,N)$ where  $N$ is a positive integer and $k$
a non-negative integer
we consider the following two signed measures

\[
\nu-k2^{-N}\cdot \mu\quad\text{and}\quad
(k+1)2^{-N}\cdot \mu-\nu\tag{i}
\]
The Hahn decomposition applied to
$\nu-k2^{-N}\cdot \mu$ gives a maximal set
\[
S_N(k)\in P_+( \nu-k2^{-N}\cdot \mu)\tag{ii}
\]
and similarly we find a maximal set
\[
T_N(k)\in P_+((k+1)2^{-N}\cdot \mu-\nu)\tag{iii}
\]


\medskip

\noindent
If $N$ is fixed 
the measure 
$ (\nu-k2^{-N})-(\nu-(k+1)2^{-N})= 2^{-N}\cdot \mu\geq 0$. This implies that

\[ 
S_N(k+1)\subset S_N(k)\quad\text{for all}\quad k=0,1,\ldots\tag{iv}
\]

\noindent
Moreover, since
$\nu$ is absolutely continuous with respect to $\mu$ it is clear that:
\[
\cap_{k\geq 1}\, S_N(k)=\emptyset\tag{v}
\]
Finally the reader may observe that
\[
S_N(k)\setminus S_N(k+1)= S_N(k)\cap\, T_N(k)\tag{vi}
\]


\noindent
Let us put
\[
W_N(k)= S_N(k)\cap\, T_N(k)\tag{vii}
\]
From (vi) it follows that 
$\{W_N(k)\}$ is a family of disjoint subsets of $\Omega$ and we
notice  that

\[
k2^{-N}\mu(E)\leq \nu(E)\leq (k+1)2^{-N}\mu(E)\quad\colon\,
E\subset W_N(k)\tag{viii}
\]
The reader may also verify the set-theoretic equality
\[
 W_N(k)=W_{N+1}(2k)\cup W_{N+1}(2k+1)\tag{ix}
 \]
for all pairs $k$ and $N$,
Next we construct a sequence of functions by:
\[ 
f_N=\sum_{k=1}^\infty\, k2^{-N}\cdot \chi_{W_N(k)}\quad\colon\, N=1,2\ldots \tag{x}
\]
Using (ix) the reader may verify that
$\{f_N\}$ increase with $N$ and (viii) entails that
\[ 
\int_E\, f_N\cdot d\mu\leq \nu(E)\leq
\int_E\, f_N\cdot d\mu+2^{-N}\cdot \mu(E)
\quad\colon\,
E\in\mathcal B
\tag{xi}
\]
\medskip


\noindent
Now $\{f_N\}$ is a non-decreasing sequence and
there exists
a limit function
\[ 
f_*=\text{lim}_{N\to\infty} f_N
\] 
where the convergence holds almost everywhere and it is celar that
(xi) implies that

\[
\nu(E)=\lim_{N\to\infty}\int_E\, f_n\cdot d\mu=\int _E\, f_*\cdot d\mu
\quad\colon\,
E\in\mathcal B
\]
This gives $\nu= f_*\cdot d\mu$
and Theorem 3.3, is proved.
\bigskip


\centerline {\bf{3.4 A general decomposition.}}
\bigskip

\noindent
Let $\mu$ be a positive measure.
For any other positive measure
$\nu$
there exists a unique
decomposition of $\nu$ into a sum of one measure which is singular with
respect to $\mu$, while the other term is given by an
$L^1(d\mu)$-function. More precisely one has:
\medskip

\noindent  
{\bf 3.5 Theorem} \emph{Given a positive measure $\mu$ every other
positive measure $\nu$ is of the form}
\[ 
\nu=\nu_s+fd\mu\quad\colon\quad \nu_s\perp\mu\quad\text{and}\quad 
f\in L^1(d\mu)
\]
\bigskip

\noindent 
\emph{Proof}
To find the singular part $\nu_s$ we put
\[
M=\max\,\nu(A)\quad\colon\quad A\in\mathcal N_\mu\tag{1}
\]


\noindent
By  $\sigma$-additivity there exists some   $A_*\in\mathcal N_\mu$ such that
$\nu(A_*)=M$.
Define the measure $\nu_*$ by:
\[ 
\nu_*(E)=\nu(A_*\cap\,E)\tag{2}
\]
Here  $\nu_*\perp\mu$.
Put $\gamma=\nu-\nu_\ast$. The construction of $\nu_*$ gives 
\[ 
A\in\mathcal N_\mu\implies \gamma(A)=0\tag{3}
\]
Then Theorem 3.5 gives $\gamma=f\cdot \mu$
for some
$f\in L^1(\mu)$ and Theorem 3.6 follows.
\bigskip



\centerline {\bf{3.6 The Vitali-Hahn-Saks theorem.}}
\bigskip

\noindent
Let $\Omega,\mathcal B,\mu)$ be a probability space as in 
(0.1), i.e.  $\mathcal B$ is a Boolean $\sigma$-algebra and
$\mu$  a non-negative $\sigma$-additive measure with total mass
$\mu(\Omega)=1$.
Let $\mathcal N(\mu)$ be the $\sigma$-algebra of null-sets.
A pair of $\mu$-measurable sets are identified when
their Boolean difference is a null-set.
If $A\in\mathcal B$
then $A_*$ denotes its equivalence class.
Let $X$ denote the space whole points are such
equivalence classes of subdsets from $\mathcal  B$.
A metric is defined on $X$
by
\[
d(A_*,B_*)=\mu(A\setminus B)+ \mu(B\setminus A)
\]
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{Exercise.}}
Show that $X$ is a complete metric space. 
\medskip

\noindent
Next, an additive real-valued function $\lambda$
defined on
$\mathcal B$ is $\mu$-continuous if
\[
\lim_{\mu(A_*)\to 0}\,\lambda(A)=0
\]
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{Theorem.}}
\emph{Let $\{\lambda_n\}$ be a sequece of
additive $\mu$-continuus functions such that}
\[ 
\lim_{n\to\infty}\,\lambda_n(A)\tag{*}
\] 
\emph{exist for every $A\in\mathcal B$.
Then, for each $\epsilon>0$ there exists $\delta$ such that}
\[
\mu(A)\leq \delta\implies
\max_n\, |\lambda_n(A)|\leq \epsilon\tag{*}
\]
\medskip

\noindent
\emph{Proof.}
For each $\epsilon>0$
and every pair of integers $n,m$ we get the closed sets in $X$ given by
\[
\Sigma_{n,m}=\{A\in\mathcal B\,\colon\, 
|\lambda_n(A)-\lambda_m(A)|\leq\epsilon/2\}
\]
Set
\[
 \Sigma_p^*=\bigcap_{n,m\geq p}\Sigma_{n,m}
\]
Then (*) entails that
$\cup\, \Sigma^*_p=X$.
The Baire category theorem gives sopme  $p$ and
$\delta>0$ such that
\[
\mu(A)<\delta\implies A\in \Sigma_p^*
\]
Shrinking $\delta$ if necessary 
\[
\mu(A)<\delta\implies \max_{n \leq p_*}\lambda_n(A)\leq\epsilon/2
\]
Now the reader can check that (*) holds. inequality
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{Exercise.}}
Show that if $\lambda_*(A)$ is  the limit value in
the theorem for each $A$, then
$\lambda_*$ is additive and $\mu$-continuous.














\newpage



\centerline{\bf\large III. Lebesgue Theory}
\bigskip


\centerline{\emph{Contents}}

\bigskip



\noindent
0.  Introduction
\medskip

1. Dyadic grids and Lebesgue points
\medskip
