\bigskip











\centerline {\bf Introduction}
\bigskip

\noindent
The Lebesgue measure  is invariant under translations as well as rotations in
${\bf{R}}^n$.
Thus, 
the volume of a cube depends only upon the length of its sides and not upon its position.
We shall   construct measures which do not enjoy this invariance.
If $f$ is a locally integrable function in
${\bf{R}}^n$ in the sense of Lebesgue then
we obtain a measure
which to every 
bounded Lebesgue measurable set $E$ assigns
the mass
\[
 \int_E\, f\cdot dx
\]
\medskip

\noindent
We say that $f\cdot dx$ is an absolutely continuous 
measure with respect to $dx$.
The new class consists of \emph{singular} Riesz measures $\mu$ whose 
mass is concentrated to a null set in the sense of
Lebesgue.
Among these occur discrete measures which attach
masses at some sequence of points.
Less evident is the description of singular Riesz measures whose
mass of every singleton set is zero.
If $n=1$ we  described such measures in ¤ I
via  non-decreasing and continuos functions
$f(x)$ on the real $x$-line whose derivative is zero outside a set with
Lebegue measure zero. 
When $n\geq 2$
we have no clear "geometric picture" of a singular Riesz measure.
They are of a more implicit character and in general  one can just say that 
a Riesz measure $\mu$ represents a continuous linear form on
the space of continuous functions.
This follows from a  result due to Friedrich Riesz
which asserts that if $K$ is a compact subset of
${\bf{R}}^n$ and $C^0(K)$ the Banach space of continuous and 
real-valued functions on $K$, then its dual space consists of singular Riesz measures 
supported by $K$.
The \emph{Riesz representation formula} will 
be established  in ¤ 5.
\medskip

\noindent
In ¤ 1 we construct 
Riesz measures 
in
${\bf{R}}^n$ using dyadic grids which is
similar to
the construction of the Lebesgue measure, except that one now
arrives at a  family of $\sigma$-additive measures which
may assign positive mass
to null sets.
In ${\bf{R}}^n$ there exists  the algebra
$\mathcal B({\bf{R}}^n)$
of \emph{Borel measurable functions.}.
Using results from the section about from abstract measure theory 
we will show that if
$f$ is a bounded Borel function and $\mu$ some compactly supported
Riesz measure then there exists an integral
\[
\int f\cdot d\mu\tag{*}
\]
Their existence and properties
constitute a major part in the theory about Riesz measures in
${\bf{R}}^n$.








\newpage

\centerline {\bf 1. Dyadic grids.}
\bigskip

\noindent
If $N\geq 0$ we obtain the family $\mathcal D_N$ of
half-open cubes where we for each $n$-tuple
$\nu_\bullet\in{\bf{Z}}^n$ assign the cube
\[ 
\square_N(\nu_\bullet)=
\{ x\colon\, \nu2^{-N}\leq x_\nu<(\nu+1)2^{-N}\}
\]
It is clear that $\{\square_N(\nu_\bullet)\}$
is a disjoint covering of ${\bf{R}}^n$
for each fixed $N$. Next, let $\Omega$ be a bounded open set.
For each $x\in\Omega$ there exists a unique smallest integer $N(x)$
such that
$x$  belongs to a cube $\square\in\mathcal D_{N(x)}$ with
$\square\subset\Omega$.
This cube is denoted by $\square(x)$. The reader may verify
the implication
\[ 
y\in\square(x)\cap\Omega\implies\square(y)=\square(x)
\]
Hence  $\Omega$  is a unique
union of disjoint half\vvv open dyadic cubes. In other words, there exists
a denumerable sequence
$\{x_\nu\}$ in $\Omega$ such that the cubes
$\{\square(x_\nu)\}$ are disjoint and
\[
\Omega=\bigcup\,\square(x_\nu)\tag{*}
\] 



\noindent
We shall use this unique decomposition of open sets
to construct a family of additive functions.
Let $\mathcal D_*$ be the family of subsets
of
${\bf{R}}^n$ given by a finite and disjoint union of cubes
$\{\square_\alpha\}$ where each
$\square_\alpha\in \mathcal D_{N(\alpha)}$ for some
integer $N(\alpha)\geq 0$.
Suppose  that
\[
 \mu\colon \mathcal D_*\mapsto {\bf{R^+}}
 \] 
 is a non-negative and  additive function which means
that
 \[
 \mu(\cup \square_\alpha)= \sum\, \mu(\square_\alpha)
 \] 
hold for every  finite and disjoint family of dyadic half-open
cubes. 
\medskip


\noindent
{\bf{1.1 The $\mu$-measure on open sets.}}
Let $\Omega$
be a  bounded
open set. The unique  decomposition (*)
defines the number:

\[ 
\mu(\Omega)=\sum\, \mu(\square(x_\nu))\tag{**}
\]
The right hand side is a positive series.
Since
$\Omega$ is bounded the series is convergent.
To see this it suffices to observe that $\Omega$ is contained in some finite 
union of $K$ many cubes from the family $\mathcal D_0$. 
Since $\mu$ is non-negative and  
additive, each finite partial sum of the series in (**) is bounded
by the sum of $\mu$-masses over this $K$-tuple of cubes
in $\mathcal D_0$.
which entails that  (**) is convergent.
Next, let
$\{\Omega_\nu\}$ be a sequence of pairwise disjoint open sets
whose union is bounded. The uniqueness of
dyadic decompositions implies that
\[
\mu(\cup\, \Omega_\nu)=\sum\, \mu(\Omega_\nu)\tag{1}
\]

\medskip


\noindent
{\bf{1.2 The $\mu$-measure on compact sets}}.
Let $E$ be a compact subset of ${\bf{R}}^n$.
If $N\geq 0$ we set
\[ 
\rho_N(E)=
\sum\, \mu(\square)\quad\colon\quad
\square\in\mathcal D_N\quad\text{and}\quad \square\cap E\neq\emptyset
\]
If $N\geq 1$ and some $\square\in\mathcal D_N$ has 
a non-empty intersection with
$E$ it may occcur that some of the half-open cubes
in $\mathcal D_{N+1}$ which are contained in
$\square$ have empty intersection with $E$.
This observation shows that 
\[ 
N\mapsto \rho_N(E)\quad\text{decreases with}\quad  N
\] 
Hence there exists the limit
\[ 
\lim_{N\to\infty}\rho_N(E)
\]
The limit is denoted by $\mu(E)$ and is called the $\mu$-measure of the compact set.
\bigskip


\noindent
{\bf{1.3 Additivity.}}
Let $E$ be a compact set and $\Omega$ some bounded open set which contains
$E$. 
Consider some cube
$\square (x_\nu)\subset\Omega$ in  the decomposition of $\Omega$.
Here two cases can occur : Either $\square(x_\nu)\cap E\neq \emptyset$ or else
$\square (x_\nu)\subset\Omega\setminus E$.
 From this we conclude that the following equality holds for
 every integer $N\geq 0$:
 
 \[
\rho_N(\Omega)=\rho_N(\Omega\setminus E)+\rho_N(E)
\] 
where we have set

\[
\rho_N(\Omega)=
\sum\, \mu(\square)\quad\colon\quad
\square\in\mathcal D_N\quad\text{and}\quad \square\subset \Omega
\]
and similarly we define
$\rho_N(\Omega\setminus E)$.
Since the positive series (**) in 1.1  is convergent for  open sets,
a passage to the limit as $N\to\infty$ gives:

\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{1.4 Proposition.}} \emph{If $E$ is a compact set and if 
$E\subset\Omega$ for some bounded open set, then}
\[
\mu(E)+\mu(\Omega\setminus E)=\mu(\Omega)
\]



\medskip


\noindent
{\bf{1.5 Exercise.}}
Let $\mathfrak B_*$ denote the Boolean algebra of subsets of
${\bf{R}}^n$  generated by 
all open and all compact subsets. Let
$\mu$ be a non-negative and additive function on $\mathcal D_*$ which has a bounded
support in the sense that $\mu(\square)\neq 0$ only occurs for a finite
set of cubes in $\mathcal D_0$.
Use the results above to show that
$\mu$ extends to an additive map on $\mathfrak B_*$, i.e for 
each finite family of disjoint sets $\{F_k\}$ in $\mathfrak B_*$ it holds that
\[
\mu(\cup F_\nu)=\sum\, \mu(F_\nu)
\] 
whenever $F$ and $G$ are disjoint and bounded sets in
$\mathfrak B_*$.
\bigskip

\noindent
\centerline {\bf {1.6 $\sigma$-additivity.}}
\medskip

\noindent
Above we found  an additive measure
defined on bounded sets in
$\mathfrak B_*$. Now there exists the $\sigma$-algebra
$\mathfrak B$ of Borel sets which by definition this is the smallest
$\sigma$-algebra  containing
$\mathfrak B_*$.
In order that $\mu$ can be
extended to a $\sigma$-additive measure defined on $\mathfrak B$
we must impose additional conditions. If 
$\square\in\mathcal D_N $ is a  half-open dyadic cube and 
$0<a<1$ we obtain the closed cube
$a\bar\square$ defined by
\[ 
\nu_k2^{-N}\leq x_k\leq (\nu_k+a)2^{_N}\quad\colon 1\leq k\leq n\}
\]
\medskip

\noindent
By the construction in ¤ 1.2 we can assign the
$\mu$-measure to each of these compact cubes
and since $\mu$ is non-negative 
the function
\[ 
a\mapsto \mu(a\cdot\bar\square)\tag{i}
\] 
is increasing.
Moreover we have
\[
\mu(a\cdot\bar\square)\leq \mu(\square)\quad\colon\text{for each}\quad a<1\tag{ii}
\]


\medskip

\noindent
Hence (i-i) suggest the following:

\medskip

\noindent
{\bf {1.7 Definition.}} \emph{We say that the additive measure $\mu$ is regular if}
\[
\lim_{a\to 1}\, \mu(a\bar\square)=\mu(\square)
\] 
\emph{holds for every half-open dyadic cube.}
\medskip

\noindent 
{\bf {1.8 Remark}}.
This regularity condition is necessary in order that
a
$\sigma$-additive extension. In fact, this follows
since one has  the empty intersection:


\[
\bigcap_{a<1}\, (\square\setminus a\cdot\bar\square)=\emptyset
\]

\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{The case when
$\mu$ is regular. }} Let $\mu$ satisfy
the condition from Definition 1.7.
If $\Omega$ is a bounded open set and
$\epsilon>0$ we  find $N$ such that


\[
\mu(\Omega)<\rho_N(\Omega)+\epsilon\tag{i}
\]
Here $\rho_N(\Omega)=\sum\, \mu(\square_\nu)$ where the sum extends over a finite
set of cubes in $\mathcal D_N$.
The regularity condition  gives some $a<1$ such that
\[
\sum\,  \mu(\square_\nu)<
 \sum\,  \mu(a\cdot\bar\square_\nu)+\epsilon\tag{ii}
 \]
With $E=\cup\, a\cdot\bar\square_\nu$ 
it follows that

 \[
\mu(\Omega)<2\epsilon+\mu(E)\tag{iii}
\]
Notice that
$E$
is a \emph{compact} subset of
 $\Omega$.
Since $\epsilon>0$ can be arbitrary small  we have therefore proved:

\medskip

\noindent
{\bf {1.9 Proposition.}}
\emph{Let $\mu$ be regular, Then}
\[ 
\mu(\Omega)=\max_K\,\mu(K)
\] 
\emph{hold for every bounded open  set $\Omega$
where the maximum is taken over compact subsets of $\Omega$.}
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf {1.10 Exercise.}} Let $\mu$  be regular and
$E$ is a compact set. If $\delta>0$ we set
$E_\delta=\{ x\colon\,\text{dist}(x,E)<\delta\}$. Show that
\[
\lim_{\delta\to 0}\, \mu(E_\delta)=\mu(E)
\]
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf {1.11 Extension to the Borel algebra}}.
Denote by $\mathfrak B$ the 
$\sigma$-algebra generated
open and compact subsets of ${\bf{R}}^n$. Let $\mu$ be a regular and bounded
measure.  Using the
results above  it follows that $\mu$ extends  n a unique fashion to
a $\sigma$-additive measure defined on
all sets in $\mathfrak  B$.
So here

\[
\mu(\cup\, F_\nu)=\sum\,\mu(F_\nu)
\] 
\medskip

\noindent
hold for every denumerable disjoint union of Borel sets.
\bigskip



\noindent
{\bf {1.12 Non-negative Riesz measures}}.
This class consists of non-negative and regular measures $\mu$ defined as above.
We have seen that the $\sigma$-additivity follows from the regularity. Moreover 
the resulting $\sigma$-additive function on bounded Borel sets
is fully determined by the values assigned to all dyadic cubes.
\[
\square_N(\nu\cdot)=
\{ x=(x_1,\ldots,x_n)\,\colon\,\nu_j2^{-N}\leq  x_j<(\nu_j+1)2^{-N}
\}\tag{*}
\]


\noindent
Let us restrict the attention to the
cube
\[
\square_*=\{0\leq x_j<1\,\colon\, 1\leq j\leq n\}
\]
Then $\mathcal D_N$ contains 
$2^{Nn}$ many pairwise disjoint 
and half-open cubes whose  union cover $\square_*$.

\medskip
\noindent
By an induction over $N$
we  assign a non-negative mass to these cubes in
$\mathcal D_N$.
The  constraint is to preserve  additivity.
First $\mathcal D_0$ is a single cube to which we assign
the unit mass. Next, in $\mathcal D_1$
we have
$2^n$ cubes and we assign a non-negative mass to each
of them so that the sum is one.
Next, let
$N\geq 1$ and suppose that a mass $m_N(\nu\cdot)$ is assigned to
every cube $\square_N(\nu\cdot)\in\mathcal D_N$.
Notice that  a given
cube
$\square_N(\nu\cdot)\in\mathcal D_N$ is the union of
$2^n$ many cubes from
$\mathcal D_{N+1}$. To each of these we assign a mass so that
the sum of these $2^n$-many masses is equal to
$m_N(\nu\cdot)$.
\medskip

\noindent
This inductive construction works  for all $N$.
Suppose we have  performed such a construction over all 
positive integers $N$.
Hence a mass $m_N(\nu\cdot )$ is assigned to
$\square_N(\nu\cdot )$ for every
$N$ and each of the $2^{Nn}$  many
$n$-tuples $ \nu\cdot =(\nu_1,\ldots,\nu_n)$.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf {The regularity condition.}}
Above we have seen how an inductive construction
yields an additive measure $\mu$.
But it does not follow that it is regular, i.e. the condition from 
Definition 1.7 is not automatic.
So here one encounters a set-theoretic problem, i.e. to describe
conditions during  the inductive construction of the
assigned masses $\{m_N(\nu\cdot)\}$ in order that
$\mu$ is regular.
A necessary and sufficient criterium for this regularity is not
known, i.e. this would mean that there exists a 
fully \emph{constructive}
method to achieve all Riesz measures.
So the  reader should  be aware of the fact that the
space of Riesz measures quite abstract and we remark that
it contains measures whose  existence rely upon the
Axiom of Choice.

\newpage



\centerline{\bf 2. Singular Riesz measures}
\medskip

\noindent
By the Radon Nikodym theorem
every Riesz measure $\mu$ whose total mass is finite
is the unique sum of a singular part and 
an absolutely continuous measures defined as a density by  some
$L^1({\bf{ R}}^n)$-function. 
Consider for simplicity a  measure
$\mu$ supported by the unit cube, i.e.
it is constructed via the dyadic grid on
$\square$. 
The result below gives the condition in order
that  $\mu$ is singular, i.e. its total mass taken over a set
of Lebesgue measure. 

\medskip

\noindent
{\bf 2.1 Theorem}
\emph{The measure $\mu$ is singular if and only if the following hold:
For each $\epsilon>0$ there exists an integer $N$
such that
we can find a subfamily
$\mathcal F_N= \{\square_N(\nu\cdot)\}$ of $\mathcal D_N$
where the number of cubes in
$\mathcal F_N$ is $<\epsilon\cdot 2^{Nn}$
while}
\[
\sum\,
\mu(\square_N(\nu\cdot))>1-\epsilon\quad\colon\quad
\sum\,\,\text{taken over cubes}\,\,
\square_N(\nu\cdot)\in\mathcal F_N
\]
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf Exercise.} Prove this result.




\bigskip

\centerline {\bf 3. Signed measures}.
\medskip

\noindent
A signed measure $\mu$ supported by the unit cube
arises by the inductive construction above
when we  allow that some of
the numbers $m_N(\nu\cdot)$  are negative.
In addition to additivity we  impose the condition that
\[
 \sum_\nu\, |m_N(\nu\cdot)[=1
\] 
hold for each $N$. In this case $\mu$ is again a $\sigma$-additive 
measure defined on
the Borel algebra $\mathcal B$. Moreover, by the
Hahn decomposition theorem from ¤ 2: XX
we can write
$\mu$ as difference of two non-negative Riesz measures
$\nu_1,\nu_2$ which are $\perp$ to each other.







\bigskip

\centerline{\bf 4. $\mu$-integrals.}
\medskip

\noindent
Let $\mu$ be a non-negative measure on the unit cube constructed
as above. 
Let $f(x)$ be a continuous function 
defined on the compact unit cube.
Let $M=|f|_\square$ be the maximum norm
of $|f|$ over $\square$.
The integral $\int f d\mu$ is defined
as the limit of the increasing sequence
\[
\rho_N=
\sum_{\nu=0}^{\nu=2^N-1}\,
M\cdot \nu\cdot 2^{-N}\cdot\mu(\,\nu 2^{-N}\leq f<M\cdot (\nu+1) 2^{-N}\,)
\] 
The convergence of $\{\rho_N\}$ is robust exactly as in
the abstract theory from XX.
In this way we obtain
an additive map
\[ 
f\mapsto \int\, f d\mu\tag{1}
\]
\medskip

\noindent
With  the terminology of functional analysis this means that the
$\mu$-integral yields a continuous linear form on
the normed space of continuous functions on $\square$.
The \emph{converse} is fundamental, i.e. one has


\newpage


\centerline {\bf 5. Riesz' representation theorem.}
\bigskip

\noindent
Let $\mathcal L$
be a continuous linear functional  on the Banach space
$C^0(\square)$
of real-valued continuos functions on $\square$. Using a similar procedure as in
the proof of the Hahn decomposition theorem for signed measures one shows that 
$\mathcal L$ can be written in a unique way as the difference of two
non-negative linear forms. Let us then assume that
that $\mathcal L(f)\geq 0$ for
every non-negative continuous function. If 1 is the identity number we have
the positive number
$\mathcal L(1)$ and without loss of generality we assume that
$\mathcal L(1)=1$.
Now we construct a regular  measure $\mu$
as follows: Let $\square$ be a dyadic cube in $\mathcal D_N$ for some
$N\geq 1$.
To each $0<a<1$ we get the compact cube $a\cdot\square$ and set

\[
 \mu_a(\square)=\min_f\,\mathcal L(f)\colon \quad\text{where}\quad f|a\cdot\square=1\quad\text{and}\quad
 f\geq 0\tag{i}
\]
It is clear that
$a\mapsto \mu_a(\square)$ increases with $a$ and we set

\[
\mu(\square)=\lim_{a\to 1}\, \mu_a(\square)\tag{ii}
\]
If $\square_1$ and $\square_2$ are two different dyadic cubes in
$\mathcal D_N$ we notice that the compact cubes
$a\cdot \square_1$ and
$a\cdot \square_2$ are disjoint. This observation and
the possibility to construct continuous functions
in (i) which vanish outside arbitrary small open neighborhoods of
$a\cdot \square$, imply that
(i) yields an additive function on the family of all dyadic cubes.
Moreover, by the construction via (ii) it is automatically regular.
and hence $\mu$ is a Riesz measure.
Next, let 
$E$ be a closed subset of
$\square$. One says that $E$ is a null set with respect to $\mathcal L$ if

\[
\min_f\,\mathcal L(f)=0\quad\colon\quad f|E=1\quad\colon f\geq 0
\]
This makes sense because for every $\delta>0$ one constructs 
a continuous function $f$ which is 1 on $E$ and has compact support in
its $\delta$-open neighborhood $E_\delta$.
Let us now \emph{assume} that the boundaries of all dyadic cubes
are null sets for $\mathcal L$.
This hypothesis is not restrictive since we otherwise
can use a translation of
${\bf{R}}^n$ by an $n$-tuple of numbers from
the non-denumarable set of real numbers, i.e. after such a translation
we get null sets and place the dyadic grid in the corresponding unit cube to
construct measures as in 0.X.
Under this assumption
we obtain a non-negative Riesz measure by
the following procedure: 
If $N\geq 1$ and $\square_N(\nu\cdot)$ is a dyadic cube
we define:
\medskip

\[ 
\rho_N(\nu)=
\max_f\, \mathcal L(f)\quad\colon\, 0\leq f\leq 1
\,\,\text{and has compact support in}\,\, \square_N(\nu\cdot)
\]
One verifies easily that these $\rho$-numbers satisfy the
additivity during the inductive construction as $N$ increases and hence
we obtain a Riesz measure $\mu$. Moreover, it is clear
from the whole construction that
\[
\mathcal L(f)=\int\, fd\mu\quad\colon\,f\in C^0(\square)
\]
Notice that this gives a canonical procedure to find $\mu$ when
$\mathcal L$ is given.
\bigskip

\noindent
The conclusion from this is
the following result from the original work by F. Riesz:
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf Theorem.}
\emph{Let $\Omega$ be an open set in
${\bf{R}}^n$ and 
$\mathcal L$ is a linear form
on the space $C^0_0(\Omega)$ of continuous functions with
compact support in $\Omega$. Assume also that to every
compact subset
$K$ of $\Omega$ there exists a constant $C_K$ such that}
\[
|\mathcal L(f)|\leq C_K\cdot |f|_K\quad\colon\quad
\text{Supp}(f)\subset K
\]
\emph{Then there exists a unique - in general signed - Riesz measure
$\mu$ in $\Omega$ such that}
\[
\mathcal L(f)=\int\, f\cdot d\mu\quad\colon\quad 
f\in C^0_0(\Omega)
\]


\newpage

\centerline {\bf 6. Borel measurable functions.}
\bigskip

\noindent
In many  applications one needs to integrate
functions which are not necessarily continuous.
To construct  $\mu$-integrals with
discontinuous functions as integrands we
regard the unique smallest $\sigma$-algebra of subsets of
${\bf{R}}^n$
generated by open sets.
It is denoted by $\mathcal B$
and  sets in this $\sigma$-algebra are called Borel sets.
Since a Riesz measure $\mu$ is $\sigma$-additive
and hence we can assign a measure $\mu(B)$ to every
Borel set $B$.
Next, we consider $\mathcal B$-measurable functions
$f$. It means that a real-valued function
$f$ is Borel measurable if the sets
\[ 
\{x\in{\bf{R}}^n\quad\colon\,\, f(x)<t \}\in\mathcal B\quad
\colon\text{for every real number}\,\, t\tag{*}
\]


\noindent 
Using (*) one constructs integrals $\int\, f\cdot d\mu$
exactly as in the abstract theory from Section ¤ XX.






\bigskip

\centerline {\bf 7. Weak convergence.} 
\medskip

\noindent
Consider a sequence of non-negative   measures $\{\mu_\nu\}$ where each 
measure has total mass one on $\square$. 
One says that the sequence converges weakly to a limit measure
$\mu^* $ if
\[
\lim_{\nu\to\infty}\,\int f d\mu_\nu=\int fd\mu^*
\quad\colon\,\, f\in C^0(\square)\tag{*}
\]


\noindent
{\bf 7.1 Remark.}
Suppose that the standard dyadic grid is a null set for
all
measures -$\{\mu_\nu\}$
and also for the limit measure
$\mu^*$. Then
the \emph{uniform} continuity of each 
$f\in C^0(\square)$
shows that weak convergence holds if and only if
\[
\lim_{\nu\to\infty}\, \mu_\nu(\square_N(k\cdot))=
 \mu^*(\square_N(k\cdot))\quad\colon\, N\geq 1\quad\colon
\text{for every}\,\, k(\cdot)-\text{tuple}
\]


\noindent 
Thus, weak convergence means precisely that the 
mass distributions during the inductive
partition of $\square$ into smaller dyadic cubes
give convergent sequences of
the associated real $2^N$-tuples for every $N\geq 1$.
Next, recall Bolzano's
theorem, i.e. that  every infinite sequence of real numbers on a 
bounded interval has a convergent subsequence. 
Using this and the  
"diagonal procedure" the reader may verify the following fundamental fact:
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf 7.2 Theorem.} \emph{Let $\{\mu_\nu\}$ be a sequence of 
non-negative measures on
$\square$ - each with total mass one. Then there exists at least one
subsequence which converges weakly.}
\bigskip

\noindent
{\bf{7.3 Weak density of discrete measures.}}
Among Riesz measures occur finite sums of 
point-masses.
Consider a sequence
\[ \mu_j=\sum\, c_{j,k}\cdot x_k\quad\colon\,j=1,2,\ldots
\]
where $\{x_1,x_2, \ldots\}$ is a denumarable sequence of points in
the unit cube
$\square$.  At  the same time each $\mu_j$
is a probability measure, i.e.
\[ 
\sum_j\, c_{j,k}=1\quad\colon\, k=1,2,\ldots
\]
The condition for the sequence to converge weakly is now
easy to check.
Namely, to every $N\geq 1$ and every cube
$\square_N(\nu\dot)$ in $\mathcal D_N$
we have
\[ 
\mu_j(\square_N(\nu\cdot))=\sum_*\, c_{j,k}\,\quad\colon
\text{sum taken over those}\,\, k\,\,\text{with}\,\, x_k\in\square_N(\nu\cdot)
\]


\noindent
So above  weak convergence holds if and only if
these numbers have a limit as $j\to\infty$ for
every $N\geq 1$ and every cube in $\mathcal D_N$.
Expanding each $x_k$ in the binary system
one can proceed to get an understanding when
such a weak convergence takes place.
Notice that if the sequence $\{x_k\}$ is given from the start
then there may exist many doubly indexed 
$c$-sequences where we get a weak limit and moreover one may attain
different limit measures depending on the chosen
$c$-sequence.
This occurs in particular when the sequence
$\{x_k\}$ is everywhere dense. For example, if one has enumarated
points in
the unit cube whose coordinates are all rational numbers.
In this
situation every probability measure on $\square$ is the weak
limit of a sequence $\{\mu_j\}$.

\medskip


\noindent 
{\bf{7.4 Exercise.}}
Show that if $\mu$ is a Riesz measure with compact support then
it can be approximaed weakly by a sequence of discrete measures.



\bigskip



\centerline
{\bf 8. Products and convolution of Riesz measures}

\bigskip

\noindent
Let $n$ and $m$ be two positive integers and
$\mu$ and $\nu$ are Riesz measures
in ${\bf{R}}^n$ and
${\bf{R}}^m$ respectively.
The family of dyadic cubes in ${\bf{R}}^{n+m}$
is   obtained  by  products of
cubes from grids in ${\bf{R}}^n$ and ${\bf{R}}^m$.
This gives a unique 
product measure
$\mu\times\nu$ which
satisfies
\[
(\mu\times\nu)(\Omega\times U)=
\mu(\Omega)\cdot \nu(U)
\]
where $\Omega\subset{\bf{R}}^n$ and
$U\subset {\bf{R}}^n$ are open sets.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf 8.1 Projections of measures.}
Let $\gamma$ be a measure in
${\bf{R}}^{n+m}$
where the coordinates are $(x,y)$ with
$x\in{\bf{R}}^n$ and $y\in {\bf{R}}^m$.
We have the projection from
${\bf{R}}^{n+m}$ onto ${\bf{R}}^n$ defined by
\[ 
\pi(x,y)=x
\]
Assume  that
$\gamma$ has a finite total mass which gives
a measure
$\pi_*(\gamma)$ in
${\bf{R}}^n$ defined on Borel sets $F$
by
\[
\pi_*(\gamma)(F)=\gamma(F\times{\bf{R}}^m)\tag{*}
\]


\noindent
One refers to $\gamma_*$ as the direct image measure.

\medskip

\noindent
{\bf 8.2 Convolution.} Let $\mu,\nu$ be a pair of measures in
${\bf{R}}^n$. We get the product measure
$\mu\times\nu$ in
${\bf{R}}^{2n}$ whose points are denoted by
$(x,y)$ where
we regard
$\mu$ as a measure in the $n$-dimensional $x$-space and
$\nu$ as a measure in the $y$-space.
In ${\bf{R}}^{2n}$ we  take  coordinates
$(t,s)$ where
\[ 
t_\nu=x_\nu+y_\nu\quad\colon\quad
s_\nu=x_\nu-y_\nu
\]
Now  $\mu\times\nu$ is a measure in the
$(t,s)$ space and   the projection
$\pi(t,s)=t$  gives the measure
\[
\pi_\ast(\mu\times\nu)
\]
It is denoted by $\mu*\nu$ 
and called the convolution of
$\mu$ and $\nu$.
If $f(t)$ is a continuous function with compact
support in the $t$-space the constructions of
$\pi_\ast(\mu\times\nu)$ and 
$\mu\times\nu$ give
\[
\int\, f(t)\cdot d(\mu*\nu)(t)=\iint f(x+y)d\mu(x)d\nu(y)\tag{*}
\]
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf Remark.}
The last integral is symmetric with respect to $\mu$ and $\nu$.
Hence the convolution satisfies the commutative law:
\[
 \mu*\nu=\nu*\mu
\]
\medskip

\noindent
One can  continue  and construct the convolution of
a triple of measures, and more generally the convolution of
any number of measures in ${\bf{R}}^n$.
For example, if $\mu,\nu,\gamma$ is a triple of measures
in
${\bf{R}}^n$ then
we get the measure
defined by
\[
\int_{{\bf{R}}^n}\, f(t)\cdot d(\mu*\nu*\gamma)=
\iiint\, f(x+y+z)\cdot d\mu(x)d\nu(y)d\gamma(z)
\]
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf 8.3 Exercise.}
Show that the
convolution satisfies
the associative law.
So if $M({\bf{R}}^n)$ denotes the space of Riesz
measures with finite total variation, then convolution
equips it with
a commutative product, i.e.
$M({\bf{R}}^n)$  becomes a commutative algebra. 
The multiplicative unit is the point mass at
the origin.
One has also the inequality for norms:
\[
||\mu*\nu||\leq ||\mu||\cdot ||\nu||
\]
i.e. the total mass of a convolution is at most the product of
the individual total masses.
\bigskip

\noindent
{\bf 8.4 Example.}
Let $E$ and $F$ be two compact sets
in ${\bf{R}}^n$, each with a positive Lebesgue measure.
We get the new compact set
defined by
\[
E+F=\{ x+y\quad\colon\quad x\in E\quad\colon y\in F\}
\]
Next, the two characteristic functions
$\chi_E$ and $\chi_F$ define measures where
\[
\int\, f\cdot d\chi_E=\int_E\, f\cdot dx\quad\colon\quad
\int\, f\cdot d\chi_F=\int_F\, f\cdot dx
\]
The construction of the convolution shows that
\[
\chi_E*\chi_F= \chi_{E+F}
\]
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf 8.5 Exercise.}
Let $\mu$ be the discrete measure on the real line
which has mass $1/2$ at $x=0$ and at $x=1$.
Let $N\geq 2$ be an integer and consider the
$N$-fold convolution $\mu^N$.
Show that this is a discrete measure supported by
the integers $0,1,\ldots,N$ and the mass at
$0\leq k\leq N$ is given by
$2^{-N}\cdot \binom Nk$.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf 8.6 Exercise.}
Let $n=2$ and $\mu$
is the Riesz measure 
supported by the unit circle $\{x^2+y^2=1\}$
where it is given by the constant angular measure, i.e.
if $f(x,y)$ is a continuous function then
\[
\int\, f\cdot d\mu=\frac{1}{2\pi}\cdot \int_0^{2\pi}
f(\cos\theta,\sin\theta)\cdot d\theta
\]
Consider the convolution of $\mu$ with itself, i.e.  the Riesz measure
$\nu=\mu*\mu$.
By (8.5) the support is $T+T$, i.e. the set of points $(x,y)$ of the form
\[
x=\cos\theta+\cos\phi\quad\colon\,
y=\sin\theta+\sin\phi
\]
The reader is invited to
find the picture of this set and try to investigate the resulting
measure.
One gateway for this is to employ Fourier transforms.
First one has
\[ 
\widehat{\mu}(\xi,\eta)=
\int_0^{2\pi}\, e^{-i\xi\cos\theta-i\eta\sin\theta}\,d\theta
\]
We use polar coordinates in the $(\xi,\eta)$-space.
With $\xi=r\cos\phi$ and $\eta=r\sin\phi$
the addition formula for the cosine function shows that the
integral becomes
\[
\int_0^{2\pi}\, e^{-ir\cdot \cos(\theta-\phi)}\,d\theta\tag{i}
\]
Since the cosine function is periodic (i)  depends
on $r$ only and expressed by the function
\[
r\mapsto \int_0^{2\pi}\, e^{-ir\cdot \cos \theta}\,d\theta\tag{ii}
\]
As explained in the chapter devoted to distribution theory
the Fourier transform of $\mu*\mu$ is $\widehat{\mu}^2$
which entails that it is a function of $r$ only.
Via Fourier's inversion formula it follows that
the measure $\mu*\mu$ is invariant under rotations.
By (xx) it is supported by a disc of radius $2$ centered at the origin
and determined by the function
\[
\phi(s)=(\mu*\mu)(\{x^2+y^2\leq s^2\})\quad\colon 0\leq s\leq 2\tag{iii}
\]
The precise expression  of this $\phi$-function
is not so easy. See ¤ xx for a further account which involves
the construction of certain Bessel functions.
This iilustrates that the passage to convolutions  can  lead to quite
cumbersome computations.














\newpage

\centerline{\bf 5. Stokes Theorem}
\bigskip


\noindent 
Consider a bounded and connected open set $\Omega$ in $\bf R^n$.
Its boundary $\partial\Omega$ is  compact but we do no assume that 
$\partial\Omega$  is
connected.
A boundary point $p$ is  \emph{regular}
if there exists some open ball $B$ centered at $p$ and a real-valued
$C^1$-function $\phi$ in $B$ such that
\[
\Omega\cap\,B=\{\phi<0\}\quad\colon\,
\partial \Omega\cap\,B=\{\phi=0\}\quad\colon\nabla(\phi)\neq 0
\]
It is clear that the set of regular boundary points is a relatively open
subset of $\partial\Omega$. Denote this set by
$\mathfrak{reg}(\partial\Omega)$.
Put
\[ 
K=\partial\Omega\setminus\mathfrak{reg}(\partial\Omega)\tag{1}
\]


\noindent
We impose the condition that $K$ has Hausdorff measure zero in dimension $n-1$.
Next there exists the area measure
on $\mathfrak{reg}(\partial\Omega)$ to be denoted by 
$dS$ which  
is found locally as follows:
Let $p$ be a regular boundary point
and assume that the partial derivative
$\frac{\partial\phi}{\partial x_n}\neq 0$ at $p$.
The implicit function theorem gives
an open neighborhood $U$ of $p$ where
\[
\phi(p+x)=(x_n-a(x'))\cdot \rho(x)
\]
for a pair of real-valued $c^1$-functions $a$ and $\rho$. Here
$\rho>0$ in $U$ and
\[
\Omega\cap U=\{x_n<a(x')\}
\]
where $a$ only depends on $x'=(x_1,\ldots,x_{n-1})$ 
and $a=$ when $x'=0$.
So locally one has 
\[
\partial\Omega\cap U=\{x_n=a(x')\}\tag{i}
\]
We can choose $U$ so that
(i) holds while $x'$ varies in a small open cube $\square$
centered at the origin in
this $(n-1)$-dimensional space.
By  a wellknown result in calculus
which follows from the theorem  of Pythagoras, the $(n-1)$-dimensional area
of
$\partial\Omega\cap U$ is given by
the integral
\[ 
\int_\square\, \sqrt{1+|\nabla(a)(x')|^2}\, dx'\tag{ii}
\]
where
\[
|\nabla(a)(x')|^2=\sum_{j=1}^{j=n-1}\,
(\frac{\partial a}{\partial x_j})^2
\]
The reader may verify that
(ii) can be written as
\[ 
\int_\square\,\frac{1}{|\frac{\partial \phi}{\partial x_n}|}\cdot
|\nabla(\phi)|\, dx'\tag{xx}
\]
where the partial derivatives of $\phi$ are evaluated at
$x=(x',a(x')$ during the integration over
$\square$.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{5.0 Definition.}}
\emph{
The domain $\Omega$ is of Federer type
if the Hausdorff measure $|K|_{n-1}=0$ and the
the total area}
\[ 
\int_{\text{reg}(\partial\Omega)}\, dS<\infty\tag{*}
\]
\medskip


\noindent
{\bf 5.1 Theorem.} \emph{Let $\Omega$ be a domain of the Federer type
and $f(x)$  a function of class $C^1$
defined in some open neighborhood of
the
closure
$\bar \Omega$. Then }
\[ 
\int_\Omega\,\partial f/\partial x_j\cdot dx=
\int_{\text{reg}(\partial\Omega)}\, f\cdot\mathfrak n_j\cdot dS
\quad\colon\quad 1\leq j\leq n
\]
\emph{where $\mathfrak n_j$ is the $x_j$ component of 
the outer normal $\mathfrak n$.}
\bigskip

\noindent
The proof requires several steps and we begin with a
case where the "ugly set" $K$ is empty.

\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{5.2 The case of 
graphic domains}}.
Consider an open cube of the form
\[ \square=\{x\colon
-A\leq x_\nu<A\colon\, \nu=1,\ldots,n\}
\]
Put $x'=(x_1,\ldots,x_{n-1}$ and
let $\phi(x')$ be a $C^1$ function
defined on the cube 
\[
\square_*=\{ -A<x_\nu<A\colon \nu=1,\ldots,n-1\}
\]
Here $-A<\phi(x')<A$ is assumed which gives  the graphic domain
\medskip
\[
\Omega=\{(x',x_n)\colon\, -A<x_n<\phi(x')\quad\colon\tag{1}
x'\in\square_*\}
\]

\noindent
Next,
let $f(x)$ be a $C^1$-function
with compact support in
$\square$.
Then we shall prove that
\[
\int_\Omega\,\partial f/\partial x_j\, dx=
\int_{\partial\Omega)}\, f\cdot\mathfrak n_j\cdot dS
\quad\colon\quad 1\leq j\leq n\tag{*}
\]
\medskip

\noindent
If $j=n$  the volume integral is 
equal to the repeated integral
\[ 
\int_{\square_*}\bigl [\int_{-A}^{\phi(x')}\, \partial f(x',x_n)/
\partial x_n\cdot dx_n\bigr ]\, dx'=
\int_{\square_*}\,f(x',\phi(x')\,dx'\tag{1}
\]

\noindent
Now $dx'=\mathfrak{n_n}\cdot dS$  on
the part of $\partial\Omega$ defined by $x_n=\phi(x')$.
Moreover, since $f$ has compact support in
$\square$ it vanishes on the remaining boundary of
$\partial\Omega$ where at least some $|x_j|=A$.
From this it is clear that (*) holds if $j=n$
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{The case $1\leq j\leq n-1$}}. We may assume that
 $j=1$ to simplify  the notations. Set
\[
\psi(x')=\int_{-A}^{\phi(x')}\, f(x',s) ds
\]
Partial integration with respect to $x_1$ gives
\[
\partial \psi/\partial x_1(x')=
f(x',\phi(x'))\cdot\partial \phi/\partial x_1(x')+
\int_{-A}^{\phi(x')}\, \partial f/\partial x_1(x',s) \,ds\tag{i}
\]
Now we integrate over $\square_*$. Since
the $\psi$-function is zero when $|x_1|=A$ we have trivially
\[
\int_{\square_*}\, \partial\psi/\partial x_1(x')dx'=0\tag{ii}
\]
In the right hand side of (i)
the second term
yields the repeated integral
\[
\int_{\square_*}\,\bigl [\int_{-A}^{\phi(x')}\, 
\partial f/\partial x_1(x',s) \cdot ds\bigr ]\,\cdot dx'=\int_\Omega\, 
\partial f/\partial x_1(x)\,dx
\]
So the vanisihing of (ii) entails that  this volume integral is equal to
\[
-\int_{\square_*}\,
f(x',\phi(x'))\cdot\partial \phi/\partial x_1(x')\cdot dx'
\]


\noindent 
Next, on the part of $\partial\Omega$ defined by $\{x_n-\phi(x')=0\}$
one has the equality
\[
\mathfrak{n_1}\cdot dS=-
\partial \phi/\partial x_1(x')\cdot dx'
\] 
Hence  (*) holds
with $j=1$ and the case
$2\leq j\leq n-1$ is treated in the same way
which  finishes the proof of (*).


\bigskip

\noindent
{\bf 5.3. Using partition of the unity}
Consider the situation in Theorem 1 and   impose the extra 
assumption that $f=0$ in an open  neighborhood $U$
of the ugly set $K$.
In this case the result in  5.2  gives Theorem 5.1 after a  partition of the unity.
Namely, the compact set
$W=\partial\Omega\setminus U$ is contained in
$\mathfrak{reg}(\partial\Omega)$ and 
for each  $p\in W$ there exists a cube
$\square$ centered at $p$
such that $\square\cap\Omega$ is a graphic domain.
By Heine-Borel's Lemma we can cover $W$ by a finite set of such cubes, say
$\square_1,\ldots,\square_M$.
Finally we can cover the compact set
$\Omega\setminus U\cup\square_1\cup\ldots\cup\square_M$
by a finite set of cubes $\square_{M+1},\ldots,\square_{M+N}$
where the closure of these cubes are contained in $\Omega$.
Then we construct a $C^\infty$-partition of the unity, i.e. we find
a family $\{\phi_\nu\in C_0^\infty(\square_\nu)\}$
so that $\sum\,\phi_\nu=1$ holds in a neighborhood of
the support of $f$.
Now  Stokes formula from (1.2)  hold for every function
$f\cdot \phi_\nu$ and by adding the result we get
Stokes formula for $f$ in Theorem 5.1.
\newpage


\centerline {\bf 5.4 How to avoid $K$}
\medskip

\noindent
Consider the general case with  no assumption on  the support of  $f$.
To prove the integral  formula in Theorem 5.1 for each $x_j$
we can 
without loss of generality  take $j=n$.
Let
$\pi(x',x_n)=x'$ be the projection to the
$n-1$-dimensional $x'$-space. The assumption that $|K|_{n-1}=0$
entails that 
the image set $\pi(K)$ is  a null set in  the $(n-1)$-dimensional $x'$-space.
Let  $\phi_1(x'),\phi_2(x'),\ldots$
be a sequence of test-functions in ${\bf{R}}^{n-1}$
which converge almost everywhere to 1 outside
$\pi(K)$, while  each of them has compact 
support in ${\bf{R}}^{n-1}\setminus K$ and 
$0\leq\phi_\nu\leq1 $ hold for every $\nu$.
\medskip

\noindent
By (5.3)  Stokes Formula holds for every function 
$\phi_\nu f$. Since the functions $\{\phi_\nu(x')\} $ are independent of $x_n$
we get
\[
\int_\Omega\, \phi_\nu\cdot\partial_n(f)\cdot dx=
\int_{\mathfrak{reg}(\partial\Omega)}\, \phi_\nu\cdot f\cdot \mathfrak{n}_n\cdot dS\tag{5.4.1}
\]
\medskip

\noindent
Since $\phi_\nu\to 1$ holds almost everywhere in
the $x'$-space, Lebesgue's dominated convergence
theorem implies that the left hand side in (5.4.1) tends to the volume
integral of $\partial_n(f)$.
There remains to prove that the right hand side tends
to the integral of $f\cdot\mathfrak{n}_n$ 
taken over the  regular boundary.
Since $f$  is a bounded function and the total area is finite
the requested limit  this follows if
we have proved:
\[
\lim_{\nu\to\infty}\,
\int_{\mathfrak{reg}\partial\Omega)}\, (1-\phi_\nu)\cdot  \mathfrak{n}_n\cdot dS=0\tag{5.4.2}
\]


\noindent
To prove (5.4.2) we let $\epsilon>0$ and put
\[ 
\Gamma_\epsilon=
\{|\mathfrak{n}_n|\geq\epsilon\}\,\cap\, \mathfrak{reg}(\partial\Omega)
\]


\noindent
Set  $M=\text{Area}\,[\mathfrak{reg}(\partial\Omega)]$. Without loss of generality we
can assume that the maximum norm of $f$ is $\leq 1$.
Then (5.4.2) is majorized by
\[
M\cdot\epsilon+
\int_{\Gamma_\epsilon}\, (1-\phi_\nu)\cdot  \mathfrak{n}_n\cdot dS=0\tag{5.4.3}
\]




\noindent
Next, on $\Gamma_\epsilon$ the area measure
$dS$ is majorized by
$\frac{1}{\epsilon}\cdot dx'$.
Hence  the sequence $\{g_\nu=1-\phi_\nu\}$ regarded as functions on
the measure space
$(\Gamma_\epsilon\,;\,dS)$ converge almost everywhere to zero which gives
\[
\lim_{\nu\to\infty}\, \int_{\Gamma_\epsilon}\, (1-\phi_\nu)\cdot  \mathfrak{n}_n\cdot dS=0\tag{5.4.4}
\]
\medskip

\noindent
In particular we can take $\nu$ so large that (5.4.4) is $<\epsilon$. Since
$\epsilon$ was arbitrary  the limit formula (5.4.2) holds
and the proof of Theorem 5.1 is finished.
\medskip


\noindent
{\bf{5.5 Remarks about   Federer domains.}}
Suppose that $f$ is a non-negative $C^2$-function and
the bounded domain $\Omega$ is defined by
$\{f<a\}$ for a pair of real constants $a>0$.
To each $1\leq j\leq n$ we set $\partial_j(f)=
\frac{\partial f}{\partial x_j}$.
On the regular part of $\partial\Omega$
the outer normal
\[ 
{\bf{n}}_j=\frac{\partial_j(f)}{|\nabla(f)|}
\]
Let us assume that
$\nabla(f)\neq 0$ in $\Omega$.
So for ech $1\leq j\leq n$
there exists the $C^1$-function in $\Omega$ defined by
\[ 
g_j=\frac{\partial_j(f)}{|\nabla(f)|}
\]
If $\delta>0$ is small we have the domain
$\Omega(\delta)= \{f<a-\delta\}$ 
and  Stokes theorem gives
\[
\int_{\Omega(\delta)}\, \partial_j(g_j)\,dx=
\int_{\partial \Omega(\delta)}\, g_j\cdot {\bf{n}}_j\cdot dS
\quad\colon \,1\leq j\leq n
\]
By (xx) it follows that
\[
\sum_{j=1]}^{j=n}
\int_{\Omega(\delta)}\, \partial_j(g_j)\,dx=
\, \int_{\partial \Omega(\delta)}\, dS
\]
If $\Omega$ is a Federer domain one expects that
there is a limit formula
\[
\lim_{\delta\to 0}\,  \int_{\partial \Omega(\delta)}\, dS=
 \int_{\text{reg}(\partial \Omega)}\, dS
\]
\medskip

\noindent
The passage to the limit can be
understood via distribution theory.
For each $\delta>0$ we denote by $\chi_\delta$ the characteristic function of
the open det
$\Omega(\delta)$.
At the same time
$\{g_j\}$ are bounded funtions in
$\Omega$ and the defintion of distribution derivatives entail
that
\[
\frac{\partial \chi_\delta}{\partial x_j}(g_j)=
-\int_{\Omega(\delta)}\, \partial_j(g_j)\,dx
\]
So (xx) means that
\[
\sum\, \frac{\partial \chi_\delta}{\partial x_j}(g_j)=\text{vol}_{n-1}(\partial \Omega(\delta))
\]
At the same time the distributions $\{\chi_\delta\}$
converge
to the distribution defined by the characteristic function of
$\Omega$. Indeed,  this is so because the $n$-dimensional Lebesgue measure of
$\Omega\setminus \Omega(\delta)$ tends to zero.
Passing to distribution derivatives it follows that
\[
\lim_{\delta\to 0}\,
\frac{\partial \chi_\delta}{\partial x_j}=
\frac{\partial \chi_\Omega}{\partial x_j}
\]
But this weak limit in the space of distributions
need not impliy that evaluations on
the bounded $g$-functions give a limit since they
need not
extend so nicely to the closure of $\Omega$
where the gradient vector of $f$ may vanish at some points.
This illustrates  why the conditions in Theorem 5.2 are essential.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{The polynomial case.}}
A case of special interest occurs when 
$f(x)$ is a real-valued polynomial. By Sard's Lemma the
set of critical values is sparse
and situations as above arise when we in addition assume that
the polynomial is hyper-elliptic, i.e when
\[ 
\lim_{|x|\to+\infty}\, |f(x)|=+\infty
\]
In this situation the boundsries
of $\{f<a\}$ are smooth excpet for a finite set of positive numbers
and it turnd out that
they become Federer domains.
However, this cannot be deduced by pure measure theoretic arguments.
One must
use a more refined analysis to
profit upon the hypothesis that $f$ is a polynomial. It would lead us too 
far to discuss this in more detail.
Let us only mention that
the assertion that
we get Federer domains rely upon
the study of semi-algebraic sets, i.e. here one empoys
the Seidenberg-Tarski theorem and also certain straitfications and
a geometric integration theory developed in Whitney's very interesting text-book
\emph{Geometric integration theory}.
So even if Theorem 5.1 is quite general the reader should be aware of the fact
that
extra work often is needed to verify that a 
specific domain is of the Federer type.
More generally domains arising as real-analytic polyhedra
satisfy the Federer condition and
this leads to general Stokes formulas while
currents are integrated on so called semi-analytic sets in arbitrary
real-analytic manifolds.
Here inequalities due to Lojasievicz are used. But it would bring us to
far to
discuss this in more detail.


























\newpage



\centerline {\bf {6. The Hardy-Littlewood maximal function}}.
\bigskip

\noindent
Let $f$ be a non-negative  $L^1$-function on the real line
supported by $x\geq 0$.
Its forward maximal function is defined by


\noindent
\[
f^*(x)=\max_{h>0}\,\frac{1}{h}\int_x^{x+h}\, f(t) dt
\]


\noindent 
Above it  suffices to
seek the maximum when $h$ runs over  positive rational numbers
which we enumerate as $\{q_\nu\}$. For a fixed $\nu$ we have a continuous
function
\[
g_\nu(x)=\frac{1}{q_\nu}\int_x^{x+q_\nu}\, f(t) dt
\] 
Hence  $f^*(x)$ is the maximum of 
$\{g_\nu\}$ which implies that
$f^*$
is 
\emph{lower semi-continuous}.
So if  $\lambda>0$ we get the open set:
\medskip

\noindent
\[
E(\lambda)=\{x\,\colon\, f^*(x)>\lambda\}
\]


\noindent
{\bf 6.1. Theorem}
\emph{One has the inequality}
\[ 
\text{meas}\,E(\lambda)\leq
\frac{1}{\lambda}\cdot\int_{E(\lambda)}\, f(x)dx
\]

\medskip

\noindent \emph{Proof.}
With  $g(x)=\frac{f(x)}{\lambda}$ we put
$E_g=\{ g^*>1\}$ and there remains to 
show that :
\[
\text{meas}(E_g)\leq\int_{E_g}\, g(x)dx
\tag{1}
\]
To get (1)  we consider the primitive function
\[ 
G(x)=\int_0^x\,g(t)dt\tag {2}
\]
It is non-decreasing and continuous. Let $\{(a_\nu,b_\nu)\}$
be the disjoint intervals of $E_g$. 
\medskip

\noindent
\emph{Sublemma.} For each $\nu$ one has
\[
 G(b_\nu)-G(a_\nu)\geq b_\nu-a_\nu
\]
\medskip

\noindent \emph{Proof.}
Suppose strict inequality holds. By continuity we find
$x>a_\nu$ with $x-a_\nu$ small so that
\[ 
G(b_\nu)-G(x)<b_\nu-x
\]
Next, since $x\in E_g$ there exists $\xi>x$ such that
\[ 
G(\xi)-G(x)>\xi-x
\]
Then $b_\nu<\xi$ must hold. On the other hand, since
$b_\nu$ is a boundary point of $E_g$ we also have
\[ 
G(\xi)-G(b_\nu)\leq\xi-b_\nu
\]
Now we get a contradiction since it would follows that
\[ 
G(\xi)-G(x)= G(\xi)-G(b_\nu)+G(b_\nu)-G(x)
<\xi-b_\nu+b_\nu-x=\xi-x
\]
\medskip

\noindent
\emph{Proof continued.}
Since the intervals $(a_\nu,b_\nu)$ are disjoint we get

\[ 
\int _{E_g}\,g dt=
\sum\int_{a_\nu}^{b_\nu}\,g dt=
\sum\, G(b_\nu)-G(a_\nu)
\]
By the Sublemma the last sum is $\geq\sum(b_\nu-a_\nu)$
which is equal to $\text{meas}(E_g)$ and Theorem 6.1 is proved.




\medskip


\noindent
{\bf  {An $L^2$-inequality}}. 
If $x$ is a Lebesgue point of $f$ we have
the inequality
$f^*(x)\geq f(x)$. Using Theorem 1 we shall now
establish an inequality for $L^2$-norms. 
\medskip

\noindent 
{\bf 6.2. Theorem} \emph{For every square integrable function
$f(x)$ supported  $x\geq 0$
one has the inequality}
\[ 
||f^*||_2\leq 2\cdot ||f||_2
\]
\emph{where $||\cdot||_2$ denotes the $L^2$-norm.}
\bigskip

\noindent
\emph{Proof.}
Since we claim an a priori inequality it suffices to prove the
result when $f(x)$ is bounded and has compact support.
Let $N$ be a positive integer which  eventually will be very large.
Keeping $N$ fixed we set
\[ 
E(\nu)=\{\frac{\nu}{N}\leq f^*<\frac{\nu+1}{N}\}
\quad\colon\quad\nu=0,1,\ldots
\]
Set
\[ 
f_-^*=\sum\,\frac{\nu}{N}\cdot\chi_{E(\nu)}\quad\colon\quad
f_+^*=\sum\,\frac{\nu+1}{N}\cdot\chi_{E(\nu)}
\]

\noindent 
\emph{Sublemma} For each $N\geq 1$ we have
\[
\int_0^\infty\, (f_-^*(x))^2dx\leq 2\cdot \int_0^\infty\, f_+^*(x)\cdot f(x)\,dx
\]
\medskip

\noindent
\emph{Proof of Sublemma}
Set $E_\nu=\{ f^*>\frac{\nu}{N}\}$.
The left hand side is equal to
\[ 
\sum_{\nu\geq 0}\,
\frac{\nu^2}{N^2}\cdot(\,|E_\nu|-|E_{\nu+1}|\,)=
\sum_{\nu\geq 0}\,
\frac{1}{N^2}\cdot\,|E_\nu|\cdot(\nu^2-(\nu-1)^2)\leq
\sum_{\nu\geq 0}\frac{2\nu}{N^2}\cdot |E_\nu|
\]
\medskip

\noindent
By Theorem 6.1 the last sum is majorized by
\[
\sum_{\nu\geq 0}\,\frac{2}{N}\int_{E_\nu} f dt=
\sum_{\nu\geq 0}\,\frac{2(\nu+1)}{N}\int_{E(\nu)}\, fdt
\]
By the construction of $f^*_+$ the last sum is
$2\int\, f^*_+(x)\cdot f dx$ and the Sublemma is proved.
\bigskip

\noindent
\emph{Proof continued.}
The Cauchy-Schwarz inequality for $L^2$-norms gives:
\[
\int\, f^*_+(x)\cdot f \,dx\leq ||f^*_+||_2\cdot ||f||_2
\]
Hence the Sublemma gives
\[
||f^*_-||^2_2\leq 2\cdot
 ||f^*_+||_2\cdot ||f||_2
\]
Theorem 1 follows since the $L^2$-norms of $f^*_+$ and $f^*_-$
both tend to $||f^*||_2$ when $N$ increases.
\bigskip

\noindent {\bf Remark.}
The proof of the $L^2$-inequality  extends easily  to 
$L^p$ inequalities when $1<p<1$ where
the constant $2$ is replaced by
$\frac{p}{p-1}$.
In Theorem 1 we regarded the
forward maximum function. One can  also 
define the backward maximum function and 
without any restriction on the support of an $L^2$-function 
$f$ on the $x$-line its full maximal function 
\[
f^{**}(x)=\text{max}_{\xi,\eta}\,\,
\frac{1}{\xi+\eta}\int_{x-\eta}^{x+\xi}\, f(t) dt
\]
with the maximum taken over all pairs of positive numbers
$\xi,\eta$. Then
Theorem 6.2 gives:
\[
||f^{**}||_2\leq 4\cdot ||f||_2
\]



\newpage














\centerline
{\bf {7. Rademacher functions} }

\bigskip

\noindent
For each positive integer $N$
the  interval $[0,1)$ can be 
decomposed into half-open intervals
\[
\Delta_N(\nu)=[\nu2^{-N},\nu+1)2^{-N})\quad\colon\quad 0\leq
\nu\leq 2^N-1
\]
Define the function $R_N(x)$ by
\[ 
R_N(x)=(-1)^\nu\quad\colon\quad x\in \Delta_N(\nu)
\]
This construction applies to each $N\geq 1$ and it is clear that
\[
\int_{\Delta_N(\nu)}\, R_M(x)dx=0\quad\colon\quad
0\leq\nu<2^N-1\quad\colon\, M>N
\]
It follows easily that
\[ 
\int_0^1\, R_N(x)\cdot R_M(x)dx=0\quad\colon\quad M\neq N
\]
Next, let $\{\alpha_\nu\}$ be a sequence in $\ell^2$, i.e.
$\sum\,|\alpha_\nu|^2<\infty$ and
consider the partial sum functions
\[ 
S_N(x)=\sum_{\nu=1}^{\nu=N}\, \alpha_\nu\cdot R_\nu(x)
\]
We shall analyze the limit  as $N\to\infty$ using
expansions of real numbers in the binary system.
That is, when $0<x<1$ we have a series expansion
\[
x=\epsilon_1(x) 2^{-1}+\epsilon_2(x) 2^{-2}+\ldots\quad\colon\quad
\epsilon_\nu(x)=1\,\,\text{or}\,\, 0.
\]
The expansion is unique unless $2^Nx$ is an integer for some $N$.
Ignoring this denumerable set we define for each $x$ the partial sum
of its binary expansion
\[ 
\xi_N(x)=\sum_{\nu=1}^{\nu=N}\,\epsilon_\nu(x)\cdot 2^{-\nu}
\]
So here $\xi_N(x)\leq x<\xi_N(x)+2^{-N}$ and
with these notations  the following hold for the given
$\ell^2$-sequence above:
'\medskip

\noindent {\bf 7.1. Proposition} \emph{For each $x$ and $N\geq 1$ one has
the equality}
\[ 
S_N(x)=\sum_{\nu=1}^{\nu=N}\, (2\epsilon_\nu(x)-1)\cdot\alpha_\nu
\]


\noindent
The straightforward proof is left to the reader. Now we announce
\medskip

\noindent {\bf 7.2. Theorem}
\emph{For each sequence $\{\alpha_\nu\}$ in $\ell^2$ there exists a null set $E$
in $\{0\leq x\leq 1\}$
such that one has a
pointwise limit when $x$ is outside $E$:}
\[
\lim_{N\to\infty}\,
S_N(x)=\sum_{\nu=1}^\infty\,(2\cdot \epsilon_\nu(x)-1)\cdot\alpha_\nu
\]
\medskip

\noindent 
\emph{Proof.}
Since the $R$-functions is an orthonormal family in
$L^2[0,1]$ we have
\[
\int_0^1 S_N(x)^2dx=\sum_{\nu=1}^{\nu=N}
\,\alpha_\nu^2\quad\colon\quad N\geq 1
\]
It follows that $\{S_N\}$ is a Cauchy sequence in $L^2[0,1]$ and 
hence it converges in the $L^2$-norm to a limit function $S(x)$.
Let $\mathcal L(S)$ be the set of Lebesgue points for $S$.
If $x\in\mathcal L(x)$ we have
\[
S(x)=\lim_{N\to\infty}\,
\int_{\xi_N(x)}^{\xi_N(x)+2^{-N}}\, S(t) dt
\]
From the construction of the partial sum functions it follows that
$\lim\, S_N(x)=S(x)$, i.e.  the  pointwise limit exists
at each Lebesgue point of $S$. Theorem 7.2. follows
since $(0,1)\setminus\mathcal L(S)$ is a null set.



\newpage

\centerline{\bf\large 8. The Brownian motion}

\bigskip


\noindent
{\bf Introduction.} Inspired by P.J. Daniell's work  [DA] which fascilated previous
results about  infinite products of measure spaces, 
Norbert Wiener gave a rigorous  construction of
a stochastic process $\mathcal W$ in continuous time 
with the following properties: For each
$t>0$ one has a normally distributed random variable $W_t$ whose variance is $t$
and  $\{W_t\}$
have independent increments, i.e if $s<t$ then the random variables
$W_t-W_s$ and $W_s$ are independent. Wiener employed 
Fourier series to construct such a process
whose
sample space is a
\emph{denumerable} product of ${\bf{R}}$. 
The book
[Pa-Wi]   contains
an interesting historic account about the role of the
Browninan motion outside the
"abstract world of mathematics".
Here we add some further comments.
\medskip

\noindent
Albert Einstein's  first scientific paper was published in december 1900.
It
contains a statistical analysis of experimental data from phenomenon
of capillarity and shows that Einstein    already during the early
period
of his career  was familiar 
with  statistical arguments.
Five years later it led to his articles [XX] which
justified the existence of atoms
and made it possible to determine the Avogrado number.
After Einstein's work  many skilled experimental
scientists used stochastic analysis
based upon the Brownian motion. Among those who 
applied Einstein's recipe was
Theo Svedberg  who received
the Nobel Prize in chemistry in 1925 (6 ?).
The interested reader may  consult the 
book \emph{Atoms} by Jean Perrin [Per]
which gives a fascinating description of the nature of
Brownian motion and describes   experiments
which predict that individual paths
are non-differentiable.


\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{8.0 Wiener's construction.}}
The first rigouros proof that
almost every Brownian path is continuous and even
has a certain Hšlder continuity is due to Wiener who
used random Fourier series expansions of sine-functions.
In general, let
$\{a_k\}$ be sequence of real numbers
such that $\sum\, a_k^2<\infty$.
Next, $\{\rho_1,\rho_2,\ldots\}$
is a sequence of independent normally distributed variables, i.e. each
$\rho_k$ has mean-value zero and variance one.
With $t$   regarded as a time parameter
we get a stochastic process:
\[
t\mapsto W(t)=
\sum_{k=1}^\infty\, 
a_k\cdot \sin kt\cdot \rho_k\quad\colon 0\leq t\leq \pi/2\tag{i}
\]
It is obvious that the mean value of 
each $W(t)$
is zero and the variance becomes
\[
\sum_{k=1}^\infty\, 
a_k^2\cdot \sin^2 kt\tag{ii}
\]
Next, for a pair  $0<s,t\leq \pi/2$ 
the mean value
\[
 E(W(t)\cdot W(s))=
 \sum_{k=1}^\infty\, 
a_k^2\cdot \sin kt\cdot \sin ks=
\frac{1}{2} \sum_{k=1}^\infty\, 
a_k^2\cdot (\cos k(t+s)-
\cos k(t-s))\tag{iii}
\]
At this stage Winer employed a special $a$-sequence.
Expand the continuous function
$t$ on $[0,\pi]$  in a cosine series
\[
t=
\sum\, b_k\cdot \cos (kt)\tag{iv}
\]
Here
\[
\frac{\pi}{2}\cdot b_k=\int_0^\pi\, t\cdot
\cos kt \, dt=\frac{1}{k}\cdot \int_0^\pi\, \sin kt\, dt
\]
The right hand side
is $\frac{2}{k^2}$ if $k$ is odd and otherwise zero. So if $k$ is odd one has
\[
b_k=\frac{4}{\pi\cdot k^2}
\]
Set
\[
a_{2k+1}=\sqrt{b_{2k+1}}\quad\colon\, k=0,2,\ldots\tag{v}
\]
Then (iii-iv) entail that

\[
E(W_\epsilon(t)\cdot W_\epsilon(s))=\frac{1}{2}(t+s-|t-s|)=
\min\, (t,s)\tag{*}
\]
Recall from basic probability theory that
a pair of non-correlated normal distributions are independent.
From (*) it follows that
the stochastic process $\{W(t)\}$ has independent increment, i.e.
if $0<s<t\leq \pi$ then
$W(s)$ and
$W(t)-
W(s)$ are independent normally distributed variables with variance $s$ and $t-s$.
This means that
$\{W(t)\}$ yields a Brownian motion.
\medskip


\noindent
By  Wiener's construction the sample space for
the whole stochastic process in the continuous time parameter is
given   by a denumerable  product of independent 
normal distributions on the real line.
Starting from the construction above, Wiener also analyzed
the continuity of individual paths.
For a given sequence of values of the indepedent
$\rho$-variables
we have a function of $t$ with the sine-series expansion
(i).
From the above
\[
a_k=\frac{2}{\sqrt{\pi}\cdot k}
\]
when $k$ is odd while $a_k=0$ when $k$ is even.
Since the series
$\sum\, k^{-1}$ diverges we do not get an absolutely convergent
series even if
the set of $\rho$-values is bounded. So the
continuity of
$W(t)$ is not
automatic.  The reader can consult
[Paley-Wiener page xx-xx]
for a  proof that if $\delta>0$ then 
$W_\epsilon(t)$ is Hšlder continuous of order
$1/2-\delta$ for almost every outcome of the
$\rho$-sequence.
In ¤ 8.1 we give another construction of
the Brownian motion and establish a precise result about
the continuity for individual
Browninan paths.
\medskip


\noindent
{\bf{Parabolic PDE-equations.}}
Einstein's work  has shown that
a {probabilistic interpretation} to solutions of partial differential equations
which appear in diffusion processes of colloidal material
is very useful.
This inspired  later work dealing with the
Brownian motion and the heat-equation such as in the joint article
[KKP] by Khintchine, Kolmogorv and Petrowsky  from 1930.
Here one finds 
precise results about 
continuity properties of individual Brownian paths based upon
studies 
of boundary value problems
for the heat equation.
Later Paul Levy found   a more direct proof
of the continuity properties of
individual Brownian paths which we give in ¤ 8.2.
From a historic point of view considerable
credit must be given to Fourier and Laplace.
Even though  the  interplay between 
PDE-equations of parabolic type for heat conduction 
and probability theory was not fully explicit in
Laplace's pioneeringt work in probability theory from 1820,
the
mathematical frame was  paved via Fourier-Laplace transforms.
In ¤ 8.2 we follow Fourier and Laplace to 
construct parabolic PDE-equations associated to
stochastic processes in continuous time
with independent increments.

 
 
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf {Bachelier's work.}}
 To the  historic account one must add the pioneering
work by X.X Bachelier whose  article \emph{La Bourse}
from 1900 calculates
probability densities attached to the 
Brownian motion
with barriers.
For example, given some $a>0$
we can ask for  the  time when a Brownian
first hits  the barrier
$x=a$. This yields a probability distribution
which depends on $a$ and was determined 
explicitly by Bachelier.
His  
text-book on probability theory 
from  1910 is the first modern account dealing with stochastic processes
on an advanced level.
The Brownian motion can be
used to investigate
various   probability distributions, apart from the
"hitting probability" on a given arc on the boundary of a domain
in ${\bf{C}}$ which correspond to a
harmonic measure. At several occasions in these notes
we expose this "tautology" 
between  probabilistic interpretations 
via the Brownian motion and
solutions to the heat equation and other boundary
value problems which
are obtained via solutions to the Dirichlet problem.







\bigskip














\medskip

\noindent
\centerline {\bf 8.1 The Levy-Lšf  construction.} 
\medskip

\noindent

The subsequent material  is inspired from 
notes by Anders Martin-Lšf who attributes the main
constructions  to P. Levy.
We seek a stochastic process $\{X(t)\colon\, t\geq 0\}$
such that for every pair $0\leq s<t$, 
$X(t)-X(s)$
is a normally distributed 
random variable with variance $t-s$ and  independent of
$X(s)$. In particular each  $X(t)$ is normally distributed
with variance $t$.
Recall that two normally distributed variables are independent if and only if
they are uncorrelated. Hence, if $E$ denotes expected value, 
the condition that the process has
independent increments is 
equivalent  to the condition that
\[
E(X(t)\cdot X(s))=\text{min}(s,t)\quad\colon\text{for every pair}\,\ s,t
\]
Before we give the construction of a stochastic process satisfying the conditions above
we  make some observations  if such a process is given.
\medskip

\noindent {\bf 1. Conditioned mean-values.} Let $s<t<u$ and
consider the  mean value of $X(t)$ when $X(u)$ and $X(s)$ 
are given, i.e. we seek
\[ 
E((X(t)\,|\, X(s),X(u)
\]
Since the  stochastic variables $X(s),X(t),X(u)$ are normally distributed
it is wellknown  that this conditioned mean value is a linear function
of $X(s)$ and $X(u)$, i.e. there exists a pair of real numbers $(a,b)$
such that:

\[
\bar X(t)=E((X(t)\,| X(s),X(u))=a\cdot X(s)+b\cdot X(u))
\] 
Since we  are assuming independent increments, 
$X(t)-\bar X(t)$ is independent of both $X(s)$ and $X(u)$. A calculation
which is left to the reader gives
\[
a=\frac{u-t}{u-s}\quad\colon\quad b=\frac{t-s}{u-s}\tag{*}
\]
If $t-s\to 0$ while $u$ stays fixed, then
$a\to 1$ which reflects that the conditioned mean value
is close to $X(s)$.
Inserting the values of $a,b$ we now have a linear function

\[
\bar X(t)=\frac{(u-s)X(s)+(t-s)X(u)}{u-s}\quad\colon\quad s<t<u
\]
which coincides with $X(s)$, respectively $X(u)$ at the end points of
the interval $[s,u]$.
\medskip

\noindent {\bf 2. The conditioned variance.}
With $a$ and $b$ chosen as in (*) we get:
\[ 
V(t) =E((X(t)-\bar X(t))^2\,|\, X(s),X(u))=
\] 
\[
E[(a(X(t)-X(s))+b(X(t)-X(u))^2]=a^2(t-s)+b^2(u-t)=
\frac{(u-s)(t-s)}{u-s}
\]


\noindent
Hence the graph of $V(t)$ is a parabola whose values at
the end points $s$ and $u$ are zero. The maximum is attained at
the mid-point which reflects that we obtain a maximal variance
when random values taken by $X(s)$ and $X(u)$ affect $X(t)$ in equal parts.
\bigskip

\noindent
{\bf 3. An  inductive construction.}
We shall construct the Brownian motion over the unit interval $0\leq t\leq 1$.
After one can of course continue to any time interval.
Let $N\geq 1$ and put 
\[
t_\nu(N)=\nu\cdot 2^{-N}\quad\colon\quad 0\leq\nu\leq 2^N
\]


\noindent 
From  previous remarks we obtain a stochastic process
as follows:
Let $\chi_1,\ldots,\chi_{2^N}$
be independent and normally distributed variables, each
with mean value zero and variance
$2^{-N}$.
Consider a time value
\[ t=k2^{-N}+\delta\quad\colon\quad 
0\leq k\leq 2^N-1\quad\colon\,0\leq\delta\leq 2^{-N}
\]


\noindent At this moment of time
we define the random variable
\[ 
X_N(t)=\sum_{\nu=0}^{\nu=k-1}\,\chi_\nu+(1-\delta)\chi_k+
\delta\chi_{k+1}\tag{i}
\]
\medskip

\noindent
With a sample space of dimension  $2^N$ over the real line,
the outcome consists of   values  taken by the independent 
and normally distributed
$\chi$-variables. This
sample  
determines $X_t$ for every time value $0\leq t\leq 1$.
Hence the  outcome of each sample point consists of 
a piecewise linear curve
$t\mapsto \gamma(t)$
defined on $[0,1]$ with eventual corner points at
when $t=k2^{-N}$, where $\gamma(k2^{-N})$ is the sum of the
first $k$ many $\chi$-variables.
\bigskip

\noindent
{\bf 4. Passage to the limit.}
We have constructed a  process
$\{X_N(t)\}$
whose sample space is of dimension $2^N$.
For the inductive construction we pass from stage $N$ to 
$N+1$  allowing "white noise" during   time intervals 
$[k2^{-N},(k+1)2^{-N}]$. This means that we introduce $2^N$ many
new random variables $g_1,\dots,g_{2^N}$ which
are independent of those which were used to get
$\{X_N(t)\}$.
 Moreover, every $g_k$ is normally distributed 
with variance $2^{-N-1}$.
Thus, at stage $N+1$ we have a process $t\mapsto X_{N+1}(t)$
where

\[ 
X_{N+1}(k2^{-N}+\delta 2^{-N-1})=X_N(k2^{-N})+ \delta g_k
\colon\, 0\leq\delta\leq 1
\] 
\[
X_{N+1}((k+1/2)2^{-N}+\delta 2^{-N-1})=
X_N(k2^{-N})+  g_k+
\delta g_{k+1}
\colon\, 0\leq\delta\leq 1
\]
\bigskip

\noindent
Inductively we get a sequence of processes
$\{X_N(t)\}_{N\geq 1}$ whose  sample spaces increase.
Thus, at each stage "white noise" appears, expressed  by a  block of $2^N$
many $g$-variables.
The whole  sample space is  a
denumerable product of the real line on which we define the
usual product of the Lebesgue measure.
The outcome from a sample point is  a sequence of polygons
$\gamma_1,\gamma_2,\ldots$. At a dyadic time value 
$t=k2^{-N}$ the polygons
$\{\gamma_\nu\}$ pass the same point, 
There remains to investigate   when the  sequence of these polygons
converge to a continuous limit curve. It turns out that the convergence
holds for
almost every sample point which by the above
is taken from the denumerable product of copies of the real line
${\bf{R}}$.
\bigskip


\noindent
{\bf 5. Continuity of Brownian paths.}
We shall study the effect of white noise 
during the passage from  stage $N-1$ to the stage $N$.
For this purpose we consider a family
$\chi_1,\ldots,\chi_{2^N}$
of independent normally distributed with mean value zero and variance one.
Define for each $\alpha>0$:
\medskip
\noindent
\[
\Pi_N(\alpha)=
\text{Prob}\{ \text{max}\,\colon\,  2^{-N/2}|\chi_k|\geq\alpha\,\colon
1\leq k\leq 2^N\}
\]

\noindent
Thus, if $g_k=2^{-N/2}\chi_k$ are the random 
variables which cause white noise,
then
$\Pi_N(\alpha)$ is the probability that at least some $g_k$
has absolute value $\geq\alpha$.
By the previous discussion this means that we measure 
the probability for the \emph{maximal distance} between the curves 
$\gamma_{N-1}$ and $\gamma_N$
over the whole time interval.
So  we want are upper bounds for
the $\Pi$-numbers with $\alpha$ small as $N$ increases.
Here is the crucial inequality:





\medskip

\noindent
{\bf 6. Proposition.}
\emph{For all pairs $N,\alpha$ one  has the inequality}

\[ 
\Pi_N(\alpha)\leq \sqrt{\frac{2}{\pi}}\cdot 
\frac{2^{N/2}}{\alpha}\cdot
e^{-2^{_N}\cdot\alpha^2/2}
\]
\bigskip

\noindent
\emph{Proof.}
Consider first a single normal distribution
with variance one.
If $A>0$ we have the tail
probability
\[ 
\frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi}}
\int_A^\infty\,e^{-x^2/2}dx\leq
\frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi}}\int_A^\infty\,\frac{x}{A}\cdot e^{-x^2/2}dx=
\frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi}}\cdot \frac{e^{-A^2/2}}{A}
\]


\noindent
Put
\[
 A=2^{N/2}\cdot \alpha\quad\colon\quad \xi=
 \sqrt{\frac {2}{\pi}}\cdot
 \frac{1}{A} \cdot e^{-A^2/2}
 \]
 
 
 \noindent
Notice that $1-\Pi_N(\alpha)$
is the probability that $|\chi_k|\leq A$ for every $k$.
The tail inequality applied to every $\chi_k$ gives
\[ 
\text{Prob}\{|\chi_k|\geq A\}\geq
 \sqrt{\frac {2}{\pi}}\cdot
 \frac{1}{A} \cdot e^{-A^2/2}=\xi
 \]
The elementary
inequality $(1-t)^M\leq Mt$ for every $0<t<1$
therefore gives
\[ 
\Pi_N(\alpha)\leq
(1-\xi)^{2^N}\leq 2^N\cdot\xi=2^N\cdot \sqrt{\frac {2}{\pi}}\cdot
 \frac{1}{A} \cdot e^{-A^2/2}
\]
Since $A=2^{N/2}\alpha$ the last inequality proves Proposition 6.
 \medskip
 
 \noindent {\bf 7. Specific choice of
 $\alpha$}.
With $\alpha=2^{-N/2}\beta$
we have
\[ 
\Pi_N(\alpha)\leq\sqrt{\frac {2}{\pi}}\cdot
2^N\cdot\frac{1}{\beta}
\cdot e^{-\beta^2/2}=\sqrt{\frac {2}{\pi}}\cdot
\frac{1}{\beta}\cdot e^{\,\text{Log}(2)\cdot N-\beta^2/2}\tag{7}
\]
\medskip

\noindent
Let us choose
\[
\beta=[2 N\cdot \log 2\,]^{\frac{1}{2}}\quad\colon\quad\theta>1
\]
\medskip

\noindent
Then the last term in (7) becomes
\[
\frac{1}{\sqrt{\pi\cdot\log 2}}\cdot
2^{N(1-\theta^2)}
\]
\medskip

\noindent
So with this specific choice of $\alpha$ we have established
the inequality
\[
\Pi_N(\alpha)\leq
\frac{1}{\sqrt{\pi\cdot\log 2}}\cdot
2^{N(1-\theta^2)}\quad\colon\quad
\alpha=
[2 N\text{Log}(2)\,]^{\frac{1}{2}}\cdot 2^{-N/2}\tag{8}
\]
\bigskip

\noindent
{\bf 8. Remark.}
Keeping $\theta>1$ fixed
and choosing $\alpha(N)=
[2 N\text{Log}(2)\,]^{\frac{1}{2}}\cdot 2^{-N/2}$
for each $N$, we get the convergence of the series
\[
\sum_{N=1}^\infty
\Pi_N(\alpha(N))\tag{9}
\]
\medskip

\noindent
In the construction of the Brownian motion the
"white noise" as we pass from period $N$ to $N+1$
arises from  $2^N$ many independent normally
distributed variables, each with variance $2^{-N}$.
So $\Pi_N(\alpha(N))$ measures the maximal deviation 
over the whole time interval $[0,1]$
from
a polygon at period $N$ to the new polygon at period $N+1$.
\medskip


\noindent
The convergent series (9)
implies that a single Brownian path is almost surely continuous and the choice of
$\{\alpha(N)\}$
entails that 
the resulting limit curves are almost surely 
Hšlder continuous of order $\sqrt{t}|\cdot\text{Log}(\frac{1}{t})$.
Summing up
we have established the following:
\bigskip

\noindent {\bf 9. Theorem.}
\emph{A  Brownian path is almost surely Hšlder continuous of order
$\sqrt{t}\cdot\log (\frac{1}{t})$.}
\bigskip

\noindent
{\bf{10. A result by Khintchine.}}
Theorem  9
asserts
that
almost every Brownian path is Hšlder continuous of
order
$\sqrt{t}\cdot\text{Log}(\frac{1}{t})$ for \emph{every} 
value of $t$.
A relaxed condition would be request
that  Hšlder continuity which
holds for almost all time values.
This leads to a result due to Khintchine
which asserts that almost every Brownian path
is Hšlder continuous of order
\[
\sqrt{t}\cdot\text{Log}(\text{Log}(\frac{1}{t}))\tag{*}
\]
for almost every time value. Notice that since we have taken a double Log-function 
we come closer to Hšlder continuity of order $\frac{1}{2}$
in (*). The proof of (*) uses Khintchine's \emph{Law of the iterated logarithm}.
For the proof of (*) we refer to [Khi] and the interested reader may also
consult his excellent text-book on statistical mechanics in
[Khi] which offers a good introduction for students of mathematics
without
prerequisite knowledge about physical laws.



\bigskip


\centerline{\bf{8.2 Parabolic PDE-equations and
stochastic  processes.}}
\medskip


\noindent
Following original work by Laplace and Fourier we
derive the parabolic PDE-equation
which is used to express   the joint probability distribution of
a vector-valued stochastic process in continuous time.
Let $n\geq 1$ and consider
a process 
$X(t)=(X_1(t),\ldots X_n(t)$ 
which is governed by infinitesmal  deterministic  drift
together with  a Brownian motion:
\[ 
dX_k(t)= b_k(X(t),t)\cdot dt+ \sigma_k(X(t),t)\cdot dW_k(t)
\quad\colon 1\leq k\leq n\tag{*}
\]
Above $\{b_k(x_1,\ldots,x_n,t)\}$ and
$\{\sigma _k(x_1,\ldots,x_n,t)\}$ are
real-valued functions defined for $x\in{\bf{R}}^n$ and  $t\geq 0$
and
$W(t)=(W_1(t),\ldots,W_n(t)$
is  a vector-valued Gaussian distribution
expressed by a density function $g(\omega_1,\ldots,\omega_n;t)$
where   no drift  occurs, i.e.
\[
\int_{{\bf{R}}^n}\, \omega_k\cdot g(\omega;t)\, d\omega=0
\quad\colon 1\leq k\leq n\tag{i}
\]
We have also  covariance functions
\[
B_{jk}(t)=
\int_{{\bf{R}}^n}\, \omega_j \omega_k\cdot g(\omega;t)\, d\omega
\quad\colon 1\leq j,k\leq n\tag{ii}
\]
At time $t=0$ the random variable
$X(0)$ has a joint density distribution
$f_0(x)= f_0(x_1,\ldots,x_n)$. If $t>0$ we denote by
$f(x;t)$  the density of $X(t)$ and
$\{\partial_j=\frac{\partial}{\partial x_j}\}$
are   derivation operators with respect to the $x$-variables.

\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{8.2.1. Theorem.}}
\emph{The function $f(x,t)$ satisfies the
PDE-equation:}
\[
\frac{\partial f}{\partial t}=
\sum_{k=1}^{k=n}\, \partial_k(b_k\cdot f)+
\frac{1}{2}\sum\sum B_{j,k}(t)\cdot
\partial_j\partial_k(\sigma_j\sigma_k\cdot f)\tag{8.2.1}
\]
\medskip

\noindent
\emph{Proof.}
Introduce the characteristic function
\[
\phi(\xi;t)=  \int_{{\bf{R}}^n}\,
e^{i\langle \xi,x\rangle}\, f(x;t)\, dx
\]
By definition (*) means that
if $\Delta$ is a small positive number then
\[
\phi(\xi;t+\Delta)=
\iint_{{\bf{R}}^n\times
{\bf{R}}^n}\,
\exp\bigl[ \sum_{k=1}^{k=n}\,
i\xi_k(x_k+b_k(x;t)\cdot \Delta+\sigma_k(x;t)\omega_k\cdot \sqrt{\Delta}\bigr]
\cdot
f(x;t)g(\omega;t) \, dxd\omega
\]
Then (i) and (ii) together with  a Taylor expansion up to order two gives:
\[
\lim_{\Delta\to 0}\,
\frac{\phi(\xi;t+\Delta)-\phi(x;t)}{\Delta}=
\]
\[
\sum_{k=1}^{k=n}\, i\xi_k\cdot 
\int_{{\bf{R}}^n}\, e^{i\langle\xi,x\rangle}
b_k(x,t)\cdot f(x;t)\,dx-
\frac{1}{2}\sum\sum B_{j,k}(t)\cdot
\xi_j\xi_k\cdot 
\int_{{\bf{R}}^n} \, e^{i\langle\xi,x\rangle}
\sigma_j(x,t)\cdot\sigma_k(x,t) \cdot f(x;t)\,dx
\]
\medskip

\noindent
Now (8.2.1) follows from Fourier's inversion formula.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{8.2.2 Remark.}}
In order that Fourier's inversion formula applies
one should assume that the functions $\{b_k\}$ are
in $L^1$Êand  the $\sigma$-functions are in $L^2$.
In practice more regularity is imposed, i.e. the $\sigma$-functions are of class
$C^2$ and the $b$-functions of class $C^1$ in which case
the parabolic
PDE-equation (8.2.1) is expressed
without
distribution
derivatives.
One refers to (8.2.1) as a diffusion equation
and in the context of PDE-theory 
the equation in Theorem 8.2.1 is said to be
parabolic.
Some 
text-books
prove Theorem 8.2.1 via 
the "Ito Calculus". Personally 
I prefer the original 
proof by Laplace and Fourier above where
the  parabolic PDE-equation is established  under
relaxed conditions on
the $b$- and the $\sigma$-functions. Moreover, 
Parseval's equation gives
a precise
insight about $L^2$-properties.
\medskip


\noindent
{\bf{8.2.3 Uniqueness results.}}
Already the case $n=1$ leads to subtle problems. In fact, it was discovered by
Gevrey and Hadamard that solutions to the basic heat equation
\[
\frac{\partial f}{\partial t}(x,t)=f''_{xx}(x,t)
\]
in general are not unique, i.e. non-trvial solutions can exist even if
$x\mapsto f(x,t_0)$ vanishes identically for some time value $t_0$.
It is ony in the analytic case where  
solutions expressed are 
expressed by 
the integral formula
\[
f(x,t)=\frac{1}{\pi\cdot \sqrt{t}}\int_{-\infty}^\infty\, e^{(x-\xi)^2/4t}\,
f(\xi,0)\, d\xi
\]
However, this integral formula is not valid
if $x\mapsto f(x,0)$ increases too fast.
Here an  involved
analysis is needed 
which goes   beyond    elementary studies 
of stochastic PDE-equations.
For results concerned with the non-analytic case
we
refer to work by Beurling and  Holmgren,
which is exposed in ¤ xx.













\newpage



DELETE


\bigskip


\centerline{\bf{9. Riesz' representation theorem.}}
\medskip

\noindent
Consider  a Booelan algebra $\mathcal B$
of subsets from some sample space $\Omega$.
Denote by ${\bf{ba}}(\mathcal B)$ the set of  additive 
and bounded real-valued measures $\mu$, i.e. there
exists  a constant $C_\mu$
such that
\[ 
|\mu(A)|\leq C_\mu\quad\colon A\in\mathcal B
\]
The total variation of $\mu$ over a set $A$ is defined as
\[
|\mu|(A)= \max\, \sum\, |\mu(E_k)|
\]
where $\{E_k\}$ is an arbitrary familly of disjoint subsets of
$A$.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{Exercise.}}
Show that $A\mapsto |\mu|(A)$  is an additive bounded
measure where $C_{|\mu|}$ can be taken as $2\cdot C_\mu$.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{9.1 Jordan's decomposition.}}
Let $\mu$ be given in
${\bf{ba}}(\mathcal B)$. For each $A\in\mathcal B$ we set
\[
\mu^+(A)=\max_{F\subset A}\, \mu(F)\quad\colon\quad 
\mu^-(A)=\min_{F\subset A}\, \mu(F)
\]
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{Exercise.}}
Show the two equalities:
\[
 |\mu|= \mu^+ +\mu^-\quad\colon\quad \mu=
 \mu^+-\mu^-
 \]
\medskip


\noindent
Next, let $S$ be a compact metric space
and $\mathcal B_S$ the Boooelan algebra generated by open sets.
Let $\mu$ be a non-negative function in
${\bf{ba}}(\mathcal B_S)$, i.e $\mu(A)\geq 0$ hold for
$A\in\mathcal B$.
Define  $\mu^*$ 
on  $\mathcal B_S$ by
\[ 
\mu^*(A)=\min_{A\subset U}\, \mu(U)
\]
where the mimium is taken over open sets $U$ which contain $A$.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{Exercise.}}
Show that $\mu^*$ is additive. The hint is  that
$\mu^*(U)=\mu(U)$ for open sets and  in the next step one verifies that
$\mu(F_1\cup F_2)+\mu(F_1)+\mu(F_2)$
hold for each pair of disjoint closed sets.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{9.2. Borel integrals }}
As already explained in ¤ 0.1 from the introduction the two additive
measures $\mu$ and $\mu^*$
yield integrals with respect to continuos functions on $S$.
We show the equality below for every
$f\in C^0(S)$:
\[
\int\, f\cdot d\mu^*=
\int\, f\cdot d\mu\tag{*}
\]
Since every $f$ is the difference of two non-negative functions it suffices to prove (*) when 
$f\geq 0$ and after scaling that
$0\leq f\leq 1$. We may also assume that
$\mu(S)=1$.
Given a positive integer $N$
we set for each $0\leq k\leq N-1$:
\[
 a_k=\max_{k/N\leq x\leq (k+1)/N}\,f(x)
\,\colon\,  b_k=\min_{k/N\leq x\leq (k+2)/N}\,f(x)
\]
\[
A_k=\{k/N<f\leq (k+1)/N\}
\]
Since $\mu^*$ is non-negative one has
\[
\int\, f\cdot d\mu^*\leq \sum\, a_k\cdot \mu^*(A_k)\tag{i}
\]
Next, if $\epsilon>0$ the construction of
$\mu^*$ gives an $N$-tuple of
open sets $\{U_k\}$ where $U_k$  contains
$A_k$ and 
\[ 
\mu^*(A_k)>\mu(U_k)-\epsilon/N\tag{ii}
\]
Above we can  shrink $U_k$ and  assume that
\[
U_k\subset \{k/N<f(x)<(k+2)/N\}\tag{iii}
\]
From (ii) a summation gives
\[
\epsilon+\int\, f\cdot d\mu^*\leq \sum\, a_k\cdot \mu(U_k)\tag{iv}
\]
If $\omega_f(N)= \max\{ a_k-b_k\}$
the normalisation $\mu(S)=1$ gives
\[
\omega_f(N)+\epsilon+\int\, f\cdot d\mu^*\leq \sum\, b_k\cdot \mu(U_k)\tag{v}
\]
Let $\{\chi_k\}$ be a partition of the uniry with respect to
$\{U_k\}$.
The definition of the $b$-numbers entail that
$f\geq \sum b_k\cdot \chi_{U_k}$ and 
(v) gives
\[
\omega_f(N)+\epsilon+\int\, f\cdot d\mu^*\leq \int\, f\cdot d\mu\tag{vi}
\]
Above  $N$ is arbitrary large and
the continuity of $f$
entails that $\omega_f(N)\to 0$.
Since
$\epsilon$ can be arbitrary small
we conclude that
\[
\int\, f\cdot d\mu^*\leq \int\, f\cdot d\mu
\]
In the same way we get
\[
\int\, (1-f)\cdot d\mu^*\leq \int\,(1- f)\cdot d\mu
\]
Since  
$\mu^*(S)=\mu(S)$ 
we conclude that
(*) holds.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{9.3 Exercise.}}
Use the results above to prove Theorem 0.1.1 from the introduction.
A hint is that Jordan's decomposition entails that
if
$L$ is a continouos linear functional on $C^0(S)$
then
we pass to its extension $\widehat{L}$
which is represented by an additive measure $\mu$
on the Boolean algebra ${\bf{ba}}(\mathcal B_S)$.
and Jordan's decomposition gives $\mu=\mu^+-\mu^-$
which reduces 
the proof  to the case when
$L$ is non-negative.
\medskip
 
\noindent
 {\bf{9.4 The $\sigma$-additivity of regular measures.}}
Assume that $\mu=\mu^*$.
The reader may first verify   that $\mu^+$ and $\mu^-$
also are regular
in Jordan's decomposition.
Let us then consider the case when
$\mu$ is regular and non-negative
If $U$ is an open set in $X$ the regularity applied to its closed complement shows that
\[
\mu(U)=\max_{F\subset U}\, \mu(F)
\]
with the maximum taken over closed subsets.
Let us then take a denumerable sequence $\{A_n\}$ if pairwise disjoint sets
in $\mathcal B_S$
and define its outer measure by
\[
\widehat{\mu}(A^*)=\min_{A^*\subset U}\,\mu(U)
\]
Given $\epsilon>0$ we 
choose $U$ so that
\[
\mu(U)< \widehat{\mu}(A^*)+\epsilon
\]
Next, apply the regularity
to $A_n$ which gives  an closed set
$F_n\subset A_n$ such that
\[
\mu(A_n)\leq \mu(F_n)+\epsilon\cdot 2^{-n}
\]
Since $\mu$ is non-negative we have
\[
\sum_{n=1}^{n=N}\,\mu(F_n)\leq \mu(U)
\]
This hold for every $N$ and since $\epsilon>0$ was arbitrary we conclude that
\[
\sum\,\mu(A_n)\leq  \widehat{\mu}(A^*)\tag{*}
\]
To prove equality in (*)
we use again the regularity and 
find open sets $A_n\subset U_n$ such that
\[
\mu(U_n)\leq \mu(A_n)+\epsilon\cdot 2^{-n}\tag{i}
\]
Since  $\cup \,U_n$ is an open set which
contains $A^*$  we have
\[
\widehat{\mu}(A^*)\leq \,\mu(\cup U_n)\tag{ii}
\]
Next, the regularity applied to the open set
$\cup\,U_n$ gives a compact subset
$F$ such that
\[
\mu(\cup\,  U_n)\leq\epsilon+\mu(F)\tag{iii}
\]
Heine-Borel's Lemma gives an integer $N$ such that
$F$ is contained in the finite union $U_1\cup\ldots\cup\,U_N$.
Hence (i-iii) give
\[
\widehat{\mu}(A^*)\leq \epsilon+\sum_{n=1}^{n=N}\, \mu(U_n)\tag{iv}
\]
Finally, by (i) the last term is majorized by
$\epsilon+\mu(A_1)+\ldots+\mu(A_n)$ which entails that
\[
\widehat{\mu}(A^*)\leq 2\epsilon+ \sum\,\mu(A_n)
\]
Hence equality holds in (*).
One can continue by an induction in the
transfinite construction of Borel's $\sigma$-algebra and
conclude that $\mu$ has a unique extension to a $\sigma$-additive
measure to Borel's algebra.
\medskip

\noindent
{\bf{Remark.}}
The interested reader may consult
[Kolmogorov] for a further account
about
consistency during the construction of $\sigma$-additive measures.
